THE RED PAGE.
J. Brunton Stephens writes 1 —
In the notices called forth by the publication of
the “.Life of Tennyson by tiG Sou,” reviewers
evidently felt disposed to criticise the poet’s work
more freely than at. the time of his death, when
the sense of national bereavement would have
been less tolerant of dispraise. In some quarters,
indeed,, it seemed almost as if there were a re-
action against Tennyson or, rather, as: if the
publication of bis memoirs bad furnished occasion
for the manifestation of a long-sup)iressed reaction ;
but, now that one is neither influenced by the
pathos of a recent death-bed nor by the novelty of
biographical revelations, it may be a fitting time
to say a few words respecting one or two of the
adverse criticisms which touch most closely upon
Tennyson’s claim to supremacy among the poets
of his generation.
* * * *
The most formidable criticism brought to bear
upon Tennyson by those who are chiefly concerned
with pointing out his limitations, and even' by
some of those who are among his most ardent
admirers, is that he was not a great thinker. From
many quarters comes the insinuation, and even
the explicit statement, that he was merely a writer
of verses, that what power he had was in such
selection and collocation of words as exhibited a
mastery of rhymes and cadences and harmonies,
and in the use of a felicitous imagery, the chief
success of which lay at once in the glorification
and the concealment of the commonplace.
Now, this is a very serious form of disparage-
ment to be directed against the reputation of one
who made so great an impression upon his age,
and upon its highest and most representative
minds. But without admitting for one moment
that Tennyson’s poetry is lacking in what has
been called “fundamental brain work,” that he
was not a creator except in the sense of an ‘ ‘ in-
ventor, of harmonies,” I am inclined to think that
underlying this objection there is a fallacy which
consists in a misconception as to the true function
of the poet. The poet is not a Spencer or a
Darwin expressing himself in rhyme. It is not
the function of the poet to formulate new intellec-
tual conceptions. His proper work is to heighten
and intensify the conception, to quicken the con-
sciousness of that of which we are already pos-
sessed as a thought, to make us emotionally alive
to what we have hitherto only intellectually con-
ceived.
Nor is this to be regarded as an insignificant
work in comparison with that of the originator of
new conceptions. For, first of all, ideas are only
valuable to us in so far as they are potentialities of
emotion. The whole value of the intellectual
nature of man lies in its relation to his emotional
nature, or, in other words, in its relation to his
happiness.
In the second place, ideas which are not merely
ideas about abstract relations, are only half
possessed by us until they are possessed emotion-
ally as well as intellectually. Even when logically
formulated, a thought does not wholly belong to
us if it goes, no deeper than the intellect. The
consciousness must be emotionally quickened to its
reception before it is “brought home to us.”
When it is thus received, it lies no longer on the
convex or intellectual side of our nature. It is
received within, into the heart, is assimilated,
becomes part of ourselves. It is in this direction
that the work of the poet lies. He fuses with
emotion what has hitherto been only logically
accepted, and the truth thus dealt with is received
into the highest mood or mode of realisation. The.
question, then, with regard to the poet, should not
be, how many new truths has he uttered, how
many new ideas has he originated, but how far has
he quickened your consciousness, and thereby
enlarged your life? For this quickening of con-
sciousness is enlargement of life. “In each of
*s,” says Novalis, “ the larger half of our nature is
still asleep. ” To awaken this slumbering half, or -
even a portion of it, is to add to the life of the
soul, and this is precisely what the poet does,
whether the awakening is brought about by his
organ rpsh of mighty harmonies, or by the sweeter
enticements of his lute. Emotion upon an in-
tellectual basis is the highest mode of self-
realisation in relation to all that is not self, and
such realisation is life. This is what the poet gives
to us—something infinitely more valuable than is
bestowed on us by the musical composer, whose
work is most successful when it leads us, not to
realise ourselves and our relations to other things,
but to utterly forget ourselves and all else in a
trance of sensuous enjoyment. Therefore it is
that the great poets are loved above all other
writers, and that our love is so mingled with
gratitude. They have not discovered the circula-
tion of the blood, but, to use Tennyson’s own
words, their thoughts have enriched the blood of
the world. They have not formulated new laws
of nature, but they have done something greater.
They have revealed new relations between our-
selves and Nature, and have created, actually
created, new relations between the two great powers
of our own nature, the apprehending mind and
the aspiring soul. Tennyson (though not the
greatest, while high among the great, of the poets
the world has produced), is of all others the type
of poet who has given himself to fulfilling, and
has succeeded in fulfilling, this characteristic func-
tion. It was to this special work that he conse-
crated himself, as a priest devotes himself to the
service, of his God. It was in subservience to this
special aim that he laboriously cultivated and ac-
quired a technical excellence in expression so
amazing, so dazzling, that it blinds the . eyes of
even admirable critics to its subordination to the
higher purpose. Why was it that Huxley and
Tyndall and so many others of the foremost
scientific men of the day admired him, re-
vered him, loved him as they did ? Simply be-
cause he made them fed what they had hitherto
only known, because he gave worth and dignity to
the results of their researches by making things of
the intellect become tilings of the spirit. It was
because lie invested their knowledge with spiritual
attributes, and because under the exercise of his
magi an spell the material issues of their own
familiar and special work suffered a spiritual
change which transformed them into something
richer and stranger than they had appeared in the
dry. light of Science.
* * * *
As for the criticism which represents .the bulk
of Tennyson’s work as the glorification of the com-
monplace, I can only say that, to my mind, the
glorification of the commonplace is the most
notable achievement which a poet can . perform.
Almost all that can be thought and said about all
that most concerns the human mind to think, and
the human heart to feel, has been thought and
said long ago and ten thousand times over ; and
the most important truths upon all the most im-
portant thingsare commonplace. Take the whole
body of the stock quotations from the supreme,
poet of the world, which indicate his mode of re-
garding whatever there is of moment among the
subjects of human reflection—can you extract one
truth from them which was not familiar' to the
mind of the ancients, or which required any very
freat wisdom even for its earliest utterance ? Has
hakespeare revealed any profound mystery with
regard to life or death of which we can point to
him as the discoverer ? The whole thing lies in
the way of presenting it, in the art of putting it—
in throwing such illumination upon the common-
place that it stands out 'like a revelation. How
many . average persons have you not heard
express themselves, let us say, upon the
illusoriness of life ? How many average
persons are incapable of finding out for themselves
that they emerged originally from a state of non-
consciousness, and that the rest of the grave awaits
them at the end of life ? But when Shakespeare
says
We are such stuff
As dreams a/re made on, and our little life
Is rounded with a sleep ”,
what reader does not experience a new and solemn
realisation of the commonplace old truth of the
fleeting and unsubstantial nature of all that plea-
sures and frets us during our brief day on earth ?
... If I tell you that nature continues her pro-
cession and life maintains its ordinary course, but
the dead do not return, and there can be no re-
currence of any portion of past time, I am surely
not uttering anything very original or profound.
But —
Break, break, break
On thy cold, gray’ stones, 0 Sea,
And I would that my tongue could utter
The thoughts that arise in me.
0 well for the fisherman’s boy,
That he shouts with his sister at play ;
V O well for the sailor lad,
Thai he sings in his boat on the bay.
And the stately ships go on
To their haven under the hill ;
But 0 for the touch of a vanish’d hand,
And the sound of a voice that is still.
Break, break, break
At the foot of thy crags, 0 Sea.
But the tender grace of a day that is dead
Will never come back to me me. —
Is that commonplace ? Yes, but it is common-
place glorified. The sentiment is only too com-
monplace in the sad experience of us all—“Ay,
madam ; ’tis common ” —and it is just because it
is the-transfiguration of the commonplace that this
poem is a supreme achievement, supreme in the
effectiveness of its absolute simplicity.
A'nd let it be remembered, still in connection
with this part of the subject, that Tennyson’s
power of revelation through the medium of ex-
pression was not a mere matter of word-monger-
mg. It was from the intensity of the inner vision
that arose the craving for adequate expression. It
is a mistake to think that his careful elaboration
in this direction, his long search for what has been
happily called “ the predestined word,” his skilful
marshalling of his vowel and consonantal sounds
so that each becomes the co-efficient of the other,
and each co-efficient is raised to its highest power
—it is a mistake to think that all this is a matter
of mere verbal artistry. All this word-manipula-
tion was but the carrying out of the sacred duty
which he felt to be laid upon him of communicat-
ing through the fittest medium the inward grace
which had been bestowed upon himself. If his
spirit was “finely touched,” he knew that it was
“ but to fine issues ” ; and if he was so careful to
perfect his medium, it was that the “fine issues”
might pass through it to the world not only un-
corrupted from their native quality, but enriched
in the passage.
*"* * *
Let us turn now to another of Tennyson’s alleged
limitations. It is said that he lacks passion. It
is complained that he had too little of the element
of disturbance in his nature to be a sympathetic
exponent of the passions of humanity. It is re-
gretted that there is not more self-abandonment
about him. It is desiderated that he should come
down now and again from those classic heights of
his, and “let’ himself go.” In his treatment of
passion he is not wild enough to suit the advanced
ideas of the age; there is too little of the riot of
tlie blood ; there is too much restraint; too much
reticence ; in point of fact, too much morality.
Now, it appears to me that, the critics who pass
this judgment on Tennyson limit the meaning of
the word passion to passion that Iras broken
through the barriers of self-control. Of course, it
is the passion of love they are referring" tb, and
when they say that Tennyson lacks passion, it is
too evident that what they are really lamenting is
the comparative absence from his pages of “ the
raptures and roses of vice.” But what, right have
then to limit the clamant insistencies of sensuous
humanity to the sphere of the illicit ? What
reason have they for assuming—unless it be that
they are unable to conceive otherwise—that where
self-control.is strong, passion must be weak? I
shou ,d say the reyerse is more likely to be the case.
Self-control, like every other, acquired power,
attains its strength through.exercise, and this very
exercise consists in a constantly-renewed trial of
strength between the will and the force of desire.
It was owing to no tameness of spirit or poverty
of blood that Tennyson preferred to , let his
thoughts dwell among whatsoever things are pure,
and lovely, and of good report. Every constituent
of his nature was characterised by virility, and
virility is the last .'word that one would apply to
the imps of misrule by which the neurotic de-
cadents of the day are possessed, and which they
are pleased to call tlieif “passions.”
For my own part,. I consider -that in nothing
does Tennyson show himself a truer artist than in
his treatment of guilty love. If vice: does not run
rampant through his pages it is because it does
not run rampant through real life. In real life,
tlie concealment of vice is as. much a fact as the
existence of it. It is characteristic of vice to seek
obscurity, and he who sees life clearly, and sees it
whole, will not try to ignore it; but neither will
he sin against his sense of perspective by giving
it a prominence and a dominance which, if carried
into reality, would make social .life impossible.
* * * *
It is because of Tennyson’s avoidance of the un-
seemly, because of his love of the lovely and the
pleasant, that, by the class of critics we are speak-
ing of, he is charged with being “conventional.”
Conventional as regards his choice of subjects he
may have been—though I, for one, do not admit
it —but conventional in his mode of presentation
he certainly was not. His manner may have
become conventional now, but the fault is that of
his imitators, and the very fact that the poets of
the day find such difficulty in getting away from
the Tennysonian manner is sufficient of itself to
prove that, with him, it was an individual origina-
tion, and was therefore not conventional as far as
he was concerned. ‘ ‘ Tennyson, ” says a critic in
a recent issue of Literature, “has fixed the form
of expression for all nineteenth century poets of
thought on all the greater things of human life and
destiny; and adhesion to. it, conscious or uncon-
scious, is probably a necessity for any later poet.”
In other words, Tennyson, by the suffusion of his
immense individuality, has made Tenriysonianism
conventional, but that is a very different thing
from being . himself chargeable with conven-
tionalism.
*: * * *
And here I should like to make a remark which,
though perhaps not altogether in sequential .con-
nection with what has gone before, is not entirely
alien to"the subject. It appears tome that a large
number of the poets of the present day are en-
slaved by a much more baleful influence than the
seductive easiness of conventionalism. The dread
of being, or of being thought, conventional plays
havoc with their poetic faculty. Instead of hold-
ing on to the truth that to conceive greatly, to
conceive finely, is the characteristic exercise of
the highest poetical activities, they give their
approbation and their labor to the evolution of
originalities, singularities, eccentricities. In their
fear, I would say their snobbish fear, of being
classed with personally-conducted travellers over
the old routes, they turn aside into by-paths in
search of the morbid, the corrupt, the grue-
some and the brutal, not because they really
prefer this , class of research, but because of the
facility which it offers for reporting something
new, something that will communicate a sensation
even if it be nausea. No wonder so many of them
are pessimists. But theirs is not the philosophic
pessimism. The beaten highways of life along
which all the great poets have travelled are odious
to them simply because they themselves lack the
faculty of glorifying the commonplace. _ The real
defect of these writers appear to me to lie in their
failure to perceive that, after all, the principal
phenomenon of social life is health. But, alas,
health is conventional! . . . And, to turn again
to Tennyson, if he preferred to dwell chiefly on
what was sane and wholesome, such preference
was only a part of the insatiable hunger after
perfection which was in him a passion—a passion
that was not less fervent because, after his
long upward struggle through clouds of doubt
and over the molten lava of fiery emotion, it
manifested itself, when he had reached his majes-
tic calm, in a serenity of thought and clarity of
expression which makes us forget that the steady
beauty of the light which illumines his pages was
the result of inward processes of heat. Had he
stopped halfway, and showed us only the cloud and
the lava, he would have been lauded nowadays as
one of the “poets of revolt.” Yes: that is it.
The “poets of revolt” are the poets who have
stopped half-way. But Tennyson climbed the
whole way, nor lost in. his, upward struggle one
smallest portion of the equipment with which lie
started on his journey. Sympathy, clarity,
sanity—sympathy of feeling, clfirity of thought,
sanity of soul—these were his equipment. (Sym-
pathy, clarity, sanity; these three; and lam
almost inclined to say, the greatest of these is
sanity.

“Scot”: Forming# has been under discussion
in Dunedin. A witness in the Laira case in the Supreme
Court, spoke of the Wakatipu being foreninst the sand-
dredge. “This is the first introduction of Scotch into
the evidence,’’ commented counsel Solomon. “But,”
said .In t.ice Penuefather, "foreninst is every hit as good
Irish as it is Scotch.” And a newspaper corr. added:
1 No Scotchman says foreninst. . . . Forenenst is a good
old English word, as jiirehiiist is good present-day Irish.”
[Murray's dictionary gives foiiwnt and fornemt as .current?
Scotch and N. English ; says nothing of the Irish use,
comntnn enough. It is a compound of fore and orient,
and means “ right opposite to, over against.'’ But there
is a second (Scotch) meaning, “ with regard to.”]
E. J. : Some years ago a man named Hobler
published a biography of his father. He declared that
the old man was one of the first to take up and stock
country on the Murrumbidgee, and that the blacks called
that, region “Nivya Nivva.” Since then the term has
been applied to all back country in Australia.
“ The mere opinion of tlie literary critic, then,
his I-like-it or I-like-it-not, is at bottom of liLtle value.
Indeed, whenever he ventures upon dogmatism, whether
in laying down the rules and canons of art or in passing a
sweeping judgment upon anyone’s reputation, he is try-
ing to do what no predecessor has successfully accom-
plished. But a useful career lies before him if he will be
content to bring into harmony a man and his work so
that, the inevitable excellence and the equally inevitable
blemish may be seen in due relation to their cause. In
fact, his worthiest ambition is to reduce that mass of
ignorance and prejudice which prevents the vulgar from
choosing between good and evil. For to glorify the
worthless is an injury to the worthy.”— Lond. Academy.
TEE GHOST SHIP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Behold her on the silent sea,
Yon vessel like a spirit there!
Moved in a dream’s reality,
As if she trod the air.
None can tell from what creek or bay
She sailed out, or by night or day j
They watch her like a vision gone
Over the Sea’s oblivion.
And, lo ! she fades a spectre thin,
Part of the moonlight and the sea,
As if the waves and stars met in
A moment’s phantasy !
Or is it they stand hushed apart
And listen to her breathing heart
As the ghostly pulse is stirred
To the voice of a faery bird ?
—A bird that chaunts somewhere between
The waters and the starry skies
A mystic song of what has been
Seen not of human eyes
Since when the world grew into birth,
And the white Moon enamoured Earth:
And she as in a vision gone
Moves to the music on—and oil.
R. Crawford.

Established 1849.
ProVideot Society.
With the close of the year 1898, the Australian Mutual Provident Society will complete FIFTY YEARS of its
existence. Members are invited to make this a RECORD YEAR by taking out additional policies themselves, and by
inducing their friends who are not already assured to become members. ALL POLICIES ISSUED before
the end of the year will share in the division of profits to be made as at 31st December next. Amount of Cash Surplus
divided amongst the members during the 49 years ending 1897 was £7,726,170* The Cash Bonus distributed amongst
the Policyholders for the single year 1897 was £445,235. EVERY YEAR A BONUS YEAR.
Accumulated Funds, £14,479,578. Annual Income, £2,094,706.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Rebels.
("And some are rebels on the hills!”)
A flowery path was our path to tread,
A beaten road that the rest had gone,
But how could we stay when our hearts were wed
To the mountain-tops and the cliffs of stone ?
In vain for us down in the valley shone
The golden sun and the white mists whirled,
And out on the mountains lorn and lone
We are the rebels of all the world.
The clouds stoop chill to us, overhead,
And round us for ever the chained winds groan ;
Beneath us the warmth of the world is spread
And the garden grace of the Earth lies prone
Where roses are ruddy and brown bees drone,
And, lover to lover with leaves dew-pearled,
The oak-trees croon in an undertone—
We are the rebels of all the world.
Why have we climbed to the cliffs and fled ?
And why from the fairest hopes have flown
To war with a wandering weird instead,
By all stars flouted and all winds blown ?
Because we are heirs to an outlaw throne
Where Freedom flaunts on the flags unfurled,
And the mountain tracts are our own, our own •
We are the rebels of all the world !
l’envoi.
We have charted a realm on the hills unknown,
We have chosen a home in the cloud-wreaths
curled;
Though princes may pity and kings condone,
We are the rebels of all the world !
WILL H. OCILVIE.
The Honeymoon Trip.
Oh, this ’ere little ship is a tough, taut ship,
An’ she creams the billows around
With snow from her prow and the froth of her lip
As she leaps to the outward bound.
The wind is a-singin’ in the low, grey sky
(An’ the cap.’s as drunk as a lord),
There’s a star in the west like a tomcat’s eye
Or a jewel in a rajah’s sword.
Oh, she’s sweet as a bride with a long white train
A-trailed on a carpet o’ blue;
The wind an’ the waves make the organ’s refrain
As she hastens the aisle-way thro’.
An’ the six little stars that glimmer astern
Are the bridesmaids decked in their best ;
An’ the lights of the “ Cross ” right royally burn,
And the moon is a lonesome guest.
Ah, you’re off, my Ship, on your honeymoon trip,
And the waves fall sobbing behind ;
The lights of the Home-Land sorrowf’lly slip,
And the sails swell out to the wind.
Good-bye ! Au revoir ! For ye’ll comeback a-day,
Your breast to the lolloping sea,
Till you swim the warm wave of the green wide bay
In the peace of the ’long shore lee.
HUGH RAYMOND M'CRAE.
When the Kain is Makin' Crosses in
the Air.
You ken hear the old man shoutin’
As us kids start fer an outin’
When the rain is makin’ crosses in the air ;
In his voice a tone complainin’—
“ Carn’t yer see how rough it’s rainin’?
Come inside an’ stop yer sniv’lin’. Do yer hear ? ”
So us boys an’ little Linda
Flatten noses at the winder
Watchin’ other kids a-playin’ on the square ;
An’ it makes us sulky—rather !
He’s a fair dead nark is father,
When the rain is makin’ crosses in the air.
Fer it’s ree-ly only spittin’.
But the sunshine on it sittin’
Makes it look as if the clouds were in a tear;
There’s a rainbow in the sky, there,
But —the old ’un won’t look nigh there
When the rain is makin’ crosses in the air.
But we beat him now quite easy
Ev’ry time the grass gets greasy
When the rain is makin’ crosses in the air,
Fer it goes without me sayin’—
When it’s jest too wet fer playin’
Why it’s jest too wet fer workin’ —which is square.
An’ nex’ mornin’, risin’ early,
Don’t the old un’s tongue grow curly
As he looks about for wood that isn’t there ?
An’ the balm he gits to stop it
I s —“ Well, dad, how could we chop it
When the rain was makin’ crosses in the air ? ”
MILKY WHITE.
Ariel’s Song.
I drink thee, breath of the soft June air;
The blue-bell sweet shall my beaker be.
The draught I drain has a fragrance rare—
(O ! where is the drinker to match with me?)
To thee, sweet maid, with the soft blonde hair
And blue eyes smiling—a melting pair,
To thee!
I drink thee, silvery morning dew,
The tulip tall shall my beaker be.
The fairest tulip that meets my view—
(O ! where is the drinker to match with me ?)
To thee, sweet maid, with the nut-brown hair
And dark eyes dancing—a witching pair,
To thee !
I drink thee, beam of the morning sun,
The rose, the rose shall my beaker be.
Spring—wine —sunshine in my draught are one
(O ! where is the drinker to match with me
To thee, sweet maid, with the raven hair
And black eyes flashing—a roguish pair,
To thee !
Q.
P. LUFTIG.

HISTORY or
YANKEE DOODLI
SEE
DIXSON’S TOBACCO HOARDINGS.
lO An>?ri?a. |0 AGstralia.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
General, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight
aroused them. Marching with drum
and fife was an old man, Jeremiah Wyn-
ton, and his son and grandson. The
old father, with silvery flowing locks,
sturdily strode through the field. The
son with bandaged head fifing lustily,
the youth, catching the spirit of his
grandsire, rolled out with him the soul-
inspiring rattle of the drum. Father, son,
and grandson had accompanied the
troops as volunteers, and were ever in
the forefront of the fight. The fierce
light of battle was in their eyes, they
knew no fear, and many a wild cheer
ai ose, as on that fateful day Yankee
Doodle was heard as never was heard
before or since.
YANKEE Doodle as a Trade Mark
is admitted by all to have no equal
on this side of the World, and, as
a Tobacco, Dixson and Sons determined
that it should enjoy an equal reputation,
consequently the resources of their great
establishment, and patient trials and ex-
periments (involving several visits to
America in search of suitable leaf with
which to produce the tobacco sought
after) resulted in a Dark and Bright
Tobacco, which to-day CHALLENGES
THE WHOLE WORLD IN COMPE-
TITION, and that it is appreciated may
be gathered from the fact that the sales
for New South Wales alone now reach
Tons per Day.
The objection of many smokers to
Dark Tobacco is its strength. Dixson’s
have overcome this, and Dark Yankee
Doodle, while possessing a delightful
flavor, is not strong. Then again, Light
or Bright Tobacco, although mild, burns
the tongue. Bright Yankee Doodle is
equally mild, richer than other tobaccos
in aroma, and DOES NOT PARCH
THE TONGUE. Every smoker should
TRY FOR HIMSELF.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 31, 1898.
The Broken-down Machine.
Three Legislatures—those of Maoriland,
Victoria, and New South Wales—got to the
end of their session almost simultaneously,
and the end of them all was more or less
chaotic. That is to say, two of them closed
up badly, and the third did it much worse.
The last days of the Cornstalk session were
mere football scrum and dog-fight, and there
were 50-hour sittings, and everybody’s temper
got on edge, and quarrels and riots arose,
and Bills were either rushed through without
consideration or thrown out without thought
or intelligence. Each one of the Par-
liaments hove a great part of its cargo of
legislation overboard within sight of port, as
always happens in the last days of a session,
and the remainder was landed much damaged.
Premiers Seddon and Turner kept a firm
hold on their respective parties to the end
and fled into recess with some kind of dig-
nity ; and Premier Reid, who lost his hold
of everything in the latter days, was kicked
there without any dignity at all. But the
verdict, even in the best of these cases, was
that Parliamentary Government, as it is now
practised, is a failure and a fraud.
* * m *
Every session sees the same record. It is
an understood thing that all important
reforms must be introduced first into the
Assembly, because it is the House that
represents the people, while the other isn’t.
If any of them were first introduced in the
Council, and that unrepresentative institu-
tion threw them out without the Assembly
having a chance to consider them, the
visible result would be the same as if it
threw them out after the Assembly had con-
sidered them. They would only be thrown
out in either case. But the people, through
their representatives, must have a chance to
express an opinion, and reform measures
must go to the Council with all the weight of
popular opinion behind them, if they are to
have any show at all—not that they have
any too much show even then. If the Vic-
torian One-Man-One-Vote Bill, for instance,
had been thrice started in the House of 48Rich
Landlords, and thrice rejected there, it would
be technically no worse off than it is now,
after being thrice passed by the Assembly
and as often thrown out by the Landlords.
Also, a great deal of the Assembly’s time
would have been saved. But any possible
hope that it has lies in its presentation to
the Council as a measure that is backed by
a large majority of the people’s representa-
tives, and it is the same with every other
democratic idea. Therefore, it is necessary
to originate all important Bills in the Lower
House, and in the early days of the session
Bills rain on that Chamber like locusts. In
these early days the House sits about three
days a week. It adjourns early. It wastes
its time over abstract motions about the
abolition of sin, also motions of censure,
personal explanations, and complaints about
the late lamented dog that was found in the
Government tank. As a rule, the session is
two-thirds over before anything is really
done, and before the slipshod Ministry
awakes. Then, in the frantic tail-end of the
session, the Estimates are galloped through,
and £2,000,000 of loan money is voted in
five minutes, and forty unworkable Bills are
passed without consideration in the small
hours of the morning when the Assembly is
practically dead to the world, and fifty more
are thrown overboard, and the session con-
cludes with all the repose and dignity of
something that is blown up by dynamite.
» * * •
The Upper House has practically nothing
to do in the first half or two-thirds of the
session but drowse and accumulate bitter
contempt for the people’s representatives.
But in the last week or so it receives about
two dozen long, intricate measures that the
tired Assembly has rushed through without
really knowing what is in them. The
Council is requested to consider all these in-
telligently and in detail, and to do them at
the rate of about one important measure per
half-hour. Any brain it ever had grows
addled with the pressure, and any good-will
it ever had grows sour. The Council’s
situation is an anomalous fool of a situation
anyhow. It is supposed to put right, in
about a week, all that the Assembly has put
wrong in four months. At the same time it
isn’t supposed to make any serious amend-
ments because that is flying in the face of
the people’s delegates. And if it rejects
Bills it is jumping on the most sacred
principles of Representative Government.
And if it passes them with all their faults it
thereby shows its own uselessness, and fails
in its duty as a revising Chamber. And if
it does revise them carefully (it never does,
but this possibility is mentioned to round off
the argument), then the prorogation over-
takes it in the middle of the work, and half
the results of the session are lost among the
unfinished business. The average Council is
stupid and old and Tory and cantankerous,
and has no constructive power worth men-
tioning, but the kind of treatment it meets
is a fair excuse for some of its vices. A few
rare Premiers, like Atkinson, Ballanoe,
Seddon, and Turner, contrive to make a
semblance of keeping the Assembly up to
its work, but in New South Wales, at all
events, half the legislation is done by the
Lower House in its sleep in the last days
when everything is falling to pieces, and the
Council either kicks it through or kicks it
out at random. And the awful state of that
legislation, when it is done, is fully ex-
plained by the mode of doing it.
* * * *
The Bulletin has an idea of a new kind
of Upper House which would put an entirely
different complexion upon this dreadful
business. It would have the Upper House
paid like the Assembly, equal to it in num-
bers, equally responsible to the people, and
elected on exactly the same franchise, with
only one difference—that it would be chosen
by the whole nation voting as one constitu-
ency, while the Assembly would be elected
as at present. To make the new system
workable it would probably be necessary to
elect Ministers for a fixed term by the com-
bined vote of both Houses, instead of letting
them happen accidentally as at nresent; and,
as both Houses would have equal power, it
would be essential to make a provision for
deadlocks by referring disputed points to a
joint sitting. This scheme would make the
Upper House useful instead of useless as at
present. It would be a working body,
whereas the present institution has either to
say “ditto” to the other House all the time,
and be absolutely superfluous, or say “no ”
to the people's chamber, and thus outrage
every principle of Representative Govern-
ment. Also, it would create a legislative
body which would represent the country as a
whole, and whose business it would be to
consider the interests of the country as a
whole, whereas at present every man only
holds a brief for the interests of his one little
constituency, and the country at large hasn’t
a single representative.
* • * *
And, in addition to everything else, it would
subdivide the work and give Parliament a
chance to get its head above the slough of
arrears of legislation. If both Houses were
equally powerful, equally representative, and
equally democratic, then new business could
be started in either Chamber indiscrimin-
ately. Half the Bills of the session could be
started in one House and the rest in the other;
the Houses could do an equal share of the
dreary digging and delving in committee, and
divide the work of throwing out undesirable
products between them. Each one, of
course, would have to revise the other’s
work, but if they represented the same
people and interests and popular senti-
ment they could take a good deal of
each other’s work for granted. Between
them there would, at least, be twice
as much time to do the work in,
and there would be a fair chance that every
important measure would be carefully over-
hauled in every detail by one representative
House anyhow. A good many of them might
have the luck to be intelligently considered
by both Houses, but as a general thing the
fresh intellect of one people’s House would
get a look-in at every measure. The pre-
sent system works very differently. Taking,
as an example, the chaotic session of Now
South Wales Parliament which has just
closed, fully one-half of the most important
measures were huddled through by the
Assembly when it was in the last stage of
weariness and aggravation, and had no brain
for details, and was half-asleep and one-
quarter dead through continuous sittings.
They were badly drafted in red-hot haste,
and most of them went through with all
their imperfections still in them. Then the
Council looked at them for a moment with
the last exhausted scrap-end of its intellect
also, and in most cases let them go into the
statute-book or the waste-paper receptacle
promiscuously. The Council had very little
power to do good if it had wanted to, and it
was too tired to want to, and it hadn’t the
time in any case.
* * * *
The parliamentary machine as at present
constituted is in a constant state of partial
breakdown. If it doesn’t relegate some of
its work to somebody it will probably get
more broken-down each year till there is
very little of it left. The Council, as at
present constituted, isn’t an aid, but an
enemy. Not only has the Assembly to do
all the hard work itself, ( but it has to find
time to jump on its nominal partner and
keep him from undoing it. The Council,
being Tory by the very nature of its consti-
tution, can t be trusted to do any detail work
on democratic measures, or to sift out good
schemes from bad, or to initiate anything
that is reasonably desirable. If it rejects
measures that doesn’t mean that they are
probably the kind that a democratic com-
munity should reject; it only means that
the Assembly has to do all the work over
again. If the Assembly sends up a demo-
cratic measure in a crude and unwork-
able form it knows in advance that the
nominee Chamber doesn’t want to make
it any less unworkable, and that its
amendments are probably intended to make
the scheme more impossible than it was be-
fore. The Assembly, instead of having a
packhorse to carry part of its goods on its
uphill journey, has to carry all the goods it-
self and the horse too. And the uselessness
of keeping ahorse that won’t carry anything,
and which has to be carried itself, requires
no demonstration.
South Australia’s Xmas Crisis.
South Australian Parliament adjourned for
the Xmas fowl in the middle of a political dead-
lock ; and unless the ameliorating influences of
that bird help to soften the asperities that now
exist, it will meet again about three weeks
hence with the deadlock in as good working
order as ever. The two Houses have come into
violent collision, and, as usual, the House of
Property—the one which represents far the
smaller body of electors, and to which the
Government isn’t responsible—has a certain
advantage. The popular Chamber wants to do
something, and the unpopular one wants to
leave things as they are, and so long as the two
Houses have equal power, and there is no joint
sitting or Referendum, or other authority that
can be effectively appealed to in case of dis-
agreement, the House which is quite content
to leave things as they are starts with a few
yards lead. It has merely to lie down and
sleep and wait the course of events—unless the
country wakes it up with a brick. The South
Australian Upper House is certainly the most
Democratic thing of its kind in Australia—not
that that is saying much. South Australia is
the only one of the five mainland provinces
which pays its Councillors, and there is no
rigid Fat qualification for members. But
its constituency is narrowed by a property
qualification for electors, and the result is that
the Council represents 46,000 electors, while
the Assembly speaks on behalf of over 138,000.
Moreover, the Council is a continuous body.
One-third of its members retire every three
years, so it takes nine years to make a complete
change in its personnel. Even a pretty good
Democrat is liable to go bad when he is freed
from all responsibility to the electors for so
long a period as nine years. The Council can’t
be dissolved at a crisis. It can’t be cleaned
out and completely renewed when a wave of
popular fervor sweeps over the land of Crow-
eaters. It never represents the popular senti-
ment of the people ; just before a Council elec-
tion due in (say) 1899, one-third of it would
represent the way the people felt in 1896, and
one-third would have a mandate dating back to
1893, and the remainder would have no in-
structions from its constituents of later date
than 1890.
* * • •
Therefore, even in Democratic S.A., the
Upper House is mostly a slow and eminently
Tory institution. There was an occasion, some
years ago, when for a little while a democratic
majority had possession of the House of Sleep,
and on that occasion the Ministry foolishly
threw away an opportunity which may not
recur again for a dozen years, or a century. It
ought to have dropped everything else and
spread itself on the work of liberalising the
Council franchise while it had a majority to do
it with, and shortening the Councillors’ tenure
of office, and making the Upper House dis-
soluble like the Assembly, so that it would be
as directly responsible to the people as the
other branch of the Legislature. Then, having
made certain that the Council would not re-
lapse into its usual state of Toryism, it could
have proceeded with other reforms at its
leisure. But the S.A. Government let its one
opportunity slide. It passed some valuable
measures, certainly, during the brief period
when the progressive party bossed both Houses,
but the usual Council bye-election came along,
and the Fat element got on top again, and the
chance drifted away, to return in the year of
GoD-knows-when, A.D.
* * * *
Premier Kingston’s Household Suffrage Bill,
devised for the purpose of largely widening
the Council franchise, was presented to that
body when it was too late to hope for any good
results, and the Council carried it outside with
contumely, and rolled it downhill with derision.
Whereupon the Kingston Ministry proposed,
in the Assembly, to submit the matter to a
Referendum at the coming Assembly general
election, and obtain, from the Assembly’s con-
stituents, a direct expression of opinion as to
whether they were or were not in favor of the
proposed liberalising of the Council’s constitu-
tion. The proposal was, to a certain extent,
mere frill and ornament. Nobody doubts the
result. Nobody supposes for a single moment
that a solid majority of the Assembly’s con-
stituents—the manhood and womanhood of the
province—is not in favor of the wholesale repair
of the obstructive House of Slumber. The
mass of the people has stood by the Kingston
Ministry with a steady persistency and an un-
wavering confidence quite novel in the un-
settled, stormy, changeful politics of S.A. And
there is every reason to believe that it will
continue doing so. In any case, the direct
announcement by the Assembly’s constituents
that it was in favor of Council reform would
make no legal difference at all in the situation.
The Council is only responsible to its own con-
stituents, who are the Tory fragment of the
community. And, according to the letter of
the law, it doesn’t need to pay any immediate
attention even to them, for if the Council is
wholly Tory now, and its constituents, having
changed their views, wanted to make it wholly
Liberal, it would take them six years to bring
the change about, and the Upper House is
under no legal obligation to take the slightest
notice of anybodj'’s new ideas till then. If the
mass of the people said they were in favor of
Council reform they would only tell the Council
in a formal way what it knows perfectly
well already. The only difference would be
that the Council would then have to give up
swearing that it didn’t know it.
* * • *
But the Council’s attitude is that no Referen-
dum can be taken without its consent, which it
has no intention of granting. On the other
hand, Premier Kingston maintains that the
Assembly can take a Referendum of its own
constituents who naturally include the
Council’s constituents—whenever it pleases, on
its own responsibility. According to the strict
letter of the law, it is quite possible that King-
ston is wrong; according to equity and the
principles of popular government, it is dead
certain that he is right. The theory of all
popular government is that the majority rules.
The majority can’t rule unless it has the means
of definitely expressing how it wants to rule on
the matter under discussion, and the Govern-
ment which proposes to lay a disputed question
before the entire adult community has all
the moral right on its side, as against the
House which refuses to let the people be heard.
Yet there is no written law authorising a direct
vote of the people, and there can’t be any such
written law unless it is passed by both Houses ;
and the Council, which doesn’t want to hear
from the people on any such unpleasant subject,
certainly wouldn’t pass the law in question. If
Kingston now formally introduced a Bill
authorising the Referendum, and got it passed
by the Assembly, and saw it ignominiously
fired out by the Council, he would thereby
admit that the Council’s sanction was
necessary, and, failing to get it, the
appeal to the nation would have to fall
through. So he wisely goes on the principle
that there is no law to prohibit the Referendum;
and on the broad and rational idea that the
Assembly has a right to ask the opinion of its
constituents as freely as it pleases, on any sub-
ject it pleases, and in any way it thinks best;
and that the people, in a community where the
people are supposed to govern, have a right to
express their opinions fully on any matter of
national importance. According to the narrow
and literal reading of things, Kingston may
be wrong ; the man who is right in equity is
generally wrong in law. But it is a hundred
times better to be wrong by trusting the people
too much than be right by trusting them too
little.
• * * •
The S.A. Council expressed its lofty disap-
proval of the whole matter by refusing to pass
the Appropriation Bill and closing its front
door with a bang for four weeks. The Assembly
replied by again asserting that the Referendum
should be taken, whether the Council disap-
proved and banged its door or not, and that it
had an absolute right to consult its own con-
stituents if it wanted to. The Council alleges
that it won’t proceed with any business, or
vote any money, or do anything except
curse about its offended dignity, until the
Assembly backs down, and the Assembly
practically defies the Council to do its
worst. It is fervently to be hoped that
the Assembly will stand its ground to the
bitter end, come what will. Whatever the
dry, yellow parchment aspect of the case may
be, or whatever the bloodless letter of the law
may say, the Kingston Government has every
principle of justice and fair-dealing on its side.
Even the law is a dubious matter ; there is no
statute dealing with the case, and there is
nothing to go upon save mouldy precedents—if
there even is a mouldy precedent. In any case,
statute or no statute, every genuine Democrat
must be on the side of the party which proposes
to carry its appeal to the nation at large, and
against the one which refuses to let the nation


be heard. Also, every Democrat must be on
Kingston’s side if only because he is the first
Australian Premier, since the far-back days of
Higinbotham and Berry and M'Culloch in
Victoria, who has shown himself ready to fight
for the rights of the people’s representatives
against the arrogance of the House of Property.
For the sake of a good cause whose supporters,
as a rule, are woefully gone at the knees, it is
to be hoped that he will maintain his attitude
to the last. The Council will pass the Appro-
priation Bill, anyhow, before the interest on
the public debt falls due, for if that went un-
paid there would be such excitement in com-
mercial circles that Property would be in-
juriously affected.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Vic. Railway Deficit.
The great Mathieson, who took over the Vic.
railways with such a blare of trumpets, is fall-
ing into disrepute with politicians. And his
hustling Traffic Manager, W. Fitzpatrick, is
spoken of as his successor when his term ex-
pires. The Age keeps asking why, under the
Mathieson regime, the Victorian railways lost
£400,000 last year, while the N.S.W. lines
made a profit of £30,000, and begins to broadly
hint that Mathieson must show r a £30,000
profit also, or else go as Speight did. Yet the
reasons of the discrepancy are perfectly plain
and obvious to anyone who is willing to see
them. N.S. W. didn’t make a profit of £30,000;
the Auditor-General reports that the railway
accounts are the usual “ fake,” and that there
was really a loss of over £30,000. Victoria’s
railway expenditure includes about £60,000 a
year for pensions; N.S.W. railway pensions are
charged to the Superannuation Fund. The
Melbourne suburban railway traffic is cut into
by the competing trams ; the Sydney trams are
State property, and don’t compete. Victorian
rates are a shade lower. Its railway system
has to pay interest on some of Gillies’ boom
lines that have been wholly abandoned,
whereas there is nothing of the sort in N.S.W.
Victoria has four times as much railway
as N.S.W. in proportion to area, and 25 per
cent, more than N.S.W. in proportion to popu-
lation—in other words, it is over-railwayed;
and its lines are crowded together in the centre
of the province where they compete ruinously
with each other. Also, Victoria has no great
coal traffic like N.S.W.; and its harvest for
the last three years averaged 5£ bushels per
acre against N.S. W.’s 10 bushels, which badly
damaged the grain traffic. Finally, its railway
debt bears a higher average interest than
N.S. W.’s because less of it was incurred since
the era of 3 per cent, loans. These are the
obvious reasons why Mathieson doesn’t make
the Victorian lines pay, and when the auto-
cratic Syme, of the Age, tells him vaguely that
it has to be done Syme is demanding that bricks
shall grow on trees.
The Moral of Joe Myers.
Joseph Myers, the Wellington (M.L.) munici-
pal councillor, who lately made a sprint for
parts unknown, whereby various people were
greatly surprised to the extent, it is alleged, of
£6OOO, left a letter behind him in which he
stated that bookmakers and 100-per-shent
money-lenders had been his ruin. Inquiry
seems to fully bear out his statements, too.
There are two notable features in Myers’ case.
One is that, though he was a vigorous gambler,
it wasn’t the tote which wrecked him. Nor
did it ever wreck any other gambler that this
paper knows of. No one ever puts sensational
sums on a horse per medium of the tote,
because one peculiarity of that instrument is
that the more you put on your particular
horse the less odds you get. If there are 10
horses in a race, and each has 10 £1 subscribers
on him, then the odds are 9 to 1. And if the
plunger—the man who has ultimately to bolt tor
Monte Video—comes along and puts £2OO on
one horse, then he reduces the odds he is get-
ting to much less than two to one, while raising
the odds to the people who have backed the
other horses to something over 25 to 1. The
tote is a pleasant thing to put a pound or two
on, but it is discouraging for the man who
wants to put on £lOO or £2OO, therefore it is
distinctly unfavorable to the huge kind of
gambling which made Joseph Myers light
out for South America, or Greece, or space.
If there is any individual whose promising
career has been blighted by the tote his address
and the way he managed to blight himself are
at present unknown, and if the M.L. Govern-
ment had the courage to trust to the tote alone,
and make bookmaking an offence punishable
with five years hard labor, the next Joseph
Myers would probably be able to stay at home
in the buzzom of his family. That is one
point. The other is that an anti-usury
law which will come down on the 100
per shent man like an avalanche might
also help to save the next Joe Myers from
downfall. The tremendous frauds charged
against Joseph may or may not be proved, but
the fact remains that he was a promising pub-
lic man who lost all his own money—whether
he lost £6OOO of other people’s or not; and he
did it neither through drink nor through
the totalisator. In fact, it seems that if be
had stuck to the tote he would have been
Saved, for the machine wouldn’t have given
him credit. It was wild betting with book-
makers, who took his p.n., and thereby led
him into debt, that made the trouble. A
machine which would never have allowed
Joseph, even in his wildest moments, to bet
beyond the cash he actually had in his pocket
would have been his salvation—provided he had
been absolutely restricted to it.
David of Macedon.
David Syme, the Melbourne Age autocrat, has
felt called upon to refute, in the columns of his
journal, certain charges against his character
levelled by Tory Frank Madden on the floor
of the House. " Angry Syme’s refutation suc-
ceeded in making Madden look an even more
wrong-headed politician than he was known to
be previously, but it did not leave him entirely
dead upon the field. Assuming that his princi-
pal allegations are satisfactorily squelched, he
still can raise a kick. Madden stated that
SvtUE had come by crooked ways to the posses-
sion of 60 acres of land at Mbunt Macedon,
Vic. He told the Legislative Assembly that
the land was selected under circumstances of
jobbery and corruption in 1865, as being aurif-
erous soil, and that 40 of the 60 acres were
obtained by “dummy” means ; whereas David
SyMEclearlyproves that he got the land from the
Crown for residential purposes in 1872, without
“dummying” or false-pretences of any kind.
In his statement of the case concerning the sale
of the aforesaid freehold estate to the Vic.
Government, in ’B5 or thereabouts, Syme cuts
a less triumphant figure. Madden practically
accused him of “ squeezing ” the Government
when the bargain was made, and his explana-
tion doesn’t get away from the fact that Madden
had cause for suspicion. Governor Loch wanted
a country residence. Up to then, Governors
had not enjoyed the free run of a “ summer
retreat,” where they could breathe cool moun-
tain air at the country’s expense. The first
time Gov. Loch went to Macedon he rented
David Syme’s suitable homestead ; later on, he
suggested that the Macedon property should be
made a vice-regal possession. Therefore the
obsequious Government bought the “retreat”
at Macedon, and added it to the other expenses
of vice-regal maintenance. Here is David
Syme’s own account of this transaction ;
Mr. Skene (Surveyor-General) was instructed by the
Government of the day to visit the place and value the
property, but I never saw him and was not aware at the
time that he had been instructed to make a valuation.
Mr. Skene’s report is amongst the papers filed in tins
matter, and in that report he values the land at £1145,
improvements, £2684; total, £3829. And he adds, “ I
have no doubt but that the buildings and other improve-
ments cost in the course of completion a much larger
sum than is set down by me as their present value, on
account of the much higher prices then ruling for mate-
rial, and the enhanced cost of transport arising from the
entire absence of road communication at the time. This
I would estimate at not less than 25 per cent.” According
to Mr. Madden I . . . put it into Mr. Skene’s head to in-
flate the valuation by 25 per cent. All this is so utterly
and absurdly improbable on the face of it, &c., &c., &c.
Here the influential newspaper proprietor fails
to show himself ill-used by the vituperative
Madden. The value of any property is the
price at which it can be replaced and not the
price which it cost to put upon any given spot
at a time when material and labor were dearer,
and the accomplishment of the work involved a
larger expenditure of time. Still in these gaudy
days the Government was understood to exist
for the purpose of being a fool, and apparently
it simply “ resumed ” Syme in the same regard-
less fashion as it did everybody else. The
transaction was a small Government job, but
pretty nearly everything that happened about
then was a job. And if any government
offered to buy Frank Madden’s property at
25 per cent, over its present value ! !
Forty-Eight Hours on Melbourne Trams.
As a result of the Commission of Inquiry, Melb.
Tramway Go. throws a sop to the growling dog
in the shape of 2s. per week increase of wages
to its drivers and conductors. There is no pre-
tence of generosity in the matter —it is purely
a piece of policy ; and at the same time that
the left hand offers the increase the right gets
in a back-handed blow in the shape of a warn-
ing that any demand for shorter hours will be
strenuously resisted. Yet the Sunday follow-
ing this announcement a meeting of over 800
employes unanimously demanded the eight-
hour day, with extra pay for overtime, and
forwarded to the Premier a request that the
recommendation of the Commission in this re-
spect should be given effect to. Premier
Turner was lauded at the meeting for his
pluck in appointing a Commission ; it will be
interesting to see if his alleged backbone re-
tains its pristine stiffness. Meanwhile the
5 per cent, rise is not such a gaudy circum-
stance as it appears. It apparently applies
onlv to drivers and conductors, though the
whole of the employes have, during the last
seven years, suffered no less than five reductions
of 5 per cent. each. Furthermore, comparing the
returns for the last-published four months of
’9B with those of ’97— all slack months—the
income of the company shows an actual in-
crease of .£7320, or about £22,000 per annum,
out of which the shareholders throw these
employes £6OOO per annum. It was stated
at the Sunday meeting that the men were
willing to forego all increase of pay, willing to
put up with their five reductions of wages, if
the Eight-hour Day were granted them. This
in face of the fact that sec. 62 of the Tramways
Act provides that neither men nor animals shall
be worked for more than 48 hours per week,
except on special occasions, and, even then,
for not more than 60 hours per week, overtime
to be paid for at a special rate. It is this
“ special ” rate for all time over 48 hours that
the company disputes, yet the Act distinctly
uses the word “special,” and it may be
pertinently asked, What and wdiere is this
“special” rate of pay? furthermore, the
company calmly and persistently refuses the
right to combine. Any man who talks
“ unionism,” or who speaks out too freely, is
quietly discharged, wherefore the number of
new hands being continually trained ; even
many of those who gave evidence before the
commission, and to whose action the increased
wages received by present employes are due,
are not now to be found wearing the uniform.
Also, the two plucky individuals who, after
great pressure, moved the chief resolution at
Sunday’s meeting, will probably be induced to
resign shortly. And what does it profit the
province at all ? The sole profit accrues to the
original holders of the watered stock, and to
those who bought, and to those who gamble
therein.
The Great Failure.
What an admission of the utter failure of
Christianity as an improving force in Australia
was Anglican Bishop Thornton’s hysterical de-
nunciation at Ballarat Church Congress of the
vice and crime of country and town life ! Yet
nobody in Victoria has had such encourage-
ment, such a free hand to apply its panaceas,
as have the churches. They were endowed
with princely estates, given the picked sites of
city and town and village for their machinery
depots and “ w’orship-sheds,” and they had the
free run of the education system until enraged
people, in desperation at their feebleness and
chaotic methods, had to expel them. The press
has reported everything they have wished to
say without stint of space or of encouragement
—to such an extent, in fact, that if the Social-
ists or Anarchists had had half the same chance
Australia might now be an organised Social
Democracy or a Free Community. They have
had all the financial and social support that
squatters, bankers, importers, manufacturers,
Bumbledom and Government House could give;
they had on their side all the Conservative
inertia of centuries-old habits of churchgoing,
reverence for the cloth and coupling of the
Bible with Magna Charta ; they have had all
the advantages which a practical monopoly of
university management could give ; and, after
all, one of their highest dignitaries at this time
of day pronounces the most disgraceful of all
words in such circumstances —failure ! And, as
corroboration of the miserable verdict, the
Australian public doesn’t worry its head one
jot about the matter, and is probably rather
satisfied than otherwise that it is so.
Sisyphus and Co.
The Vic. House of 48 Obstructives had matters
pretty much its own way during the last hours
of the session. The People’s Chamber had
been sitting night and day rushing Bills
through, and, on the Friday night, it sat wait-
ing and fuming while the “ other place”—the
Place of Unspeakables—in a calmly deliberate
and leisurely fashion, dallied witli sundry
amendments. The Land Bill was amended,
and, sooner than lose it altogether, the Assem-
bly accepted the alterations. Then the Plural
Voting Abolition Bill —which had already been
amended by the solid 48 —was once more re-
turned to the Assembly with contumely, in a
take-it-or-leave-it manner, whereupon the
Lower House regretfully and painfully laid
it aside in the uncertain hope of an in-
glorious resurrection. The Meat Supervi-
sion Bill, which had occupied a lot of
time in both Houses, being considered too
heavy a morsel for final discussion at the fag-
end of a session, was gently discharged. The
Amended Poisons Act was also assisted outside
and deposited in the rubbish-box ; and the Im-
migration Restriction Bill, which the Council
had so altered that it had become instead a Bill
for the Encouragement of an Unlimited Influx
of Niggers, was cast out, the Assembly refusing
to take any Unlimited Nigger, and the Council
insisting on its amendments. The Turner
Government carried one progressive measure of
some note—the Factories Act Amendment;
but in the main the session ended with the Fat
element on top, as usual.

Gaol for Costs.
A Democratic lawyer to The Bulletin :
Judge Moleswortii the other day issued an attachment
against a Victorian insolvent for failing to obey an order
of the court for the payment of £92 costs, incurred when
insolvent unsuccessfully applied for a certificate of dis-
charge.
The Judge was reluctant to do this, and in fact directed
that the order should lie in the office for a month, but
felt himself compelled to grant it, on account of a
Supreme Court precedent. The Judge said to the
counsel against the insolvent, “You are asking me to
send a man to gaol for not paying a debt which he is un-
able to pay.”
The community is under the delusion that a man cannot
now be sent to gaol for debt; although it is also aware
that a fraudulent omission to pay a debt is punishable.
There was no question or charge of fraud in this particu-
lar case ; and accordingly the attachment order issued
will cause an uneasy feeling of insecurity in many an un-
fortunate debtor’s mind. Other creditors will hardly
view complacently a situation which leaves them out in
the cold altogether or shies a small dividend at them,
while it gives Gent One his costs in full, payment en-
forceable by imprisonment for an indefinite period.
Surely if it is right to allow a man to go insolvent on
account of his inability to pay his debts, it is wrong to
subject him to the worst consequences of non-payment, in
the days before insolvency facilities were allowed, for a
debt incurred by reason of the insolvency. If it is right
that all creditors, before insolvency, must take what they
can get, and he profoundly grateful that the trustees and
lawyers don’t take it all, it is surely wrong that a lawyer
creditor should have the privilege of gaoling the insolvent
in order to get his pound of flesh. It almost looks as if
the lawyers had succeeded in entrenching themselves
above the ordinary law ; that, in fact, the position of
things is “ one law for the rich, six months’ gaol for the
poor, and full costs, enforceable by imprisonment, for the
lawyer.” Such a straining of the doctrine of contempt of
court tends to bring court into greater contempt. It
amounts to a practical repeal by judicial finesse of the
Act for the abolition of imprisonment for debt. Since
the breach of any order of the court is a technical con-
tempt, any refusal (through inability) to pay a debt after
a court has ordered payment thereof is punishable by
imprisonment if there's any logic in Judge Molesworth’s
order. Yet we know that, as a matter of fact, such is not
the case except where lawyers’ costs and such like are
concerned.
The only chance a man thus imprisoned has is to work
up the sympathy of the Cabinet in the matter, and get
released as a favor. The whole thing reeks of the worst
Fleet prison days, and what gets the average man’s dan-
der up is that it’s all for the benefit of the lawyers.
Support—and no Concessions.
The late session of N.S.W. Parliament was a
deplorable affair from a Democratic point of
view. Everything that presented any sem-
blance of progressive legislation went over-
board in the last days of utter demoralisation,
when the House fell to pieces, and grew tired
and snarly, and looked like the man who had
backed the wrong dog coming back from the
dog-fight. The Navigation Bill was dropped—
not that it was much of a Bill in any case.
What is fVanted is a measure to prevent old
worn-out soft-wood coffin - ships—the con-
demned hulks that are bought cheap in South
American ports and sold here for a song
—going to sea and drowning their crews, and
Premier Reid’s pompous, useless, flabby little
measure left things in this respect exactly as
they were. It didn’t fasten the responsibility
of the departing ship’s seaworthiness on
anybody, and when nobody is responsible
nothing is done. Still it threatened to do a little
good in a few small ways, and it was carefully
dropped with the cordial approval of the Govern-
ment’s representative in the Council. The
Early Closing Bill was dropped also. The
State Land Bank Bill of Minister Carrothers
never got really started. The promise con-
tained in the speech at the opening of Parlia-
ment that “ a Bill to give effect to the long-felt
desire for a change in the constitution of the
Legislative Council will be submitted ” proved
to be the same old lie over again. The Local
Government Bill promised in the same speech
never arrived. The Bill providing a very half-
hearted and inefficient court of arbitration in
industrial disputes vanished somewhere, and so
did the measure for abolishing the attachment
of wages for debt. And, by way of making the
collapse complete, Premier Reid broke his pro-
mise to the Labor-party, as he breaks all his
other promises, that he would allow a night for
the further consideration of the Suffield case ;
instead he allowed a minute or two at four
o’clock in the morning, when the dog-tired frag-
ment of a House refused to listen. After this
session it looks as if N.S. W. might as well give
up all hope of getting any good whatever out
of the Labor-party which started with such a
blare of trumpets in ’9l, and might throw that
broken joss in the dust bin. The election of
last July put the Labor-party back in its
original position as the party which held the
balance of power. Reid and Brunker and
Potato Young are men who want office badly
and would do almost anything on this earth to
’ retain it. Reid especially is notorious as a
man who must have office, for sufficiently
obvious reasons. Their valued billets are
wholly at the mercy of the Labor-party. And
if the Labor members had cared to intimate
that the passing of a genuine Navigation Bill—
one that would really put an end to the mur-
derous coffin-ship industry, and an Early-
Closing Bill, and a State Land Bank law,
and a measure for placing the Council
on a democratic basis, was the lowest
price of their support they must have got
their price. The Reid Government could
pass all these things if it liked, by the simple
process of sending enough new democratic
nominees to the Council to carry them—but it
doesn’t like. And the Labor-party made not
one serious effort to force the Government into
motion. The doctrine of support in return for
concessions appears to be played out, for the
support was there all the time and there were
no concessions. The Labor-party still talks
democratic ideas as much as ever, but it does
practically nothing for democratic principles
nowadays.

Drink on the Premises.
A miserable, half-fed, frowsy person named
Tregaski lately broke into Christ Church
(South Yarra, Melbourne) and looted the sacred
fane of its sacramental wine. He devoured
two bottles of the accursed drink in a con-
venient porch. Then the liquor demon caused
him to return to the massive building, and he
was found next morning slumbering placidly
w’itli his pants hung over the altar, and his feet
in the font, and all the communion service
spread on the floor around him. When he was
taken to the local watchhouse Tregaski ex-
plained that he never robbed houses where
people lived, but stuck to churches. Judge
Madden awarded this particularly poor speci-
men of a criminal five years (!) for his last.job
—as much as a man would get for any brutal
assault short of actual murder; and a
particularly vindictive Christian Govern-
ment is now making preparations to go
for him on other charges of church robbery.
There are one or two striking features about
this case, and about cases of sacrilege in general.
When a man burgles a church he gets from
twice to five times as much punishment as if
he broke into any other kind of building, and
the churches are apparently quite satisfied with
this arrangement. At least no one ever heard
of them asking that church burgling should be
reduced to the same level as plain burgling,
or that the miserable wretch who gets an
appalling sentence for entering the church
should be let off a little easier. It is, presum-
ably, because the churches preach the doctrine
of forgiveness in theory that they are so much
more vindictive than the publican or the
tobacconist in practice. Another point is that
the church, which preaches the blessed doctrine
of poverty, shouldn’t have rich communion-
plate lying about to tempt weak brethren to
crime. Third point is, that it doesn’t look well
for a church to keep intoxicating liquor on the
premises, especially as it speaks very severely
about drink on every possible occasion.
♦
The N.S. W. Labor-party hardly made even a
pretence of being in earnest in its demand that
its fellow-toiler, Suffield (at least he was a
fellow-toiler in the days when the Labor mem-
bers really toiled) should have the inquiry to
which the law clearly entitles him. Though it
holds the Ministry absolutely at its mercy, and
can obtain pretty nearly any concession it likes
to demand, it didn’t keep Premier Reid up to
his promise that a night should be allowed for
the further discussion of the matter. When
the subject drifted casually across the horizon
of a thin House at 3 a.m., it hardly even asked
in an audible voice for what it said it wanted.
It didn’t point out that the law distinctly pro-
vides for an inquiry in any case where there is
a doubt as to the righteousness of a conviction,
and that the Government’s persistent allega-
tion that there could be no inquiry except
where fresh evidence was adduced was untrue,
and a grossly illegal contradiction of the
letter of the statute. It didn’t explicitly
tell its bond - slave, G. Reid, that his
billet depended on his doing justice in this
matter, and giving Suffield, the miner, the
same treatment which was so cheerfully conceded
to Rofe, the solicitor. The manner in which
it compelled Reid to back down over his pro-
posed new duties showed clearly that it could
have exacted justice in this matter if it had
wanted to—only it was blankly indifferent. At
the same time, it hadn’t the brutal honesty to
tell Suffield that it didn’t care whether he got
fair play or not —that one worker and one vote
didn’t matter much, anyhow—that it was tired,
and wanted to be left in peace, and didn’t care
a straw for abstract justice. So it mentioned
the matter with a yawn at 4 a.m., and the
House looked at it for a minute or two, and
then the proposal that Suffield should have
the. inquiry to which he is legally entitled was
rejected by a majority of six.
Q. Government is going to the country
without a solitary reforming plank in its platform.
It ran for some years on the Paraguay fiasco and
the “ Socialism in our Time ” bogie ; then it raised
the Q.N.B. scare ; now it bases its claim to re-
election on the “ improved financial prospects and
improved outlook for the country.” Northern
ministers take as much credit for good seasons as
an African medicine-man.
