“The Forty Thieves” opened their box
of tricks at Her Majesty’s on Boxing Night to a
house which was crammed and something over.
The libretto of the pantomime is by Mr. Arthur
H. Adams, the Maoriland poet, and the work is
reasonably well done. Of course there is an
abundance of “gag” by the leading performers, for
which Mr. Adams is not responsible. The panto,
is, however, a great affair—from a scene-painter’s,
musician’s, costumier’s and limelight man’s point
of view—and is noteworthy for certain nega-
tive features. There are no ugly, awful, grue-
some mannikins with little bodies and Gargantuan
heads; there is no transformation scene with its
tinsel splendor and magnificent surprises of ex-
ploding water-lilies giving birth and expansive
Eink exposure to large stuffed fairies ; there is no
arleqmnade. There are, however, some weary,
weary things that are not good even of their kind.
There is a caricature of Hooley, for whom Sydney
cares not a problematical Federal capital. There is
much reference to De Roguemont—-and barely a
word concerning George Reid. (Let us say right
here, in parenthesis, that the comedy honors of the
pantomime belong to Ali Baba’s Ass, Edward, the
Ever Faithful, as quadrupeded by Mr. William Has-
san. Strange how the mention of a politician’s name
should suggest the other.) “ The Forty Thieves ”
is really the production of Messrs. Phil. W.
Goatcher and Geo. and J. Gordon, scenic-artists,
assisted by musicians, dress-makers, perruquiers,
dancing-masters, property-men, mechanists, and
calcium illuminators. The librettist and the mimes
for whose eccentricities he catered, are barely in
it. Geo. Gordon paints in hues caught from a
sunset fantasy such scenes as the Market-place in
Bagdad, Ali' Baba’s garden, and streets of the
f olden Orient. J. Gordon is happy in the interior
armony of the robbers’ cave and a street snatched
en passant from an imaginative glimpse into Bur-
ton’s-land. Phil. Goatcher scores pride of place
with his pictures of naval battles —the Defeat of
the Spanish Armada, the Capture of Manila, the
Destruction of the Spanish Fleet at Santiago—and
the fine tableaux of the Death of Nelson and the
Battle of Omdurman.
* * * •
The music is bright, catchy and effective.
The ballet of the Corn, the Poppies, and the Field
Mice is an idyll, the tiny grey rodent robbers of
the wheat-fields gamboling with quaint, nursery-
rhyme gravity about the living poppies and corn-
stalks of scarlet and gold. The Kaleidoscopic
March of ladies of noble proportions, clad in coats
of shimmering mail, with plumes and glittering
helms, and glistening gems, and hashing battle-
axes, is a sight for the imperial eye of Tamerlane
when he rode in triumph through Persepolis, or
something to make glad the soul of Kubla Khan
in Xanadhu. These scenic grandeurs are the
pantomime. It owes little to the performers.
Little Stella Tracy must be excepted, however.
Her imitation of Miss Peggy Pryde is a
fem. Little Gulliver, too, as Zig-zag, the
(lighted One, has a pair of very humorous legs.
Apart from Ali’s ass the funniest performer is
Cogia Baba John Coleman, the spouse of Ali Baba
Berkeley, who deserves passing mention. Princi-
pal Boy Pattie Browne, beautifully dressed,
shapely, piquinte, and all the charming rest of it,
is badly cast —what business has Pattie in panto-
mime, anyhow? Mr. George de Lara, with a
voice like Brough, enacts the part of the Hooli-
man with some measure of success. Miss Jennie
Opie is a vision of beauty and expansive magnifi-
cence as Abdallah, the Captain of the Forty
Thieves. She sings well, too; though vocally she is
not powerful enough to fill the big theatre. Mr. J.
W. Sweeney is worthy all sorts of commisera-
tion in the awful and thankless part of
Hassarac, the Lieutenant of a bad battalion of
puns. Miss Cesca Yollugi plays Haidee, Ganem
Pattie’s sweetheart, not too powerfully. Miss
Carrie Moore does excellent work as Morgiana.
She sings, she dances, she plays excellent comedy,
and she dresses herself with the exquisite taste of
the artiste. The bird imitations of Mr. Fred
French could easily be dispensed with. Spectacu-
larly, the present panto, is a glorious victory cal-
culated to give joy to all. The writer closes by
wishing Ali Baba’s donkey Edward a happy and
succulent New Year.
** * *
A valiant attempt at Bizet’s ever-rosy
“Carmen” was put on at Sydney Lyceum on
Christmas Eve, and didn’t fill the house with too
much audience or melody. Another week’s hard
rehearsal and an entirely new chorus, and some-
body very different from several people on the
stage, might have made the performance a success.
But as these alterations could not be effected after
the curtain rose on Saturday, this scribe came
away with the impression that he had not been
having a good time. That fascinating gypsy imp,
Carmen, received fairish treatment from Miss
Lilian Tree. This lady plays on much broader
lines than of yore, and has improved in the quality
of her acting and singing, but you are never de-
luded into. the belief that she is who she says
she is. This is partly due to the fact that she is
very large and fair and civilised, and partly to the
lengthy business she makes of getting off a table,
and partly to the absence of mercury in her fan-
dango and general movements. Farley, in his old
part of Toreador, knew his business, and gave you
a blissful few minutes of security, but persisted in
singing out of tune, especially in his warmly-
colored Toreador song. As for Leumane, as the
lover before the last, ne “ dried up ” so often that
it was scarcely possible to distinguish between the
actor and the prompter. Also, he seemed afraid
of Lilian Tree, and his clothes, and the audience,
and the sound of his own voice. The chorus
livened things up a little with a novel habit of
starting one after the other, and stopping con-
stantly to take a little air. And the orchestra,
under the gifted Allpress’s biiton, drove that long-
haired genius frenzied, and had to be beaten into
silence now and then. This was on Saturday, but
probably the opera is running more smoothly by
now, otherwise it could hardly be running at all.
** * *
The fearful and wonderful production en-
titled “ When London Sleeps, ” presented for the
first time in Sydney at the Royal last Saturday
night, by the company run by Mr. Charley Hollo-
way and Mr. William Anderson, is a lurid melo-
drama in four acts and 29 “ billed ” characters, to
say nothing of nondescript circus performers,
grooms, guests, waiters, crowds, and Hindoos.
As a guarantee of a highly sensational and
strongly-spiced article, the somnolent city has been
written up by a Mr. Charles Darrell—and the
Darrells have a monopoly of this class of work.
Also, Mr. C. R. Bailey is strongly to the fore as
manager, and one could forgive Mr. Bailey many
worse things, if only for the sake of his clever im-
personation of Citoyen Simon in Signora Maje-
roni’s production of “Marie Antoinette.” Mr.
Holloway is emphatically a coming manager.
Bland Holt will do well to look to his laurels, for
the shovv under notice is distinctly a “ Produc-
tion, ” with a cap. P. The scenery and costumes
and tableaux are splendidly picturesque, sumptu-
ous, and artistic; the ballets are quaint and ex-
ceptionally well executed ; and the chant and
adoration of Somnath, with its bacchante finale in
a fluctuant en wreathing of white-robed supple forms
is weirdly suggestive of the mysterious hidden
faiths of India. The play itself is unconvincing,
and not too ably constructed. Act succeeds act
with no very necessary continuity of plot, and one
grows weary of the absurd persecution of the
heroine by the tom-fool villain, who bangs a din-
ner-gong on the coping-stone of the General
Post Office, and puts an advertisement in the
morning papers whenever he meditates a new
brand of murder.
• * • *
The hero of the sleeping city is Mr. Robt.
Inman, who, as David Engleheart, a circus
Sampson, earns his living with his little sling, and
falls in love, in his intervals of “putting up”
5601 b., with Queenie Carruthers, “the white
queen of the slack wire. ” For a mere circus rider
David is a perfect Crichton, talks like an encyclo-
paedia, and behaves like a “blooming toff.”
Queenie Carruthers is a foundling, who is after-
wards found to be an heiress, with a male cousin
attached, the villain aforesaid, one Captain Rod-
ney Haynes. His wickedness is held aloft to
public scorn by Mr. Charley Holloway. Hence-
forth the play works out through a maze of
machinations on the part of Captain Rodney
Haynes to secure possession of the fortune of Miss
Eugenie Duggan (otherwise Queenie Carruthers)
even by murder, or worse—marriage. He is, how-
ever, continually thwarted by Sammy Blyth, as
Mr. William Gourlay is called for the nonce, a
circus clown with a gag (verbal) and a brace
of Colt’s revolvers. Mr. Gourlay is the
keystone of the piece. There is also an
intolerable policeman, who pockets tips and
bilks his tipsters. He is filled with Pod-
snapian remarks about the Queen and all the
Royal Family, and the extent of the British
Empire, and the morality of the constabulary.
The “ second villain ” and aid to the schemes of
the ■wicked “ Kapting” is a clever study in browu
umber and Indian properties, and the “chappie ”
and the circus boss are also worthy of mention.
Miss Eugenie Duggan is very charming as Queenie
Carruthers, with a lot of reserved power in her
acting and a number of delightful costumes in
white. Miss Ada Gresham enacted the part of a
lady with a past, and counterfeited so well that
she deceived even the stage policeman. Miss
Georgie Leighford was an admirable Signorina
Roberti Jonesio ; and little Lelia, as interpreted
by Pearl Hellmrieh, was a charming bit of child
acting. There were as many characters as ships in
the list of Homer, and some can be mentioned at
intervals during the coming year.
** # *
The Jubilee Singers have lifted up their
Elaintive melody to good cash purpose through the
oliday season. This writer never fully appre-
ciated the restfulness there is in the half-mournful
cadence of the Jubilee melody till he came on it
in that holiday week, amid the yell of the stuffed
duck, and the trampling of the mince pie, and the
rush and racket, and the falling of an intoxicated
Santa Claus against the back-door, and his un-
Kblishable expressions as he tried to get up again.
e Jubilee Singers were a streak of holy calm in
that time of unrest, and to hear them was like
getting away from a mad dog and taking refuge
in an old Gothic cathedral combined with refresh-
ments. Just for the Xmas week this scribe would
have felt better if the juggler and the other worldly
elements had been left out, though, but even with
them in he was well pleased. The evening he
heard the dark Jubilators was the only time he
felt really well towards his fellow-creatures all
through the glad season, when man is full of good-
will towards his fellow-man and helps him home
at night.
** * *
There is nothing to be reported from Syd-
ney Criterion save that “ Robbery Under Arms”
still holds out in many acts beneath its native gum-
tree. At time of writing it was in its sixth week,
and the hairy bushmen were going to see it in con-
siderable numbers, and coming away in great sad-
ness as they considered how very little there is in
the backblocks now to be robbed—under arms o r
otherwise.

Peggy Pry de and the Boneless One at
Sydney Tivoli have been reinforced this week by
sundry new arrivals. Fred. Poplar and Miss
Kitty Wager are the most notable of these, and
The Bulletin will probably say something nice
about them next week when its head has got down
again to its normal dimensions.
* * *
Sydney Alhambra has dropped into comedy
this week, “ Mixed, ” that fine old evergreen joke,
having been staged with Frank Hawthorne in the
part of Bosco Blithers. Hawthorne is a promising
young actor, and in the present lamentable dearth
of comedy there is abundant room for a show of
the kind. The Alhambra is a rather remote spot
for a comedy theatre, for the very best sassiety
doesn’t usually stream down Brickfield Hill ; still
the venture has done very well through the Xmas
season.
** * *
If the Brough production of “Madame
Sans-Gene ” is to get the success it merits, busi-
ness at Melbourne Bijou will presently be boom-
some. All who have read the pretty story of
soldier Lefebre and his irrepressible wife are bound
to take kindly to the stage version of it. Those
who know nothing of the book will find the play
bright and stirring in its sketches and suggestions
of Buonaparte court life. This is a much more
convincing work than the “ Royal Divorce, ” for
it deals with the homely side of imperial greatness
and rubs the gilt off the Napoleon dynasty’s ginger-
bread. Madame Sans-Gene, a laundress in the
prologue and a duchess afterwards, is the embodied
spirit of the French revolutionary times. The
emperor of to-day is the corporal of yesterday,
whose shirts she had darned and washed gratis,
and when his sisters refer to the duchess as a
former purifier of chemises she reminds them that
she did not put their underlinen into her tub,
because in those days they had none to send to the
wash. Sans-Gene is a woman who holds fast to
the naked truth in all matters. Her husband re-
proves her for trying on a new gown in the recep-
tion saloon, and showing her bare neck and arms
to the costumier, but she dismisses his protest
with the remark that she will be less decently
covered up when she is full dressed. Mrs. Brough
is unable to play the name part for all it is worth,
seeing that her voice and style are not typical of
the vulgar, warm-hearted matron. Ellen Terry
was open to the same objection, yet she made the
character effective, and Mrs. Brough does likewise.
For the rest, Brough, as Napoleon, is not so stagey
as Julius Knight, but the make-up of his face and
shoulders is rather disappointing. From the waist
downwards Brough seems very true to history, and
some of his comedy-acting is as humorous as his
legs. Joseph Carne gives a good, manly perform-
ance of Lefebre, and -Rotherham is a picturesque
Fouche, Minister of Police. A number of minor
parts are neatly filled, and the show as a whole is
an imposing entertainment.
** * *
“New Babylon,” at Melbourne Royal, is
not the drama where Bland Holt gets hanged
without the option of a fine, so The Bulletin’s
preliminary misstatement to that effect is hereby
cancelled. Bland Holt wears the blameless frock-
coat of a Yankee detective, and he tracks the
villain through a maze of exciting tableaux,
beginning with a shipwreck and ending with
revels in a thieves’ kitchen. The old reliable plot
of “ New Babylon ” is somewhat complicated by
the principal lady appearing in two different capa-
cities. Sometimes Miss Watson is an innocent
victim in a suburban cottage, and at other times
she is an abandoned female, who persuades a
jockey to pull his little tin gee-gee for the
Goodwood Stakes, wherefore Walter Baker wallops
the jockey and wins the hand and fortune of
Miss Ross on the understanding that he will retire
from the turf. There is sure to be a moment in
the dramatic career of Miss Ross when she ex-
presses her love for Baker before all the world, no
matter what drama is on the bill. Also, the un-
happy girl from the cottage is able to get married
again because her first husband committed bigamy,
and her wicked duplicate girl turns penitent over
the corpse of the light-weight who went astray.
Furthermore, Mrs. Bland Holt secures the affec-
tions of a cheerful idiot named Jeremiah, and
Detective Bland is understood to have a wife
saying up for him somewhere. The villain, together
with nis various-accomplices, are arrested for steal-
ing documents and things, but probably they are
better off in prison than if they had stayed outside
to meet their dooms at Trinity Church. The
spectacular greatness of “New Babylon ” is quite
appalling, and the efforts of the company can be
unreservedly praised. It is a very numerous co.,
but in consequence of this week being Xmas the
writer has enough peace and goodwill to meet the
emergency.
** * *
The sudden attack of pantomime at Mel-
bourne Alexandra is not quite a hopeless case.
By the time Melbourne in general becomes aware
of this unexpected event “Jack and the Bean-
stalk” will probably have put some flesh on
its second act, which was distressingly thin
and feeble last Monday night. The first
act had something to do, and did it in an
old-fashioned manner with the assistance of a pan-
tomime cow, but the cow retired from business
during the interval, and the after part had no
strong point except a respectable transformation-
scene. If the cow can get its sphere of action en-
larged by the stage-manager, the pantomime may
come to be graciously pardoned for the sake of the
children. Misses Ray Jones, Minna Phillips,
and Blanche Lewis, also George Leopold and D.
H. Caston, are the people chiefly concerned in the
outrage. Caston is the comedian who sings about
bis little back room in Bloomsbury, and he will
probably give valuable assistance when he has
learned his part. It takes the average comic
vocalist a week or more to learn a few lines of
doggrel.
** m *
“ The Geisha ” made a good start at Melb.
Princess’s, and would probably have travelled
faster and have scored higher notes of admiration
out of doors but for some excessive applause in
certain quarters on the first night, and the mis-
taken policy of forcing “encores” afterwards.
-Daily newspaper notices are to blame for this
managerial error. They mention the “enthu-
siasm ” of an audience in a cross-heading, and
seem anxious to boom the efforts of those tiresome
individuals who make a point of barracking for
every new show on its opening night. This hrs
e < r|f ec t ; °f getting the majority’s back up, and
the Firm suffers thereby. In the case of “The
Geisha” the indiscriminate encoring of the
first night couldn’t possibly be maintained by
future audiences. So the conductor last week was
giving them an encore on the slightest provocation.
The same rule is followed in a Rickards “ first
part,” but whereas the warbler at Rickards's
merely arises from his or her chair and repeats the
tail-end of a refrain, the comic-opera performer is
brought back to the footlights to sing the whole of
a long verse over again. “Geisha” verses are
very long, as a rule, and they don’t improve on a
second hearing. Besides, the opera in its natural
state is somewhat overweighted with melodies,
and too muchee encore tends to make it drag.
Miss Florence Poole’s solo numbers are especially
unsuitable for repetition on account of their foolish
lines. The really very popular numbers are the
Nursery Days duet in the first act, and Lauri’s
“ Ching-chong-Chinaman ” towards the end of the
opera. There are several other fairly joyful
ditties, but it is not until Lauri produces his song
and dance that the great difference between an
encore complimentary and an encore unanimous is
forced upon public notice. The glitter of “ The
Geisha” doesn’t fade on further acquaintance, and
the show looks quite enough like a pantomime to
be a howling success during Xmas week.
** m *
Fitzgeralds’ Circus surprises Melbourne, in
the first place, with the vast dimensions of its
tents, for the Fitz. Brethren had not previously
set up their biggest canvas in Australia’s horsiest
city. The much-postered spectacle of the elephant
and lion on a trike doesn’t come as an astonishing
surprise after one has seen them doing the same
thing all day long on the hoardings, but this is
the first time it has happened in Melbourne, and
it will serve to fill the tent eight times a week
until further notice. The circus which contrives
to be different from every other circus in its prin-
cipal feature is accepted with thanks by this
comparatively un-horsey journal.
At Poverty Point.
“ M‘G.” : The allegatiorf of plagiarism against Marshall
Hall has fizzled out very miserably. The common, ordin-
ary man in the street, who is unable to perceive any tune
in either Hall’s or Brahm’s passages, thinks the darned
thing isn’t worth stealing, anyhow, and the incident
seems less interesting than the arrest of a vag. for rob-
bing another of a dead dog.
Constance Clyde : There is a time to throw bouquets
and a time not. The time not is when the heroine and
the villain are despising each other to that degree that he
can’t with any consistency stop in the middle of it and offer
her the donkey-feed. Consequently, at the Lyceum, the
other night, the bunch of pink and white lay in all its in-
appropriateness for a full 2(1 minutes beside the triangles
whereto the obviously female Kirkland is tied up for that
good old stage-flogging that never quite comes off—till in
pity Parson North bore it off the stage and a ghostly
hand was seen receiving it at the wings.
“Jinda”: Visited a Sydney side-show t’other night.
One act was Rontgen rays applied to a Cinematographe
love-scene. Directly in front of writer sat a girl and her
beau. During first part of performance the swain’s arm
encircled his charmer. Her back hair rested lovingly on
his shoulder. Every lovey-dovey action of the phantom
pair on the sheet was copied. Suddenly the skeleton
part of the show' started. The amorous animated bones
continued hugging, and two ghastly skulls met in a loving
kiss. Ugh ! The flesh and blood lover gave one horrified
glance at the sheet, withdrew his arm—ostensibly to rub
his ear—and, turning around, whispered to me: “ It’s
red’ot, ain’t it, mate?” The girl was unaffected. She
simply nestled up closer, and cast a reproachful glance at
her rapidly cooling lover. He had one more look at the
antics of the shadowy sets of bones, then reached for his
hat, and muttering something about “ missing the ’bus,’
dragged the girl out of the show.
“ H.” : I notice it in print that a daughter of Mr. J. F.
Cathcart was lately married in England.
“ Paradis" : Alack ! A recent A (tans leader referred to
dear old Billy as David Hoskins. Such
“ Professor" Kennedy, the awful mesmerist who quitted
Australia in an unexpected way some few months ago,
and left many hired candle-eaters and kerosene-drinkers
in grief, is reported dead at Chicago. Bright’s disease
was the reason of his decease, and his tombstone alleges
that he was 45 years of age.
_ “ Melb.”: Fitzgeralds’ pitch is virgin, as regards a
circus—Flinders-street, next Australian Church. Credit-
able to the military eye of agent Morris, who did not
care to follow Probasco, on the Hippodrome enclosure—
site of Coppin’s Iron Pot, the Olympic, and of Row'e’s
antique circus—nearly opposite the Alexandra. Fitz-
geralds’ circus is just opposite where Wilson’s was pitched,
many years ago—where the railway-yard is now. It has
always been hard to locate a circus in Melb. G. B. W.
Lewis had to build Astley’s, afterwards the Princess’s.
Noble’s wa3 on the north-east corner of Bourke and
Spring streets. Chiarini pitched in Lonsdale-street east,
back of where Coppin’s Our Lodgings now is. Cooper
and Bailey, Cole, and others, took a site between Melb.
and South Melb. Fillis was on St. Kilda-road. Fitz-
geralds' circus is close to the site of G. V. Brooke’s
residence—a brick villa which stood amidst a nice garden
in Flinders-lane, next the superb mansion built by
lawyer Duerdin, with his historical cellar full of law-
books.

COLUMN
The National Mutual Life Association presents
its second balance-sheet since the amalgamation with the
Mutual of Victoria. It is now the third largest insurance
concern in Australasia, the £900,000 which the Mutual of
Victoria threw into the partnership having lifted it into
that desirable position. Its premium-income for the year
ended 30th September, 1898, was £347,417 ; interest on
investments, £132,248; total income, £479,665. The
amount added to the assurance-fund was £152,821, malting
the total t‘2,765,571. At this rate, the National Mutual
should close the century with a pile of over three millions.
The year’s expenses were 186,398. It is difficult to make
any comparison with previous years, for last balance-
sheet contained the accounts of the National Mutual for one
year and those of the Mutual of Victoria for nine months ;
and previous ones showed the two institutions separately.
But the expenses are equal to £24 19s. per cent, of
premium, or barely 18 per cent, of total income, which is
a trifle better than last year. Also the investments yield
£4 18s. 2d. per cent, per annum interest —a very good
return as present interest rates go.
The assets look very good on the whole.
Certainly the £48,480 described as “ deferred instal-
ments of annual premiums ” is a rather shadowy
security, and one which very few societies reckon
nowadays. It is arrived at by assuming that the
policy-holder ought to pay his premium annually in
advance, though he is allowed to pay it half-yearly
or quarterly. And if he pays it quarterly, start-
ing from, say, 2nd July, the society, when it
closes its accounts on 30th September, puts down
the other nine months’ premiums as an asset. Also,
if the policy starts from 2nd July and the insured pays
his premium quarterly, and dies on 30th September, the
society deducts the nine months’ premium from the sum
due to the widow, which is probably a surprise to the
widow in some cases. Apart from this, however, the
assets show no cause for serious objection. The expense
rate is still somewhat high, but the National Mutual looks
very sound and is moving ahead steadily. Still it ought
to make a better showing as compared with the Maoriland
Government Department, if private management is all
that it claims to be. The two offices are practically the
same size and the same age, the private society having a
year’s start. And at the end of 1897 the Government
Department had rather the larger fund ; it spent £4 less
in expenses out of every £lOO received ; it based its esti-
mates of future interest on investments at £3 10s. against
the private society’s £4, thus leaving a much larger mar-
gin of security in case of a fall in interest rates ; it charged
much lower premiums, and paid practically the same
bonuses.
* * * •
The National Bank of Tasmania—one of the
youngest of the Australasian banks—steadily improves
its position. For the half-year ended 30th November,
1898, the net profits were £4516, which compares with
some previous profits as follows :
November, 1892 £5,382 May, 1896 .. £4,299
May, 1893 .. 4,776 November, 1896 3,534
November, 1893 4,701 May, 1897 .. 4,012
May, 1894 .. 4,618 November, 1897 4,127
November, 1894 4,695 May, 1898 .. 4,129
May, 1895 .. 3,989 November, 1898 4,516
November, 1895 4,136
The usual dividend at 5 per cent, per annum (£3801) and
dividend-tax (> 190) absorb £3991, and leave £525 to the
good. There is still a lot to do before the National repairs
its position. It wrote £15,000 off its reserve in 1893, re-
ducing it to 122,500, and since then it has done nothing
to replace the loss. It paid 8 per cent, dividends from
May, ’BB, to May, ’92; 7 per cent, in November, ’92,
and 6 per cent, for two years after that, and this craving
for bigger dividends than the bad times warranted made
it impossible to do anything to make good the vanished
£15,000. And a reserve of £22,500 is a very small
affair for any bank which hopes to own the earth
some day. Still, the profits are increasing, and if the
National has sufficient grit to keep its dividend down to 5
per cent, for a few years more, it should become a strong
and profitable institution again in course of time. It has
a small but steadily-growing business, and a respectable
cash-reserve equal to over 22 per cent, of all liabilities to
the public, and there is a balance of £2395 at credit of
profit and loss —£2000 of which should be immediately
put to reserve lest the bank should feel tempted to go on
the burst with it next half-year, and have another 7 per
shent dividend.

Dalgety and Co., one of the very few big pas-
toral companies which have kept their heads above water,
reports a profit for the year ended 30th June, 1898, of
£83,571. Profits for seven years past have been
1892 .. .. £80,979 I 1896 .. .. £83,672
1893 .. . 79,553 | 1897 .. .. 83,002
1894 .. .. 80,913 I 1898 .. .. 83,571
1595 .. .. 83,896 |
The co. has paid an 8 per cent, dividend (£80,000) each
year, so on paper it has improved its position by £15,586
in seven years. But the deadly accuracy with which it
earns just about its 8 per cent, dividend suggests that
these are not the real profits, only the amount that it
seems desirable to exhibit to the public, while the balance
goes towards that old, old spectre—depreciation, or
to the enlargement of the inner reserves. If this con-
jecture is nowhere near the truth, Tiib Bulletin will
be glad to publish the usual contradiction. Dalgety
and Co., though it is far more fortunate than most of its
kind, is, in one respect, very like the rest. It trades on
£1,150,439 of its own funds (paid-up capital, £1,000,000;
reserve, £130,000; profit balance, £20,439), and on
£2,604,960 of debenture money and about £1,000,000 of
Other borrowings or amounts owing. All the large
pastoral concerns in Australasia seem top-heavy in this
game respect. And though the reserve looks large a
depreciation of only 3 per cent, in the assets would
swallow it all.

The once great St. JamesßuildingSociety (Melb.)
fcts along with its lamentable winding-up process. Its lia-

The National Trustees, Executors and Agency
Co. (Melbourne) follows its old policy of damning the con-
sequences so long as good dividends are forthcoming. The
net profit for the year ended 31st October, 1898, after
allowing a humble £l6 for depreciation of office furniture,
is £2390, and an 8 per cent, dividend absorbs £2400,
leaving the co. £lO to the bad. The doings of this co. for
the last seven vears have been as follows :
Net Profit. Dividend paid. Surplus
1892 ..£4706 15per cent. .. £4500 .. £6
Deficit
1893 .. 4442 15 „ .. 4500 .. 58
1894 .. 2813 10 ~ ... 3000 .. 187
Surplus
1595 .. SOOl 10 „ „ 3000 .. 1

1896 2986 10 „ .. 3000 .. 14
1597 .. 2353 8 „ .. 2400 .. 12
1593 .. 2390 8 „ ... 2400 .. 10
£22,526 £22,800
Therefore in five years out of the last seven the National
Trustees has paid away more than its earnings, and the
management of an institution which would trench on its
reserves to pay a 15, or even 10 or 8, per cent, dividend
is a woeful example of blind greed and incapacity.
During all this period practically no provision has been
made for possible depreciation of assets other than office
furniture, despite the extreme badness of the times. The
reserve has stood at the same figure (£23,400) for a long
time. Whether it is intact, or whether part, of it has
been absorbed by depreciation of assets it is difficult to
say, for the balance-sheet lumps the profits of the trustee,
business, and the receipts from interest on investments
together, and it is impossible to say how the latter are
panning out. Also, it is impossible to say what kind of
profit the trustee business yields. In any case the reserve
isn’t large, and a more prudent management would have
abstained from 15 per cent, dividends till it was a good
deal stronger. The National certainly has one of the
best trustee businesses in Australasia, but in these times
of slump, and at all other times, any concern which gets
into the habit of declaring big dividends that are not fully
earned is to be viewed with some suspicion.

Tho Derwent and Tamar Fire and Marine In-
surance 00. (Hobart, Tasmania)—the oldest concern of
its kind in the seven provinces—finished a disastrous
year on 31st October with a net loss of £10,780. This
includes£soowritten off securities on re-valuation. A divi-
dend ofsper cent. (£2500), and dividend-tax thereon (£125)
absorb another £2625, so the Derwent and Tamar ends
the year £13,405 to the bad. Until quite recently, this
co. pursued the curious old Tasmanian custom of keeping
its accounts strictly dark, but for the last three years its
results have been as follows :
Profit. Dividend and Div.-tax.
31st October, 1896 ..£8,175 .. 15 per cent. = £7,875
„ 1897 .. 8,481 .. 7i „ = 3,937
Loss.
„ 1898 .. 10,780 .. 5 „ = 2,625

This means that things are £8561 worse than they were
three years ago. Still, the institution paidall its heavy losses
(arising mainly out of the great Melbourne fire of a year
back) without trenching on its reserve ; it always kept a
large floating balance at credit of profit and loss, and it
has still £5799 at the credit of this account. The share-
holders’ funds even now consist of £105,799 (paid-up
capital, £50,000 ; reserve, £50,000 ; profit balance, £5799),
so it is a very strong affair with immense resources in
proportion to the size of its business, and a good record of
60 years’ standing. Also, its investments are solid, and
no breath of suspicion has blown on the character of the
Derwent and Tamar within the memory of anybody this
paper has ever heard of —not even the dreadful oldest
inhabitant. As a rule, it is alarmingly bad policy to pay
l dividend in a losing year, but the D. and T. manage-
ment is so steady-going that there is hardly any doubt it
will see the breach repaired before it resumes the 15 per
cent, dividends of former years.

The Executor, Trustee and Agency Co. of
South Australia, has a year’s profit of £l3lO. A 5 per
cent, dividend on the little capital of less than £ll,OOO
absorbs £536 ; £5OO is added to reserve, making it £lOOO ;
and the remainder (with previous balance of £201) leaves
£475 at credit of profit and loss. This concern started in
1880, so it took 17 long years to build up a reserve of £5OO
and a profit balance of £2Ol. In fact, it seems to have
done more to build up its reserves last year than in all the
previous 17.

A few days ago the S.M. Herald did its usual
shriek about the evils of State Life Insurance as
compared with the privately-managed article. The fol-
lowing is the shriek:—“A short time ago, when
correspondents in New Zealand sought to show that
a State Life Insurance Department could be conducted
more economically than a private life office as an argu-
ment for the extension of Government undertakings to
fire insurance department, we showed how different a
conclusion was borne out by a comparison of the results
of the New Zealand Government Life Office with those of
the Australian Mutual Provident Society, the only private
life office whose business could be fairly compared. Com-
parisons warranted the belief that the members of the
private office obtained better results in the shape of
bonuses than were received by the insurants in the Go-
vernment Life Department. Mr. Robert M'Connel, a
solicitor, of Christchurch, New Zealand, gives a forcible
illustration of this. He had policies in both insti-
tutions, running for 27 years. The bonuses on £3OO poli-
cies after 27 years amounted to £lO7 in the Government
Department, and £271 in the Australian Mutual Office.
But the premium was only £5 18s. in the former, as
against £7 11s. 9d. in the latter. Supposing the same
premium were paid to the Government as to the A.M.P.
Society, his life would be insured for £386, and he arrived
at the conclusion that his policy and bonuses would be
worth £555, against £630 in the A.M.P. Office—a differ-
ence of £75 in favor of the latter. These considerations
show that the people of New South Wales have nothing to
gain in life insurance from the establishment here of a
branch of the New Zealand Government Office, as has
been suggested.”
The Herald’s clumsily-concocted case against
State management grows rather wearisome, and it be-
comes clumsier than ever as it goes on. In the first place,
it always starts its comparison between State and private
enterprise by drawing a parallel between the Maoriland
State Department (28 years old) and the A.M.P. (50 years
old and five times larger). A large old office always
works, or ought to work, at a cheaper rate than a small
new one. Any comparison of the M.L. State office with
private institutions of its own age is carefully avoided,
for it is miles ahead of them all in cheapness of manage-
ment and in every other way. The A.M.P.’s own ex-
penses were once—only once—in its very young days,
over 48 per cent, of its premium income. And in regard
to the case quoted, it seems that if Robert M'Oonnel,
solicitor, had insured his life in both offices, and paid
£75 17s. 6d. premium in each case, and then been fatally
dropped on by a brick the same afternoon, the Maoriland
State Department would have paid his widow £3S6O,
while the A.M.P. would only have paid her £3OOO.
Most people insure to get the biggest immediate pio-
vision thpy can for their dependents, not for t e
sake of the big bonuses that may accrue in the re-
mote future, and which have to be paid for bv less
protection in the immediate present. Supposing lionett
M'Connel had paid the same premium to both offices,
then for over 22 years the Maoriland State Department
would have kept him insured for a larger sum than the
A.M.P., but in about 23 years the A.M.P’s bigger bonuses
would begin to place it in front. In the case of the man
who doesn’t live 22 years, and he is reasonably numerous,
the advantages are with the institution which gives the
biggest amount of immediate insurance for the money.
Dear Bulletin.— Nothing irritates the N.S.W.
wheat-grower more than the oft-repeated, yet never
realised, statement by Sydney scribes that “ we are going
to be a wheat-exporting province this year ! ” One would
think that there was a fortune for the country in wheat
exportation, judging' by the elation of S3'dney journalists.
I would ask them to reflect on the fact that to grow wheat
in N.S.W. for export is generally to grow it at a loss. Ex-
port price on the farm means about is. 9d. under London
prices, which Is. 9d. is about 45 per cent, of the whole on
the present prices. The only result of all this talk about
a wheat surplus is to reduce prices for the benefit of the
Millers’ Association. The estimates of the N.S.W. wheat-
crop are all wrong, and, in place of exporting wheat, the
millers will have to import some. The Sydney dailies which
issue estimates of our wheat-crops have brought forth an
exportable surplus by alleging that we are to have an
average of 10 bushels to the acre, and this in the face of
the fact that west of the dividing range we are suffering
from an awful drought, and that it is west of the dividing
range most of the N.S.W. wheat is grown. Melb. Argus
estimates the Victorian crop at only seven bushels to
the acre notwithstanding the fact that Victoria is enjoying
a very much better season than we are ; and the Argus
forecast has been undeniably accurate for the last four
years. Take the district along the Murray served by the
Oorowa and Albury train-service. The Telegraph esti-
mates that district to yield 11 bushels to t.he acre (over
2,000,000 bushels or about one-fifth of the whole produc-
tion of N.S.W.). I live in the centre of that district and I
estimate it at about five bushels to the acre. If the Syd-
ney estimates are correct one ought to be able to buy
150,000 bags of wheat in Corowa during the first ten
weeks of the new year. I predict that it will be diffi-
cult to buy 50,000 bags. I would ask all the men who
have wheat to hold it tenaciously and not take export
price.— Wheat-Grower (Corowa, N.S.W.).

v Chairman Howe, of the B.H. Junction, owned
up, at a recent, shareholders’ meeting, that 55,000 shares
were now held abroad as against 45,000 on the Australian
registers ; but the proposed transfer of office to the other
side could be blocked so long as a fourth of the shares
were held here. Further, there had been talk of amal-
gamation with the Junction North, and an English co.
was prepared to buy the mine straight out ; but nothing
would be done until the shareholders had been consulted.
The English co. is no doubt the Camden Syndicate, of
which the Ulawarra (Australia) Smelting Co. is an off-
shoot, and which is the holder of most of the 55,000
shares mentioned. The other affair —the proposed amal-
gamation—probably explains the recent loud booming of
the Junction North; though certainly that in-and-out
show has improved again.

When Somerset was sent up the other day in
Brisbane on a charge of embezzling certain Imperial funds
to the tune of £7OOO, it transpired that the cash in his
charge had not been audited for 20 years.
Mt. Morgan half-yearly report is interesting
reading. Firstly, no accidents at the big mine for 12
months. Gold per ton has declined from loz. 2dwt. 7grs.
for vear ending May 31st to 15£dwts. for half-year just
ended. Reason—increase in amount of low-grade ore
treated. In the corresponding period of ’97 the quantity
of low-grade ore treated was 28,387 tons for an average of
7jdwt. Last six months gave 69,081 tons (low-grade) for
7|dwt. to the ton. Increase in low-grade ores, therefore,
40,694 tons, or about 80,000 tons for the 12 months. The
total quantity of ore treated was 107,860 tons ; total
yield, 84,0870 z., ora thousand ozs. less than the last half
of ’97. Forty-four p.c. of all gold obtained was got from
high-grade mundic ore ; about 30 p.c. from low-grade
oxidised stone ; the balance from medium-grade oxidised
ore and slimes. Changes are imminent. The upper
works will have to be adapted to the treatment of sulphide
ores. Dividends for the last six months gave £175,000.
To reserves was carried a sum of £30,000. M ages for
period, £72,931.

Prospects for next year very bright.

More about the Golden Zone, the boss news-
paper mine of Kalgoorlie—this time that the cross-cut is
12ft. in the lode, and a big improvement is expected
when the footwall is reached, as that was where rich ore
was got in the upper levels. From which it may be taken
for granted that the 12ft. mentioned is mighty poor, or
we should have heard all about it. The public are about
as tired of the Golden Z. as the shareholders are of paying
calls, and, on their behalf and its own, Tub Bulletin prays
for a rest.

Westralian mines The Adelaide market in
these has probably seen its worst, and is winding up a
year of shocks with a fair tone, though there isn’t much
trade. London seems inclined to move again. Adelaide
and the W.A. market generally had a sharp shock last
month and at the end of Oct., but all the heavy clearing-
up has been done, and there is no forced selling. In
fact, there is no big supply of scrip. London is
mopping up quietly a lot of Associateds. Said to
be a move on to change the directorate. It may
be Kaufman and his Horseshoe crowd at the bottom
of it. Great Boulder development at 600 ft. perfectly
satisfactory. Australia East lode, at 200 ft., said to be
going into the Oroya southern boundary—if so, will make
a bis; improvement in that mine—and it can stand it.

So much winze-sinking is done for the deep
levels of Bendigo mines now that it has been found most
convenient in some cases to fix up small poppet-legs un-
derground—at (say) 2800 ft. down.
New Chum Railway (Bendigo), which is chip-
ping away below 3000 ft., has paid £78,391 in divs. from
workings’ beneath the 2300 ft. level. And there are several
more little ones in sight.

About the richest of the little revivals at Castle-
maine (Vic.) is the Central Lady Brassey, Sandy Creek.
The co. was formed in Sept., ’97 ; 30,000 shares, 3d. paid
on each. With that capital—there was £375 available-
gold was got, and a first div. paid in Dec., ’97. Since then
15 others have been paid ; total £ls,ooo—twelve months
interest on £375 ; for the reserve-fund more than cover#
the original outlay.
The splendid show which Bendigo makes for '9B
in any comparison of divs. with calls steadily turns more
of the syndicates’ eyes that way. The latest of the old
runs of country to be resurrected is the Shellback —a reef
running parallel with the Princess Dagmar, Golden Age,
and Constellation. The ancient Shellback co. paid a good
many divs., but is now quite forgotten.
Another of the apparently interminable cases
turning on the ownership of abandoned heaps of tailings
has just been decided in Melbourne by Judge Holroya.
The tailings in this case had been originally taken from
heaps in the neighborhood and passed through a battery
in slack seasons. They then meandered over the ground
on which the battery was, until 100,000 tons accumu-
lated. The owners of this ground became insolvent, and
the forfeited lease was issued to the defendants in this
action. Tailings are a great deal more valuable now than
they used to be, and the new lessees proceeded to treat
them for their own profit. The assignee of the old, insol-
vent lessees sued them for wrongful conversion, and Judge
Holroyd, following the trend of previous Vic. decisions,
held that tailings are chattels, that they don’t pass with
the lease, and that they still belong to the previous
lessee. This may be law, but it lookslike an arrangement
which will make heaps of trouble through its want of
common sense. The previous lessee, according to law,
has a better claim than the present one. But that pre-
vious lessee had gathered a lot of the tailings for battery
treatment from various other people, and his claim is
apparently only good till these other people or their heirs
turn up and identify their separate tailings from the heap.
There seems to be no finality about tailings, in fact. And,
assuming that they were the previous lessee’s tailings, he
should be liable "for trespass or for storage charges,
because he left them on the present lessee’s ground.
Where, on Judge ilolroyd's system, is the tailings ques-
tion to end ?
Williams’s mine, Bendigo, largely owned by
the Vic. Railway Minister of that name, has decided to
approach York and Durham with a view to amalgamating
both companies. A wise thing that ought to have been
done many years back, and might well be done by others
on the same line. Small areas may have worked for good
in the old days, hut nine cases in ten the folly of mining
below 2000ft.’on little claims has been very dearly paid
for. It is amalgamation or liquidation with many claim*
on Bendigo. The Williams has paid no div. for 15 years.
York and Durham, so far as this writer knows, never
paid any at all.
Timber, for fuel especially, has become a serious
item of mining expenditure on the principal Vic. fields,
and it is only a matter of time when it will be cheaper to
use coal. Recently the conservator of forests intimated
to the timber-cutters of Maryborough that they must go
further afield for supplies, and this will mean the closing
down of some of the poorer mines.

Vic. that the Mines Minister says the matter want*

time he has no desire to block the operations of capital*
ists. How very like the average Mines Minister ? Every
old digger and every dredging lease to be dealt with on
his and its merits. Why certainly.

“Constance Clyde”: “Closed-Up Shop-
keeper ’’ wails in a Sydney paper over the threat-
ened early-closing movement. Morning trade, he
says, isn’t half so good as evening, and when there
is no longer any evening biz a lot of shops will
have to close and a number of shopkeepers go to
doss in the Domain. Of course, so pig-headed is
humanity in genera], that, if forbidden to shop
after six p.m., it would go about naked and
starving rather than buy anything before six. Is
it not well-known, for instance, that in Maoriland,
where compulsory early closing is the rule, the
streets are crowded with affluent people going
about in the altogether because they aren’t
allowed to buy clothes late at night.
* * * *
The huge Tyson estate was soon pounced
upon by the hungry N.S.W. bar. At its debut on
Friday, Dec. 16, Salomons, Q.C., Barton, Q.C.,
Pilcher, Q.C. and Want, Q.C. represented the
senior bar, George Reid, Q.C., not getting a place.
Although about a dozen of the contending parties
were not represented by counsel, the court could
barely contain all the barristers appearing in the
case. The solicitors employed are also a goodly
array. And yet it was only a small formal matter
which one cheap junior could have attended to.

Dear Bulletin. —Domestic points noted :
Directlya woman marries she sheds her enthusiasm
for female suffrage. The married woman has real
eloquence when she confidentially gives vent to
her views on her mother-in-law. How such a
creature ever gave birth to a baby destined to
become her darling husband passes her compre-
hension. She would spit in her face every time
she saw her were it not rude and against her
better breeding as a lady. And the woman with
a daughter-in-iaw would rather eee chaos come to
earth than that“ that hussy” should be entrusted
with the franchise, in addition to the dragging-
down of her son. A wife never forgives her
husband for having a mother at meeting distance,
and a, mother never forgives a daughter-in-law at
any distance. On the other hand, the mother
loves her son-in-law more dearly than her daughter,
while the husband generally regards his mother-
in-law as a rather agreeable old lady, who clearly
understands that he is lord and master, and
thereby shows her good sense. It requires a
violent provocation to set him at her.—o.
« * * ,
The great precedence question has broken
out in a fresh place in Melbourne. Admiral Pear-
son started it ; and he was so boiling mad over
some person’s alleged conduct in standing on his
corn that Lord Dicky Nevill supplied the papers
with the Government House table of precedence.
Then the Legislative Council got on its hind legs.
The said table places the bishops and the arch-
bishops before the President and the Speaker.
Ihe lat Men, being mostly Presbyterians, object
fiercely, and President Zeal will boycott Govern-
ment House until the affair is settled in his favor.
Strictly speaking, there is no bishop and no arch-
bishop of Melbourne, though two men call them-
selves such.. Whenever a bishop petitions Par-
ham ent, it is entered on the minutes as “ Bill
Smith ” (or whatever his name may be), “ styling
himself Bishop of Such-and-Such.” And they
are, in the eyes of the law, of no more importance
than the heads of the Presbyterians, or of the
Baptists, or even than Jos. Syme, the Bishop of
the Atheist denomination.

“Lemuria”: In N.S.W. Assembly, the
other evening, Yacker Member Sleath referred to
the awful females who compose the committees of
many “charitable” institutions—particularly those
that look after the Girl who Did —as “ long-nosed
haybags.” Ribald, but true. The immorality of
the average young person who has to go before
these committee ladies and explain the details of
her “fall ” is a gem of rare beauty compared to
the callous and skinny virtue of her interrogators.
The self - appointed “ ladies’ committee ” is a
grisly horror that should be suppressed with
dynamite rather than allowed to handle public
money.

Several Adelaide clergymen announced
recently that they have decided to make nightly
visits to the squares and park lands with a view
to suppressing the alleged improprieties that
occur there. This is about the rockiest job any
parson can undertake, and unless they are built
on fighting principles these rev. gentlemen will
be sorry they were ever born with a face. It isn’t
many years since an Adelaide social purity official
took on a similar job, with the result that his
features for weeks resembled a plate of scrambled
cat.

Two hundred and twenty-five . persons
called at the Melbourne morgue in one day for
the alleged purpose of identifying the body found
tied up in a box floating seaward down the Yarra.
Almost every one of them stated that he or she
had lately been harrowed by the loss of a dear
female friend exactly answering to the description
of the deceased, and their object was to take
away Jane or Sarah from her damp slab and give
her Christian burial. The attendant kept moving
them on persistently, for the front rank wanted to
stay and look at deceased too long, and the rear
of the procession wasn’t getting a fair show.
Some of the visitors had their pockets bulged out
—apparently by lunch ; and they were all sickbed
over by the craving to stay and keep identifying
the body till far on into the night. In its own
small way the Yarra body is a great draw, and a
morgue admission fee of Is. —to go towards re-
ducing Turner’s deficit—is a matter worth con-
sidering.
* * • *
The Congregational Church, which owns
Dr. Bevan and Dr. Jefferis and half-a-dozen other
conspicuous men, is harder up for parsons than
any other denomination. The explanation is that
when the shepherd gets old and can’t hustle round,
or when he loses his front-teeth and can’t speak
with his original clearness, the flock thanks him
for his long and invaluable services—and sends for
another man. TheC.C. has no provision for keeping
a man in harness as the Methodists have, and no
provision for old-age pensions. A minister who
had preached for nearly a generation to one of the
wealthiest down-south congregations was thus
lately informed roundaboutly that he had become
a back number. He had to go out of the
church he had built up, to face the task of build-
ing another ; and, though he succeeded better
than most, the struggle ended in paralysis and
death.
* * • *
At the recent destruction by fire of the
Royal Oak Hotel, Wellington (M.L.), while a
frenzied mob of half-dressed women and men
rushed shrieking through the smoke-laden corri-
dors seeking a way out, a calm and superior
tourist rang for the porter to help him downstairs
with his luggage. The porter, who was feeling
his way through the smoke, trying to find and
arouse stragglers, told him to go (unnecessarily) to
blazes, and get downstairs quick. One man
jumped out of the window on to a lower building,
and went clean through the roof, escaping with a
broken leg. An American got out on the tele-
graph wires, and thence per ladder to the street.
Many ladies escaped in nightdresses, and two
commercial travellers were burned to death. And
the man abovementioned calmly humped as much
of his luggage downstairs as he could manage, and
then found the half-dressed and singed landlord,
and complained to him very severely of the porter’s
discourtesy and neglect of—haw—business.

telephone people appear the following addresses :

The “ ditto ” has been omitted in the case of 967,
and the public regards it simply as a place of
“general inquiry ”—an encyclopaedia, free in-
formation bureau, oracle, and general know-all.
They use it accordingly. Some specimens from
one day’s list of questions :
What is the Daily Telegraph pay-day ?
What is the cost of a cablegram to Maoriland?
When is the next mail-steamer to America?
What is the present wholesale price for bonedust?
When does the early train leave for Moss Vale?
Is a horse-box going- to Berry to-night?
How far is it from Hay to Aibury ?
Inquiries start: “ Hullo ! is that 967 ? ” “ Yes.”
“Who?” “What?” “When?” “How?”&c.,
&c. Querist No. 1, a city merchant, was highly
wrath at the “ misrepresentation of the Govern-
ment publication.”

by owned dogs of the particular neighborhood,
and at night there is little to be found. Some
make house to house visits to solicit alms of food,
and others burglariously enter back-yards at
night, finding out places where no dog is kept, or

TWO PHANTOMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, when all the light is fled,
Are you ever visited
By two phantoms, wan and gray,
Who would lead you far away,
Past strange faces, ghastly pale,
To the Land of No Avail ?
They are Memory and Regret-
No one may their names forget—
In their hands they hold the years
Once baptised with scalding tears ;
Years in vain you would recall,
They have bound them all in all.
Through each haunted, spectral street
Of the Past, their noiseless feet
Still will lead you on and on,
Till your face grows old and wan.
And the years are waning pale
In the Land of No Avail.
No sweet voice will greet you there,
No glad face will meet you there.
Joy and Mirth, and Love and Song,
All were crucified by Wrong.
’Tis their voiceless souls that meet
In the haunted, silent street.
They will bend stern eyes on you
When your feet are passing through ;
Eyes that burn through unshed tears,
Gathered there with tortured years;
And your heart will weep and wail
In the Land of No Avail.
CARINI.

them to feast on the refuse. Some appeal to the
well-fed dogs they meet, who often show them
where there may be scraps lying unappropriated,
and sometimes invite them to a feed or occasionally
bring something to them, but this hospitality I
have only seen extended by large dogs to those of
small size ; a dog of, say, retriever size whom no-
body owns is an outcast indeed, and speedily suc-
cumbs to hardship and peril. Some of the dogB
appear to take kindly to their condition ; others
seem to feel their position very keenly, and roam
from house to house, or accost persons in the
street, making overtures in the hope of finding a
home and lawful visible means of support. I have
seen them holding a public meeting late at night,
in the Treasury Gardens ; they assembled from
all quarters, apparently carried a resolution, and
then the leaders, with a part of the crowd, went
off together, whilst the rest straggled away to
accost human passers-by and beg for leave to
accompany them.

The ‘ ‘ Yarra tragedy ” was a most accept-
able throw-in for the Melbourne dailies, and they
gloat over the details of the boot-box packed
with nude female as though it were a Xmas box.
Said the respectable Argus last week : “ The jaws
are to be removed and placed in spirits, and there
is little reason why-this course should not have
been pursued immediately after the inquest. Froifl
the first day the body would have been extremely
difficult to identify, and the steadily-increasing
heat since then has so hastened decomposition
that the face of the deceased has now lost all
semblance of humanity.”
In a family difficulty which recently broke
out in a small Victorian town the respondent was
found in the co-respondent’s bedroom at 2 a.m.,
and trouble arose. Respondent said she had gone
there to ask co-respondent what was the best way
to make ginger-beer. The- explanation looks per-
fectly feasible, too, for any lady is liable to wake
up in the small hours of the morning and feel a

desire to know at once how ginger-beer is made,
and if she can’t sleep for wondering, and the co-
respondent knows how to make it, she would natur-
ally wake him up to inquire. Better to do that
than lose her sleep, anyhow. A lady who has
been awake all night strucking to remember the
ingredients of ginger-beer always looks drawn and
haggard and old in the morning.

Dear Bulletin. —That woman who wrote
about “ Widows and Other Women ” condensed a
lot of solid fact into her screed ; but she gave the
average male thing over 24 credit for a lot of
emotion that he doesn’t possess. The great over-
ARISTOCRATIC
INSTINCT.
OCCASIONAL GRAFTER:
“ Don’t yer think yer
ought to go to work
so metimes,
same as yer
brother? I
wonder they
stand it at
home. ”
DOOLEY (of the push): “Surely to ’eaven
a family can support one bummer!”
whelming attraction of the widow is that she
generally has the first old man’s assets. A little
income, or at least a snug home, or a house full of
furniture, make a mighty impression on the heart
of man. The widow’s charms look brighter in the
glitter of the loot that hangs around her. If there
is no loot the average male thing is very prone to
regard the widow as damaged goods. Truly, her
greater knowledge of exactly how much ankle and
underskirt go to the ounce may balance that, and
just place her level with the garden variety of
female—but nothing more. As a 30-year-old male
thing, with a jaded appetite, the writer honestly
believes that any male over 24, having the choice of
a gilded widow and an unplucked peach with an
equally valuable bullion stem, would five times
out of six bite at the younger delicacy. These re-
marks only apply to males over 24 years—under
that age they are not responsible for their actions,
and should be kept chained up. — Rangitata.
* * * *
An unmarried woman died in a Southern
city recently, and at the inquest three doctors
were examined. Dr. A. deposed that he had profes-
sionally attended the woman, and she had in-
formed him that she fell downstairsand injured her-
self. On his advice Dr. B. was called in, but she
died in less than a week afterwards. He attri-
buted death to “ exhaustion following on hemorr-
hage, resulting from an injury, causing miscarriage
and local peritonitis.” Dr. B. deposed that death
was due to peritonitis following on abortion;
Dr. C., who post-mortemed the case, said ditto,
but added that he could not say if violence had
been used. Neither Dr. B. nor Dr. C. considered
it probable that the abortion resulted from an
accident. If they had seen the infant they would
be able to say positively whether or not an illegal
operation had been performed. But the infant
was not produced, Dr. A. stating that it was in
such a condition that he had thought it best to
carry it away in his valise and drop it overboard
from a penny steamer. The “coroner’s ’quest”
evidently considered this the proper thing for a
respectable family doctor to do under the circum-
stances, and verdicted : “ We find that death was
due to peritonitis, resulting from natural causes.”
Of course, had the mutilated remains of the infant
been produced in court the verdict might have
been different; or it might not. But the law
should certainly object to any doctor dropping
valuable evidence overboard in this fashion.

“ Constance Clyde ” on erotic literature :
The solemn words of the Sydney School of Arts librarian
re woman’s fondness for the worst sort of fiction have been
duly sent on to Maoriland, and wicked woman is being
once more held up to the contumely of Man, the Chaste.
This would be all very well, but for one unrealised fact:
it is only the reading man that reads ; whereas of the
women who read, the majority are those who, if they
were men, would go for whisky instead. That the library
is their pub., and that they prefer to read about vice
rather than indulge in it is surely a step in the right
direction ; and, anyhow, it is the only outlet allowable to
the numerous woman who is virtuously married to the
large red man, called Sandy. With vice as with cruelty !
Most of us would go into convulsions if forced to witness
torture cL la Chinois ; but we all like reading about it.
In fact, the more you want to read the description, the
less you desire to see the reality. Similarly, a certain
amount of so-called immoral literature is necessary to
mental health. It is, in fact, the virus which preserves
you from the real small-pox.

Dear Bulls tin. —Am
agent for the letting of some

At the recent sitting ot
the Grand Jury in Melbourne
to consider the alleged con-
spiracy charge against the de-
tective and the Crown’s hired
widow, two jurors were stone
deaf. This was represented to
the Chief Justice. He said the
Statute didn’t provide for such
a contingency; didn’t seem to
care whether jurors had their
senses or not. So the deaf ’uns
were sworn and had to be yelled
at by the foreman, and gesticu-
lated to, and have the matter
explained to them by signs and
grimaces. For all the Statute
cares, a Grand Jury may be
formed of seven deaf men and
five imbeciles.

Dear Bulletin.— The Yarra boot-trunk
tragedy has been the means of exposing the un-
suspected friskiness of many a Melb. damsel. All
day long sorrowing parents stroll into the Melb.
morgue to see if the body is that of their daughter
Mary, and they all have the same story to tell:
“ Mary left home a week ago, sayin’ she was
goin’ to stop at Mrs. Smith’s, an’ we’ve bin to
Mrs. Smith’s, an’ they told us she’d never bin
there at all. Two or three times before she said
she was goin’ to stay at Mrs. Smith’s, an’ it turns
out now that she never went there.” The writer,
who was in attendance at the morgue for three
days after the discovery, must have heard that
story related fully 50 times, without a single
variation, except when the daughter’s name was
Ann, and she was supposed to be staying with
Mrs. Jones. The awful tendency of Mary to say
she is going to Mrs. Smith’s, and then go some-
where else, is a question which may be studied by
Bishop Thornton, of Ballarat, and the spectacled
harridans of the W.C.T.U. So far as the present
scribe is concerned, he is quite content to study
the photo of Mary, as produced by her sorrowing
parents, and to pronounce the judgment that Mary
is a very nice girl, and would have been wasted at
Mrs. Smith’s, anyhow. —M‘G.

Melb. detectives waited anxiously for the
boot-box murder reward to be gazetted before
they made any rash promises to informants, for
the Crown has a mean way of going back on its
promises. At the time of the murder for which
Castilla, the Philippine boy, was hanged, ,£lOO
reward was announced ; but, before it was formally
gazetted, Castilla was arrested. Then the Crown
didn’t gazette the reward at all, and nobody got
anything.
A South-suburban Melb. clergyman, who
is a conspicuous figure in the teetotal interest, has
just ceased bragging about a notable convert of
his. The convert was a local exhibition inebriate,
who became suddenly sober and l’espectable, and
his acceptance of grace created quite a sensation
in hop-beer circles. Of course he reverted, and
he fell in a disgustingly ostentatious manner. The
first the cleric’s family knew about it was when
the awful example clamored at their door at the
dead of night, and then raised Gehenna and fits
because they refused to take in a beer-sodden
companion of his, who he insisted was the Rev.
Blank himself. The clergyman has since learned
that the horrid example had awakened half the
suburb, hauling his shocking friend from door to
door, endeavoring to get people to take him in,

TO MY LADY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I love to think of thee at night,
Thy snow-white body laid asleep
Within a room which moonbeams keep
Tender and pure and pale with light.
I love to muse upon thy charms,
The beauty of thy perfect form,
Lying in slumber soft and warm ;
The splendor of thy restful arms.
0, night-wind blowing from the sea,
Heart-soother, sweet, consoling South,
Haste to that rosy bud, her mouth,
And kiss her thrice for Love and me.
Toy with her cheeks and glittering hair,
And perfume all the dim alcove
Where lies the lady of my love,
My goddess most divinely fair.
Thinking of her I wander on ;
Thinking of her, so pale and still,
I linger on the moonlit hill
Till night and all its stars are gone.
L. G. D.
BEYOND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Columbus of the Worlds, I felt the Night’s embrace,
And heard the winds that break upon the Shores of Space.
I saw the shadows flit across the mighty arc,
And watched a waning Sun that dwindled to a spark ;
Conceived the Awe of Life and knew that Time had died,
And I was not alone—my Soul walked at my side !
“Is this—is this the end ?” my heart cried, and a breath
In all the Silence stirred and whispered, “This is Death 1”
Then through the trembling void there throbbed a vague
unrest,
As when the Motherhood first swells the Mother’s breast,
And I, with eyes of light, that looked at Fear and smiled,
Saw DEATH at last—and, lo ! I saw a little Child 1
BERNARD ESPINASS[?].

About the best fortnight’s record of a
young Australian down-to-date is that of a South
Australian—N. W. Jolly. In the local senior
public (matriculation) exam, he came out easily
first, with the altogether unprecedented distinction
of “credits” in English, Latin, Greek, German,
pure mathematics,applied mathematics, chemistry
and physics. And while the result-lists were being
prepared, he did most to win the annual inter-
college cricket-match for Prince Alfred —as in past
years Hill and Darling did. He not only wicket-
kept brilliantly, but made top score. Then, in the
next few days, he went across to Melb. and per-
formed with equal brilliance against Wesley
College. Jolly was also the first to win the very
valuable Elder students’ scholarship. L'eitch,
probably the most precocious Adelaide ’Varsity
scholar hitherto, studied his life away at about 25.
But, then, Leitch didn’t play cricket.
