A WOMAN’S LETTER.
_ ~ Sydney, December 27, 1898.
My Dear Myee—-
Christmas again, and the old comediettas play-
ing as freshly as ever in the crowded shops. Timo,
half-past four, day before Xmas. Customers five
deep round the fancy counter. Small, thin woman
with roving eye manoeuvres through the crowd
and settles on a chair, asking vaguely for “ white
ribbon.” Harrassed shop-girl wants to know is it
to be “ wide or narrow ? ” “ I’m making a cosy.
Its I don t want it too wide.” ‘‘ 33aby ribbon,
then?” “Yes, baby-ribbon.” Box of baby-
ribbon produced, “Is that about the width,
madam?” “Is that white ribbon?” “Yes,
madam, that is white ribbon.” “That’s nice. I
wonder if that would do. Oh, that’s pretty with
the edge.” Looks at one sample five minutes.
Then looks at the other five minutes. “Is this
what you require, madam? (Yes, Mr. S., I’in
serving.)” “I’m just thinking. . . . That’s
pretty, isn’t it?” “That is twopence a yard.”
“ Twopence a yard. I wonder how much I would
want. It’s got to go round the edge of a cosy.
I m making one for a Christmas present
to” (this confidentially) “his aunt.” (“ Yes, Miss
8., I’m serving.) What length shall I cut?”
“I forgot to measure. Do you think it’s wide
enough?” (“Attend to you in one minute.)
Three yards, did you say?” “I’m just thinking.
Don’t you think it’s a little cream?” “No,
madam, these are white ribbons.” “ Well, I don't
know, I’m sure.” “Is it too narrow? Shall I
show you a wider one ? ” “I’m just thinking, ...
You see, I don’t know how much I want.” Her
eyes roam all round the shelves; suddenly she
asks, “Have you got any pink velvet ?” “Yes,
we have pink velvet. And shall you not require
any of these ribbons ? ” “ I think I’ll see the pink
velvet.” Shop-girl (aside): “It’s no good calling
to me ; I’m serving one of these women that won’t
buy anything because we’ve got everything she
wants. ”
The only pantomime of the season ought to
have snared more bruit and brilliancy than the
Firm is putting into “ Ali Baba.” The thin book
is eked out with barest helpings of pantomime
sauce, and the doings of a donkey quite eclipse the
principal actors’ share in the business. Johnny
Coleman’s method of playing lady makes up the
rest of the hilarity. As Ali Baba’s wife, the
gentleman wore the usual flat Coleman face, sug-
gesting that all the expression has been battered
out of it, yet highly expressive all the same. His
drollest frock was a crinkled yellow, that tried to
be stylish, and was ever so short in front. Elastic-
side cloth boots clad his turned-in toes, and, if he
had never said anything beyond “Fancy meeting
you,” the audience would have felt their money
well spent.
Pattie Browne. asGanem, looks as if she doesn’t
quite like her part. She wore an uneasy look at
the first night’s performance, possibly because she
hadn’t enough to do, which, in stage-land, is a
much greater trial than having too much. The
dapper little white suit of the first act made
a most dashing boy of her, and was composed
of a short white coat,, opening over white blouse,
white tights, and white cap with white feather.
Her second suit was a chic affair in cigar-brown,
braided or embroidered in white. This was fol-
lowed quickly by an elaborate creation in pink
silk decked with diamonds and emeralds, which
faithfully pursued all Pattie’s undulating lines. I
was sorry that her part gave her so poor a chance
to show what she can do well, for neither her sing-
ing nor her dancing are electrifying, and the
sparse bits of business that come her way are so
aggravatingly well done that you long to give her
a better opportunity to be the only Pattie.
Pretty Carnl Moore made her hit with the long
white legs and short skirts showing golden lining
. that figured in her gay little Parisienne song and
dance. That handsome, well-proportioned young
actress, Miss Jennie Opie, dressed Abdullah in a
telling uniform, carried herself like a soldier, and
sang as well as anyone in the piece. Little Francie
Adler was another successful vocalist. She made
an artless little fairy-queen in yellow tulle, and
was allowed to sing a pretty fancy-song in the
midst of those magnificent fairy corn-fields which
are really the chief feature of the piece.
This scene, one of Goatcher’s happiest, shows a
background of golden corn-lands, realistically
Eainted, and is livened up by a gorgeous poppy
allet. The poppy-frocks frill out like scarlet
lamp-shades from the waist, where they are nipped
in with a touch of green, and pale green legs carry
out the idea of the most effective flower in nature.
In among the poppy-people run a mass of little
grey field-mice, with long, grey tails. Child-faces
show under the mice’s visages, and little child-legs,
in grey velvet tights, skip about to the delight of
the children in the house, who don’t get their nor-
mal amount of amusement from other scenes in
this panto.
You would think that Saturday at Rand wick
gave nobody a chance to air a feather. The love
of racing must be a natural affection with Sydney
women, for the stand had quite a big complement
of sodden skirts and cloaks, and drippy men
cursed audibly at the crowds of female punters
fighting their way through torrents of rain to lay
’arf-a-crown on their fancy. Frocking, of course,
was nil ? Not at all. Rows of airy, filmy frocks
suggestive of garden-parties and clear skies
slavered conspicuously from all parts of the stand.
There seems to be one idea re race-frocking that
sticks in the public’s mind, and wouldn’t be
washed away by the second deluge—namel.y, that
your gown must trail yards behind and
before, must be composed of palest grey,
mauve, or dead-white, capped with nothing
more substantial than white feathers, and
shaded with a parasol too delicate to stand the
sun. Wet or fine, you will always come upon
bevies of these dresses at race-meetings, and on a
day like Saturday I never want to see them going
home.
Boxing Day’s sunshine brought out lots of well-
dressed women who pass unnoticed at more toney
race-meetings. Among these was the wearer of a
lovely Parisian frock of royal blue voile, the
clinging skirt built up in three simple tiers, a little
piping on the bodice, and a daring sea-blue waistbelt.
An original fancy completed this creation ; diamond
rings were worn in the ears instead of on the fingers,
not a bad idea for refined people who cannot
go without gloves. The day of the ear-ring is
evidently coming quickly; I counted a score of
perforated ears from which the marks of bondage
hung mockingly in this day of new womanhood.
Mrs. Stanley Mylins, who used to be the beautiful
Miss Maud Roberts, looked as pretty as ever in a
blue-and-white foulard with a shell pink sash.
Her sister, Mrs. Ambrose Pratt, was in artistic
white with little yellow sleeves falling off her
white ones. . With this party was a beauty-man
who is coining out strong in literature shortly.
He walked the lawn in a pointed beard like a
sketch from Du Maurier. Mrs. Rennie’s black-
and-white muslin, worn with a touch of cherry
and a black hat, belonged to the smartly-simple
tribe that is safe at any sort of function.
Speaking of the races, the unfortunate gee-gee
that goes by the name of Cin set quite a blow of
witticisms going among women who don’t joke
easily. My belief is ’! it the horse heard them,
and was flustered out of his race. “ I’ll take half-
a-crown’s worth of Cin.” “ Oh, I think you ought
to take more than that,” went the whole gamut of
changes, and is still being repeated for the benefit
of those who don’t often hear such a good thing.
A much-enlarged edition of the Lilian Tree who
last played English opera here is personating
Carmen at the Lyceum this week—Carmen in all
the glory of scarlet raiment, and pink roses in her
hair —a plump, ladylike Carmen, in looks a long,
long way from the gipsy-maid of the tradition,
But English opera always fills a want. Every
vocalist with three notes and a shoulder-shake
comes in to criticise, and tell the people around
who don’t know anything how the thing
should be done. Therefore (and, mind you,
it’s a highly respectable Therefore, and testi-
fies to the songful Australian’s disdain of
comic opera, and may have nothing to do
with every amateur’s pet hope that English opera
is going to make a place for him shortly) Satur-
day’s d.c. was long-haired beyond description. All
the musical world was there : Madame Keller-
mann, who, by the way, has just invented a charm-
ing fancy in Christmas cards, adorned with the
picturesque heads of three little Kellermanns ; the
copper-haired contralto so hugely admired by any-
one with an aesthetic soul; and all the fat tenors
and baritones of the present and past generations.
Well for Lilian Tree that her shoulder-breadth is
a capable affair. The burden of the opera fell
heavily there on Saturday night. One could not
but admire the plucky way in which she squared
up, and put on sauce piquante and a dash of
diablerie, and sang and acted as though her sup-
port was the very cream of companies. She could
not altogether redeem things ; still, Carmen
is Carmen, even with a chorus that insisted on
solo singing, and a lover suffering from an unhal-
lowed combination of coyness and home-made
trousers ; and things may be better by the time
you read this.
A fiery scarlet satin, barricaded with a lattice-
work of gleaming jet, brightened Miss Tree’s
first appearance. What a wonderful trans-
formation from the rather prim, slim Martha
of ten years ago. She is quite a dashing young
woman nowadays, and has any amount of go and
exuberance. But someone ought to tell her not
to decorate her frocks with big trellis-work pat-
terns that would broaden a broomstick. Her
third frock, a purple foundation, had more of this
huge pattern worked over it in green, and was
further aggravated by white “pickings” on the
corsage.
A vigorous burst of young-man applause greeted
Dr. Philip Muskett’s t’other-night lecture in Syd-
ney School of Arts when he quoted “Will Honey-
comb’s ” sel f-gratulation on choosing a wife who
would provide him a healthy heir to his estates.
But this sort of thing provides Angelina with a
new cause for alarm when Edwin’s eyes begin to
wear that dreamy, far-off expression that used to
mean liquor or the other girl. In place of the
heiress and the fortune-hunter, novelists of to-mor-
row will portray the sorrows of the healthy
woman and her bitter—“ No, George, you do not
love me for myself alone, but for my Jane and
William and Johnny that are to be.” Butman
will cease to be man when he takes to troubling
about children that don’t exist. There are spots
of possibly unavoidable foolishness in Dr. Mus-
kett’s lecture. F’rinstance, the moral tag that
exercise and strength of body will make a woman
more refined ; whereas, as a matter of fact, it is
extremely difficult for a healthy woman to be a
lady. A human desire for vittles is destructive to
refinement, as most people persist in understand-
ing the term.
No less than three fledglings made their first
flutter at the last Liedertafel concert. Perhaps I
ought not to call Mr. Norman Alston, the new
baritone, a fledgling, as I understand he has pre-
viously sung somewhere or other with success. A
correctly callow young Englishman of the type
adored by the average audience, he was naturally
encored for all his songs. His underground organ
seems rather low down for a baritone, and his
manner was built up of the wood that never con-
flagrates. Then there was a pretty slender pianiste,
Miss Minnie Daniels, with bird-like wrists that
weren’t equal to the heavy weight of a big Liszt
movement. Anyway, her yellow silk frock was
charmingly worn, with dark hair parted on the
side in the one becoming fashion that is never too
widely adopted. A girlish ’cellist, Miss Amy
Browne, was the third to make her primal bow
before a paying audience; she put more than the
average girlish expression into her playing, and
promises to do well some day.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Vie. Legislative Council’s At Home, if not
the biggest, is always the best-to-be-seen-at
function of the season, and gloom sits upon
the brow of the society woman who fails to
get that particular invite. Kindly William Zeal
really does know how to make things nice for his
guests. The enterprising hot wind that made
the ilacked Council Chamber a sort of ante-room
to the Inferno, and Brassey’s stertorous reading
of ‘ The Speech ’ a thing for inward groans, caused
the cool banqueting-rooms to seem a Paradise by
comparison : and each elaborately-dressed dame
or damsel who held the coveted blue card joy-
fully ascended to where the strains of music led
her. A winding rivulet of pale green silk along a
snowy length of table, amid a wilderness of palms
and flowers, was as grateful to the eye as
the oasis in the desert. The dressing kept the
viands in countenance. Mrs. Brassey, in the
pale harmony of primrose silk and white gui-
pure she wore at the garden - party, was as
amiable to all and sundry as if she had been the
hostess of the day. Most of the selectest wore fine
India muslin over light-colored silk. Mrs. Wrixon
and Mrs. Sargood kept their reputation for serious-
ness before their eyes in the hues of their gowns,
and Mrs. Turner went in strongly for gold lace.
The host looked particularly happy in his black
velvet and ruffles—it is even suspected that he has
some lurking desire of matrimony hidden in his
bachelor-breast. The Labor-members and their
belongings had betaken themselves to the House-
garden, and quite an Arcadian-touch was given to
this part of the proceedings by the heavy Hancock
and little Dr. Moloney engaging in a contest of
knocking each other’s hats off with long crooked
sticks, while their ribbon-bedecked youngsters
sported round them on the green. It was possibly,
too, the nearness of Xmas that made a group of
fat members embrace each other with tears of
rejoicing.
“Janet, Lady Clarke, with a shade of disap-
pointment on her brow and funereal plumes in her
hat, leaned over the railing of the Australia when
departing from Melbourne the other day, possibly
meditating the worth of a goodly number of folks’
effusiveness. The Austral Salon didn’t put in
even the slenderest representation ; the Alliance
was chiefly there in the form of Mrs. (Justice)
Holroyd, panting up the gangway with a big
bunch of lilies, crimson carnations, and corn-
flowers, tri-colored with broad satin ribbons. Some
private friends arrived with baskets of roses and
sweet peas, fresh from the florist’s windows. Mrs.
Madden, with her sister, Mrs. Colonel Bingham
(neither of whom brought the parting vegetable),
elaborately dressed for the Parliamentary function,
hovered round for a few minutes, and then made
off for the Government show.
“An audience consisting largely of fat frauen
and buxom fair-haired fraulein assembled in the
handsome concert-room of the German Club on
Wednesday to hear a German concert. The
temperature was a hundred and numerous in the
shade, the frauen and fraulein perspired furiously,
and for an hour and a-half on end the music was
solid Wagner—the first act of the master’s ‘Die
Walkure. ’ Many of the English part of the
audience looked as though they would have pre-
ferred ‘Walker, London,’ to ‘Die Walkure.’
Moreover, the number of big German eagles that
glared at one from the walls made one feel rest-
less. There were four of these fearful wild fowl-
two of them in brass—at the platform end, one on
each side of the room, and two were at the back,
all of them ■ with their tongues hanging out as
though panting for a drink. Moreover, there was
a big bronze bust of Emperor Wandering Willie
towering aloft.. What with the heat, and the hot
frauen , and the national fowls, and the bust, and
the hour and a-half of ‘Die Walkure,’ and the
hard chairs, the ordeal was trying.
“But the music was magnificent! Madame
Wiedermann vvas Sieglinde, and she sang the
furious music with a passion that harrowed one’s
nerves. Indeed, so rapt was she in her part that
she(caused the great joke of the evening. She
sang with the full score in front of her ; and at
one part, where Siegmund (Herr Heinmer) ought
to have chimed in, she went on with his part,
having finished her own, and left him staring
with his mouth wide open, like the brass eagle at
his back, in sheer amazement. Then all the per-
formers burst out laughing, and Madame stopped
and let Siegmund take up the running as per score.
The present writer has not heard such music for
years. . The perspiring frauen, and the thirsty
eagles, and the heat would be worth enduring over
again to enjoy ‘Die Walkure ’ so sung.
“The big Alexandra Theatre was packed from
the front stalls to the back of the gallery for the
Conservatorium students’ operatic concert. And
the show was good. Miss Adelaide Donovan, who
appeared both in the first act of Nicolai’s ‘ Merry
Wives of Windsor ’ and (in page’s dress) in act 11.
of Mozart’s ‘ Marriage of Figaro,’ was a discovery.
She can act as well as sing. She was a bit shy of
her breeches in ‘Figaro,’ and moved about at first
as though in imminent dread that a button or a
seam was going to give way. But she soon lost
the sense of strangeness, and acted Cherubine with
rare spirit. Her Mrs. Ford, in the ‘ Merry Wives,’
sparkled with humor. Marshall Hall also had a
big reception.
. “The ornamentation on the women’s costumes
m the ‘ Geisha ’ is rich and beautiful enough to be
genuine Japanese gold embroidery-work. Perhaps
it is. Certainly it is done from Japanese designs.
Dragons and other wild beasts, worked in gold
thread, spread over the skirts of the principals.
Dorothy Vane’s last dress is a marvellous Jap
creation in gold thread and vellum silk. Cer-
tainly these ‘ Geisha ’ dresses are a vast advance
on the ‘Mikado ’ Jap costumes, either here or in
London, and must have cost the Firm a big price.
Lut why, seeing that the women are so adorned,
is not real gold lace used for the naval officers’
facings ? Unless the present writer is mistaken,
the adornments in these cases are only yellow
braid.
“Re current trial of medium for unqualified
‘healing,’ there are duller ways of passing an
evening than at a stance. My favorite medium
wears an imposing white vest and is generally in-
habited by the spirit of a German doctor. That’s
why he says, ‘ Goot efenings ’ so often. When a
snug congregation at one shilling per head is
gathered, the medium pulls faces and kicks up—
which are preliminary trance symptoms. Now’s
your time to stand up and, addressing him re-
spectfully as ‘Doctor,’ consult him re your ail-
ments in the presence of the listening multitude.
One girl, who had come in her mother’s bonnet,
veil, specs., and mantle, asked in a tearful, quaver-
ing voice : ‘ What’s wrong with my knee, please
doctor ?’ And the spirit answered, ‘You are old,
sister, and your leg will not be better this side of
the grave. If you have spasms, take tincture of
capsicum, dear sister! After this seance I will
speak to you privately through the medium about
your leg and give you lotions and mixtures. I
cannot say all I wish about your leg here, dear
sister, because of two unbelievers sitting near the
door.”
“Impecunious parents, with many daughters to
provide for, might do worse than apprentice one
of the tribe to a palmist. Local hand-readers look
twice as plump and prosperous as the average
governess. And their haughty demeanor towards
clients contrasts comfortably with the conciliatory
mien, characteristic of the worritted school-marm
in her professional dealings. One fat, frumpish,
ungrammatical sibyl, residing in a fifth-rate
suburb, can afford to rest on her oars the whole of
Saturday. You should see her turn away with
scant ceremony would-be customers who, ignorant
of her weekly vacation, arrive by every tram and
vainly entreat the soothsayer for two-and-sixpenny
samples of the Future.
“ Another futurist has elegant rooms in the city.
Cool, green matting, quaint rush chairs, plants in
Oriental jars and an attendant who interprets
dreams, and discusses signs and omens while her
employer peruses palms in an inner holy of holies.
If you desire a soothing half-crown’s worth from
this particular wise woman, speak her fair, and
tactfully ignore blunders. If she starts with the
announcement that you “sing beautifully and
associate freely with a widower, whose first wife
died of mumps,” better not gainsay her. If you
do, you will be baited with gout and consumption
in the family, near relatives in the lunatic asylum,
manners calculated to win you enemies, divorce
proceedings looming over your clan, no matri-
monial prospects, etc., etc. But if tenderly
treated she will probably paint you a rosy Future.
Caebinnation Peacock, Col. Secretary (Vic.),
told the kiddies at Brassey’s school-feed, the other
day, that when he was a small boy with a big
laugh he was a guest, like them, at a similar food-
scramble. That was in Governor Bowen’s day.
Peacock remembered it, because he lost his bun on
the occasion, and his Excellency commanded that
he should be supplied with another. This little
anecdote didn’t seem to make much impression on
the audience.
“On arriving at one of the Very Naicnst ‘At
Homes ’ in Melb., recently, each visitor was
handed an artistic card, embellished with the
words, ‘ Guests are requested to remain silent
during recitations. ’ Nobody had the pluck to bolt
there-and-then, and, scared by the awful mandate,
the whole assembly sat with a dazed expression
and twiddled its thumbs, and remarked, ‘Oh, how
nice! ’ at intervals, while the hostess led off
with ‘ Curfew shall not reeng too-o-night, ’ and
followed with seven other pomes in quick succes-
sion. Then there was a pause of 30secs., while
the lady got her breath, and all the people bounded
up, thanked her for the ‘ very pleasant evening,’
and bolted for their wraps like a routed army
lighting out for a place of safety.

to sister committee-women at charitable meetings
and sich, enumerating his contributions to the
Cause with bated breath, and quoting his opinions
on social problems in self-important tones. Hearers

like butter. Among themselves they talked with
the cattishness of their kind of a possible marriage
between Jimmy and Miss Norman (the Mrs.
Governor’s step-daughter). The cheerful spinster
in question didn’t seem to trouble much either
way. In looks she was like lots more English-
women—thinnish, blonde, blue-eyed, neat-nosed.
One of ‘ Uncle Albert’s’ daughters, by the way, is
engaged to Major Somebody or other.

Wong Ming, executed at Dubbo last week,
was Nosey Bob’s fifty-sixth victim, and the fifth
Chinaman put through his hands. He was Nosey’s
seventh at Dubbo.
James Cannon went off the other day at
Portland (Vic.), aged 90. Lived and dreamed
there ever since March, 1839, when he and his
wife—who survives him—arrived overland from
Melbourne.
The just-published “ Two Stories from
Dante, literally tomslated in the original metre,
by S. W.G.,” is by Q. Chief Justice Griffith, who,
like Gladstone, is an enthusiastic admirer of the
great Italian poet.

George Jonas Johnson, of Gippsland, the
other day, applied for a certificate of discharge
from his debts. Filed 31 years ago, and only
whitewashed yesterday, so to speak. A clean-up
in his old age, and making ready for business
probabilities in the “strange countree.” The
estate paid 4s. Id. in the £ —3l years ago.
The lately-appointed Bishop of Papua, the
stately Stone-Wigg, goes shortly to England on a
begging tour. Papua probably needs a loud and
vigorous episcopal beggar more than do other
Anglican dioceses in the South—and, anyhow, the
new Australian bishop always has a pathetic
yearning to show h/s new gaiters in English
society.
A glance through any Australian criminal
album will show that the vast majority of con-
victed men wear moustaches. Clean-shaven crimi-
nals are fairly common, but whiskered ones are
almost as rare as feathered frogs. On the other
hand, the bearded “crook” belongs to the most
contemptible class of malefactor, chiefly compris-
itig as lie does the fraudulent trustee, the swind-
ling solicitor, the Sunday-school teacher who
“blews” the trust-funds of the widow and
orphan in riotous living, and the perverted-
sexual offender. After a look into
the crimes of whiskered convicts,
the average man turns the page to
welcome the burglar and he gar.
rotter as men and brothers.

barely make himself intelligible, owing to his

There died recently, in a Queensland laza-
rette, a leper who was once a wealthy and well-
connected resident of Darling Downs district.
Before being consigned to the place of living
death he was treated by several doctors and nursed
by a woman for some months. That nurse is now
also an inmate of a leper reservation.
Melb. Herald seems to keep a reporter
incessantly on the tracks of O.J. Madden. Scarcely
a day passes without Morejaw Reay recording
some thrilling incident in the life of his ex-
excellency. If Madden, C.J., is not saving the
life of some wandering infant lost in the bush, he
is seizing bolting horses by the mouth and throw-
ing them back on their haunches in Collins-street,
while during his visits to his country home at
Frankston he is continually engaging in hand-to-
hand encounters with infuriated cattle. Madden’s
struggles with infuriated cattle up and down the
Herald columns will make Victoria feel angry
some day.
Old Gavin Brown, who died the other
week, was the most respectable Melbourne stock-
broker on record. He even maintained a connec-
tion with the church—Strong’s Australian Church
of recent years—without laying himself open to
suspicion. But his appearance seemed almost too
much of a personal recommendation. No stock-
broker was ever quite as good as Gavin Brown
looked.

had a very happy life.”
MA: “ What makes you think so, dear? ”

only wished that he could live his life over

Patrick Jonah Perkins, Queensland not-
able, was, in 1864, a brewer at Woods Point (Vic.)
and made the best beer in the province—retailed
then at Is. per glass. It was a common thing for
miners on Saturday night to call for “Pat Per-
kins,” as in former times they used to call for
champagne. M'Lellan, of Ararat, talking to
Perkins once about his beer, attributed its excel-
lence mostly to the mountain waters. “Mebbe,”
said Pat, “but it takes a jaynius for all that to
turn water into good liquor. There’s Bible
authority for that same.”
Old Samuel Hanna M‘Connell, who has
died at Korong Vale (Vic.), aged 80 years, was
present at the Eureka Stockade fight—on which
side is not known. Subsequently lie assisted to
arrest the notorious bushranger, Capt. Melville,
who suicided in Melb. gaol in’s7. Melville was
sentenced to be hanged, but was reprieved—a fact
with which he was not acquainted when lie did
his suicide.

Novelist William Black, dead in his 57th
year, probably fell a victim to his easy-going dis-
position. According to G. A. Sala, Black took
no exercise—wrote hard from breakfast time till
1 p.m. ; then devoted himself to a very hearty
luncheon, and sat around in his slippers for the
rest of the day, petted by his bevy of pretty
daughters. Black’s daughters were his heroines.
Most of the Tyson yarns afloat are as dull
as wet paint; but this looks characteristic. In
the very early days Tyson and another drover
were stuck up with sheep at the flooded Ovens
River. At Bendigo they were worth .£1 a head ;
but the other drover despaired of getting there,
and sold out to Tyson at 3s. Tyson built a bridge
of forked stakes, saplings, and bark ; got the mob
across, and took the profits. That, if true, argues
ingenuity as well as grit.
Melb. Corporation ought to cut up into
strips itd&atrocious portrait of Gov. Barkly. It
should be thrust in the coal-hole, with the bust of
John Thomas Smith. In the burst of enthusiasm
over Jaeky Tommy’s demise, they stuck up the
bust in the Council Chamber, as a memorial for
ever, but positively couldn’t stand his ugly mug.
Yet it is no worse than that of Socrates, in Melb.
National Gallery. The Bungaree man exclaimed,
“Blest if they ha’n’t got old Tom Carpenter
here!” Tom was the typical goldfields member,
the old Gobtie of Coppin’s famous Parliamentary
amateur night.
Vic. Judge Madden, when he first took
his seat on the bench of the Criminal Court, used
to follow the usual practice, and remind his
prisoners for sentence when they were found
guilty. But when all the sentences of the session
are delivered on the same day, the paper - give no
space to the Judge’s-weighty remarks, and merely
record that J. Smith got seven years. Madden,
C.J., has now given up the practice of remanding,
and when a prisoner is found guilty he gets
bis yard of stern admonition and his sentence on
the spot, and Madden’s scathing observations are
reported in full.
Sydney Mayor Mat. Harris and Alderman
John Harris, brothers, have each a son named
John, pursuing fame and fortune in the old lands.
The latest is that Mnt.’s John has attained a full-
blown lieutenancy in the 6th Enniskillen
Dragoons, and that John’s John hus flourished
into a full-blown surgeon in a London hospital.
Both Mat.’s John* and John’s John are .Sydney
youths. The Australian head of the Harris
family, “Surgeon Harris” of the old Fleet days,
was a man of clinging disposition, and the way he
clung to much land at Ultimo is the secret of the
Harris greatness.
Mayor Elliott, of Ballarat, is about the
most outspoken and fearless sort of civic dignitary
that Australia has yet possessed. He it was who
once publicly told Bishop Thornton that he had
“seen a better man made out of tea leaves,” and
he got home effectually on a temperance deputa-
tion. The cold waterists desired the abolition of
derrink at sports meetings, and as they had pre-
viously waited on Elliott without success another
councillor suggested on their arrival: “Perhaps
the gentlemen may have something new to say.”
“Yes,” observed Elliott blandly from his robes,
“they may want to tell the truth this time.”
Another time Elliott was in Melb. when a temper-
ano" deputation headed by ex-Premier Jimmy

desired an Act providing, among other things,
that all strangers found in an hotel on Sunday
should be punished by fine. “Yes,” growled
Elliott, “ and they should be allowed to pay their
“ 0.J.” : A smart man in New York has
published a book called, “ Americans of Royal
Descent.” Among other statements in this pub-
lication is one which “allows” that Admiral
Dewey is descended from Alfred the Great —twin-
ing along through three English kings, one French,
and one Scotch. English earls were also mixed
up in the business, up to the point where plain
Thomas Dewey left England for Amurika in 1633
—just 204 years before the admiral was horn. The
whole thing is perfectly clear. Where it touches
Australians is in the fact that the late Salmon
Wilson, big Victorian squatter, was also des-
cended straight from Alfred the Great. And as
Alfred hadn’t a large family, Australia rises to
state that it was there first, and if there wasn’t
enough Alfred for both Salmon Wilson and Dewey
to descend from, Dewey must go away and des-
cend from someone else.
Anging Judge ’Awkins, an old racecourse
identity, who had the job of smashing-up the
Tichborne Claimant when that bulky pretender
was prosecuted for perjury, has retired from the
English bench. Hawkins, although he went on
judging for 22 years, never quite outlived, the
ferocious failures of his judicial youth. He made
an absurdly bad start with the Staunton case, in
which two men and two women were convicted on
the charge of starving Harriet Staunton (wife of
one of the male prisoners) to death. Hawkins
summed-up in a Windeyeresque style, and. passed
the death-sentence upon all four prisoners with
great enthusiasm. Then there was a revulsion of
public feeling, and an enquiry which proved
that the wretched Harriet wasn’t deliber-
ately starved, or “done” to death, but had
been unnaturally treated by her natural pro-
tectors. The three Staunton prisoners got their
death sentences commuted to penal servitude, and
the remaining girl, not having been a “ natural
protector ” of the deceased, was set free, and be-
came a fancy-priced barmaid right away. Soon
after this, reckless Hawkins bounced a jury into
convicting another fellow on a charge of murder-
ing his drunken wife by giving her all the rum she
wanted. Hawkins assured the “murderer’’ that
he would be righteously hanged by the neck till
he was dead, but Hawkins was wrong again. The
sentence got reduced to something less than a
“lifer.” A hard, inflexible old sport with warts
on his face, is the ex-Judge. He happened to
marry a wife who dropped her aspirates and was
won’t to address him as ’Enryor “’Awkins ” at
the dinner-table, but he bears no blood-relation-
ship to the coster in the song.

Some interesting reminiscences of the
early days of Maoriland have been published in
connection with the recent death in Wellington
(M.L.) of Mrs. Dougherty, relict of Captain
Dougherty. The. captain lived at a whaling station
on Queen Charlotte Sound, and at the time of the
Wairau massacre took the women and children of
the then thinly-populated settlement over Cook
Strait to Wellington in two whale-boats. He at
one time left his wife and family in Queen Char-
lotte Sound in charge of the stores, and two hos-
tile natives came to Mrs. Dougherty and de-
manded possession. She resisted their claims,
and they retired, threatening that during the
night the whole tribe would descend on the settle-
ment, burn the place, and murder the inhabi-
tants. An anxious watch was kept by the unfor-
tunate woman and her daughters, and in the
middle of the night a great fire appeared on the
summit of a hill at the back of the bay. As the
flames leapt up, innumerable natives were seen to
pass in single file, and the watchers gave them-
selves up for lost. An old “ shell-back,’’ however, ■
who was in Captain Dougherty’s employ, crept
up to reconnoitre, and discovered that the two
Maoris who had threatened the place in the day
were walking round and round the fire, to give
the impression of numbers.

Aboriginalities.
Alligator tale from Cairns (Q.) Post
It will be remembered that a mule belonging to Mr. D.
R. Lewis had its tail bitten off while swimming the
Russell River some weeks ago, and that the animal
promptly retaliated by kicking its aggressor half-way
across the stream. Some people, especially our Southern
neighbors, affected to disbelieve this story, and made
merry at the expense of the mule, the alligator and the
allegation. Last week Mr. R. Cremer, photographer, of
Cairns, visited Mr. Lewis’s plantation, and while the
owner and the visitor were strolling along the banks of
the Russell they came upon the dead body of an alligator
about 12ft. long. The reptile had its skull fractured, and
closer examination proved the existence of a distinct
mark of a hoof. Mr. Cremer at once brought down his
camera, and secured a splendid photograph of the alliga-
tor. A glance at this will convince any doubting Thomas
that the reptile was killed bv the mule.

“E.S.S.”: With all that’s been said of
Eurombar and its lagoon, little mention has been
made of its brumbies, probably the largest herd
in Australia. In dry seasons trap-yards are made
A HANDY PAIR OF BOOTS.
PATSY : “ Where be yer boots, Tim? ”
TIM: “ Och, sure, an’didn’t yer know that th' owld mahn borried
thim to kick the concait out of Maguire the other night?
Maguire summonsed him nixt mornin', an’ he had to

at water-holes, and the brumbies entering at night
are shot down in the morning for their hides. A
few of the best are drafted out, broken-in and
sold. Prices, £1 to =B3. In ’94-’95 no less than
4000 were captured, and as many as 600 were
trapped in four nights. The stench of the dead
bodies was horrible, while crows and hawks flocked
there in thousands. With the breaking-up of the
drought ends the trapping season, and the brum-
bies recruit again in the dense brigalow forests.

In the old, happy alluvial days a party had
struck it rich on the west coast of Maoriland, and
when Christmas came they went on a riot that is
still remembered on the damp slopes of the Alps.
Towards the tail-end of the spree the flash men of
the crowd took to lighting their pipes with <£l
notes. After a while a wilder spirit lit his pipe
with a £5 note and excited temporary admiration.
But the big drink-proof Scotchman of the crowd
promptly extinguished him. He wrote out a
cheque for £lOOO, and lighted a cheap cigar with
it while the crowd fell back speechless, and the £5
note man dropped partially insensible in the door-
way. Then the Scotchman stalked away, and the
company looked at each other, and gathered round
the burnt fragment of the cheque, and looked at
it, and said “ My Gawd I ”
* * * *
Droving cattle from the Logan across Dar-
ling Downs in ’B4 ; man in charge decided to cross
the main range by an old bridle-track made in the
pioneering days by Fitz, of Hirst Vale. Tackled
it at daybreak. Mob strung out, and followed the
man in the lead until the steep parts commenced ;
then to keep those leaders moving was the devil.
Shouted, swore, and threw stones for 12 hours—
till our tongues were like leather and hands blis-
tered and bleeding. Half-way up the cattle got
timid and stupid ; some lost their footing, and,
rolling over, cut the legs from under others, and
in dozens they went bumping down. And the
great boulders that bounded in their wake were
things to dodge. At intervals a beast would slip
over a precipice, and twirl noiselessly through
space, and—crash ! —thud ! The old pack-horse
joined in the sport. Down he rolled, jingling
pannikins and hobble-chains, and scattering the
blankets and the bread and the corned-beef broad-
cast. Next day searched the gorges for a grey
mare we were bringing with the cattle, and found
her hanging, dead, in the fork of a tall tree at the
foot of a precipice.— Steele Rudd.

“H.M.T.”: The hoodie rat of W.A. is
probably the easiest-tamed animal in the world.
All you have to do is to catch him young, which is
an easy matter, and he at once becomes domesti-
cated. He is about the smallest representa-
tive of the marsupials, and when he starts doing
his eight-inch jumps at your heels he sticks to you
like poverty, but you can shake him off by wring-
ing his neck.

“C.J.H.": Re “J.R.B.’s” idea that a
log of wood in a tree-fork would-be raised by
growth of tree, have seen a dead spar piercing a
growing tree half-way down the trunk. Musb
originally have fallen into the fork and been sur-
rounded as the tree grew up. Hasn’t been raised
an inch since it fell. Also, a soft-wood like coola-
bah (with specific grav. greater than ironbark)
doesn’t float worth “shucks.”

to put some barbed wire into
an old fence. He had a whole
coil with him, and when he
“ let her go ” the wire made a
bound, and soon the whole coil
had wound itself around him

gry Ranges, Vic., and the
young Englishman who repre-
sented the funds - providing
syndicate lived apart from the
camp in a nice new tent He

nal wayof his own. One night,
however, the others were awak-
ened by fearsome screeches,
and, rushing over in their
shirts, discovered England’s
tent twisted in a large ball,

from within it came heart?
rending appeals for succor.
The others flung themselves,
upon the mystery in a body,

until the youth was able to
get out —a pitiful object, bat-
tered, scratched, and scared
into fits. The “thing’’ was
still snared in the tent, and
cautious investigation ascer-
tained that it »was a big old-
man kangaroo. He had jumped
over a breakwind of boughs
clean through the roof of the
tent, and in the ruction that
followed the two got hope-
lessly tangled in the ruins.

“Wallaroo ’ : Just now the N.S.W.
table-land is groaning beneath the inflietion of
countless herds of lean kine discharged from the
waterless west, and all stock routes are strewn
with emaciated corpses. On falling, a thirst-
stricken, exhausted wreck is abandoned to several
days of patient suffering before finally p gging
out, and yet how easy for all drovers to emulate
the example of a merciful few and carry revolver
and ammunition with which to put the poor plead-
ing-eyed creature out of its misery. For cold-
blooded indifference, however, the action of a cocky
publican (owner of an abundantly watered well)
takes the cake. Small mob of horned skeletons,
smelling water, were madly striving to make a
way to it. Drover explained that they had been
waterless for a couple of days, and that the
Government well, six or seven miles back, had
given out. Would Mr. Public-house supply them ?
Was not particular about charge. Mr. Public-
bouse refused. “It would make the yard too
dusty.” And so the unfortunate, bewildered
brutes were forced away with a pitiful lowing that
sounded like a despairing human moan, and with
another day of thirst and starvation before them.
to a wild, weird chorus. Occasionally a note best
described as a howling bark—the voice of a
hybrid. _ While all other Australian animals
harmonise, all marked by nature as loitering
along four thousand years behind the times, the
dingo is a discord. The pricked ears, the char-
acteristic face mark, the wild, distant look in
the eyes and the brushy tail, distinguish him from
the tame dog. The blaek-and-tan dingo, so pom-
mon at the present day, marks a cross with the
sheep-dog. In his way, he is the squatters’ worst
enemy, for, having ail the cruelty of the pure
dingo and his greed for killing, he is less distrust-
ful of man and consequently more daring. The
pure dingo has a slinking habit and a hang-dog
expression; he is a cur by nature and necessity.
Watch him as he conies with swinging trot along
a bush-track. The dejected, half-asleep look, the
drooping head and tail, give him an air of deep
sorrow and reflection. But if the smallest twig
be broken by the way he sees it and immediately
starts into the scrub as though his enemy had
swooped down upon him. His tracks over night
tell their own story. The small foot-prints in the
dust or sand show how he trotted along to a
certain point where they cease. The last marks,
more deeply sunk, show that something startled
him, and, sticking his claws down hard he took
his side leap for cover.
* * • *
Sam was a stockman of ye olden time.
Running brumbies one day with Splodger he
galloped into a clump of timber so thick at one
spot that the only opening was through the fork
of a tree. He could have pulled up, but, as he
said: “No bloomin’ brumby ever got away from
me once I set him, and that lot wasn’t goin’ to
best me. Put ‘Blue Blazes’ at it, an’ he went
up like a rocket an’ through as clean’s a whistle.
Jes barely touched each side with me knees.”
(Sam here spat three times into the fire to allow
time for impressions). “Splodger followed on
Streak-o -Steak, but Stieak was a bit fat and
jammed in the fork of the tree. I yarded the
brumbies single-handed—the very mob 25 Bunga-
bumby cracks ’ad been after for two years. Went
back then and levered Splodger out with a long
pole. Saddle came off with him.” Sam paused
and spat seven times into the fire. “Streak’ad
urt himself strugglin’, an’ I ’ad ter shoot him.
People often wondered after how the skeleton
come to be standin’ way up in the tree. Stranded
in a flood, they reckoned. I knew better.” Sam
laughed, then spat the fire out. He was a stock-
man of ye olden time.

Dear Bulletin.—lt is a mistake of many
“ t’othersiders ” to think that the terms “Sand-
groper” and “Straight-hair ” arc synonymous for
a Westralian (white) native. A “Sandgroper ”is
a white native of Westralia. The term “ Straight-
hair ’’ was given by the free inhabitants to the
convicts on account of their always having their
hair cropped short. It cost a friend of mine a
large black eye the other evening here to discover
the difference. — Outsider.
AMONG THE COOKIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Down among the cookies, where the mealy murphies
grow,
Down among the delvers, where the dusty “ whalers’’ go,
Down among the cornfields, where the mortgage makes
the woe,
Down among the furrows dull a-harvesting we’ll go.
Where they’re up before the sunrise, and the work Is
never done,
And you’d wonder where the fun lies in a living hardly
won,
Where the binder sheaves they’re “ stooking ” in a straight
or straggled row,
And there isn’t any cooking much, a-harvesting we ll go.
Where the time to roam and love is when the roasting day
has sped,
And the laughing moon above is peeping from a cloudy
bed,
Where we love the maids and leave them with a feeling
kin to woe—
But it never seems to grieve them while the rovers come
and go.
Vio.

THE MAN WHO HADN’T GOT A THIRST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I reckon he’s the rummest cove that ever I have
knowed,
And I’ve been close on thirty year continual on the road :
I’ve run across all sorts of men when humping on the
track,
I’ve met some mixed lots going round with blueys
on their back,
I’ve heerd all kinds of notions—from the swell who
owned a run,
To the broken-down old drummer, grown cranky from
the sun,
From the mad Salvation soldier-man (who preaches to the
scrub),
To the weary-looking boozer that hangs around the pub.—
I’ve heerd them argue all day long the queerest sort
of rot,
But this cove in particular, he beat the blooming lot.
It’s this way—me an’ Greasy Bill was out at Yarranbar,
And this ’ere cove was sitting down a smoking a cigar.
He hadn’t got a word to say to Ben, or Bill, or me,
But sat there smoking by hisself as peaceful as could
be.
Some other chaps kep’ coming in, and drinking round the
bar,
But this ’ere chap he never spoke, but puffed at his cigar.
Just then, when Bill and me had drunk our grog and paid
our score,
A shearer chap from Bulyersi comes shouting in the door.
“ How are you Ben ? Let’s have a drink. Now you, what
shall it be?”
We named our liquors, one by one, until it came to he.
“ Now, stranger,” said the shearer-chap, “ I do not know
your name,
Don’t be afraid to call your drink, you’re welcome just
the same.”
Then this ’ere chap—it makes me laugh until my ribs are
sore,
For, blow me, if I ever heard so rum a go before—
He said he wouldn’t have a drink ; and then he told them
why,
And, blow me, if I didn’t think the chaps would mostly
die.
Old Ben was wiping out a glass when this ’ere chap, he
spoke,
And Ben, he gave a sort of smile—he thought it was a
joke,
Until he saw the covie’s face, and then I thought he’d
burst.
“ Oh, Lord ! ” cried Ben, “ my blooming oath, he hasn’t
got a thirst!
The man is mad, I’m sartin sure, or else he wouldn t
think
That thirst had anything to do with joining In a
drink.” '
The chaps they laughed until they shook the counter
nearly down,
And Ban, he dropped the glass he had, likewise a half-a-
crown.
“ Well, stiffen me!” said Ben, “ old man, you are the very
first,
The only man I ever knew who hadn’t got a thirst.”
G.S.E.D.

WHITE LABEL LAGER
“ Snell Two men,
Dunstable and Grutt,
were paddooking in the
side of the hill, in the
vicinity of Cassilis, Gipps-
land, when suddenly the
latter noticed the hill
coming at him. He turned
to leave, and was pounded
in the back with a dull
thud,that drove him as a
bat drives a ball. When
he pulled himself to-
gether, he found a sloping
pile of gravel where there
had been a clean high face
in the hill, and, in one
spot, a little human hair
showed up out of the dirt.
This was Dnnstahie.
Grutt’s tools were buried
under the fall, but he
went to work with his
finger-nails, and clawed
the dirt away from the
face of his unconscious
mate, and then sat apart
to consider a very difficult
situation. He was miles
away from human aid,
and was so badly hurt
himself that he could
barely crawl It would
take him hours to drag
his battered body to the
nearest camp, and mean-
while the man pressed and
packed in the gravel there
would probably die of
suffocation if not of his
injuries. Grutt continued
to think for five minutes,
then he selected a stick
and started to dig, not
about his buried mate,
but along towards tliei
race, and presently he
had a small stream trick-
ling over the fall. He
dug and dug, and suc-
ceeded in turning the
race. After that, the job
was easy ; he sluiced Dun-
stable out of the fall in
40min., and a fortnight
later the mates were graft-
ing again.
“Yarrum”: Other day, whilst out on
Lake Illawarra with a boating-party, spotted a
black shag (“ Fisherman Jack ”) on a dead tree,
close by. Was just going to shoot; but he looked
so sick that I hadn’t the heart. Before we had
proceeded far, he disgorged two unfledged butcher-
birds, but was still looking far from well. It is
not generally known that these fishermen occa-
sionally rob birds’ nests.
• ♦ • •
“ Trav.” ; Was once on an out-back coach-
trip, in company with two constables, two long-
sentence prisoners, a drummer, and nine bottles
of whisky. Second day out, driver said “He’d
be damned if he was going to drive a coach for
any 50 bob a-week ; he was going to have a sleep.”
And he did, in the rack at the back of the coach.
Then argument started between a prisoner and a
constable as to which was the better waltzer, and
it was decided to settle it forthwith, with the
drummer as umpire. The convict’s leg-irons were
accordingly fastened up with a portmanteau-strap,
the other constable whistled an alleged tune, and
they started on the weirdest of fandangos. Pre-
sently the leg-irons fell down, and they both tripped
over them, and couldn’t get up, and it took half-
an-hour to bullock them back into the coach, where
the remaining Robert was weeping on the other
Erisoner’s shoulder, and swearing he d never hand
im over at the gaol ; he’d tear his blanky uniform
off first! It was a wild-eyed and drought-stricken
procession that reached the township, 10 hours
fate, next day.

Dear Bulletin. —The peculiar twist of the
brolga’s wind-pipe, which “Barwon’ notes, is to
be seen in the males of a few other birds rurally
known as “buglers.” In the black-and-white
goose the wind-pipe makes a completo coil before
passing through the breast-bone. Undoubtedly
From their strange wind-pipes come the clear
bugle-like notes of these birds. As. to the emu, I
think he does his “ drumming ” with the throat,
and not with the foot as generally supposed.
* • • •
Re snakes’ life-tenacity. Recently put
14in. brown snake alive into methylated spirit.
Bottle quite full, no room for air; yet half an
hour later when I took snake out it was ready to
crawl off.

Dear Bulletin. —Reply to “ Barwon ”
(B. 3/12/’9B). Don’t know voice of emu or appear-
ance of brolya, but whistling swan and whooper,
which I have often shot in China, both, have in
breast-bone a cavity in which the windpipe coils
very distinctly, and thus, I understand, is produced
the clear whistling note characteristic of the swans
in that part of the world. Have heard of other
birds built in same way, but swan is only one I
oan personally vouch for.—C.F.

Gov. M‘Gregor, of Papua, gives a descrip-
tion of a remarkable implement—the fishing kite,
used at Dobu. The kite is constructed of four
leaves, each a foot long and Sin. broad. Two
strings are required, one about a third of a mile
in length or perhaps shorter, one end of which is
in the hand of the fisherman, who regulates
position of kite. The other string attached to the
kite is long enough to reach the water and may be
from 100 to 300yds. long. To the lower end of the
latter cord is attached, not a hook, but a small
tassel, half-an-inch wide and three inches long,
made of spider’s web. The fisherman gets into a
AUSTRALIAN CHIVALRY.
1ST STOCKMAN: “Is that your wife chopping wood?”
2ND DITTO: “ Yes.”
1ST STOCKMAN: “I can’t bear to see my wife chop wood; I always yet up and walk away.”
canoe, goes to sea, flies his kite so as to keep the
tassel of spider’s web bobbing on the water. The
fish that catches this entangles its teeth in the
soft, loose, elastic bunch of web, from which it can-
not disentangle itself until it is quietly lifted into
the canoe by a small, triangular net mounted on a
forked stick. The web used is the product of a
large spider very plentiful at Dobu.
The “Godless Schools.”
Little faith can be placed in the Scripture
Education League of Victoria by either friend
or foe. Before the last general elections it in-
stigated a campaign in favor of the Irish
National Reading-book, and after the poll it
indicated that it had a majority in the Assem-
bly. Meanwhile, people had inspected the
reading-books recommended. They were found
to be thoroughly unfitted for the use of child-
ren, either on the mental or moral plane. They
contained many chapters full of recondite theo-
logical disquisition, and abounded in sexual re-
ferences not usually recommended tor the
close study of mere children. M’sP. who had
pledged themselves to the Readers, got marked
copies that filled them with horror. They were
particular in looking up the League officials,
when it was found that some of them had
never read the books. The League quietly
met, and withdrew the Readers altogether. It
was announced that a conference of the
churches would propound a new scheme of
lessons. This was repudiated, as the Irish
Readers were. Now the non-R.C. denomi-
national papers are beginning to attack the
League, as the R.C. officials have long done.
The Ballarat Church Chronicle refers to the
League’s operations as an attempt “ to tinker
up the faulty existing means.” Another reli-
gious paper, the Mitre, refers to the
League as “an undenominational hybrid.”
The league gets a certain amount of support
from politicians, because the religious crowd
needs placating. It is certain that the secular
features of the Vic. system will be kept un-
touched, and the politicians who are making a
noise about “ the religious training of the
young ” know they are quite safe in occasionally
barracking for the hopeless fad of a faction that
is doomed to be always in a minority.
“ Chqx”: James Tyson lived the miserable
existence of a gold-haunted hermit, piled up a
fabulous fortune, died in his sleep, was buried in
an ordinary timber coffin —and went the way of
all millionaires. He was followed to the grave by
about a dozen vehicles—filled mostly by expectant
legacy-hunters—and his remains had hardly settled
down before the nearest of kin were clamoring at
the law-courts for letters of administration.
* While Tyson was sleeping to death at Felton
station, another well-known man in Queensland —
Theodore Wood —was making merry with some
boon companions on the Wcdonga, just before
sailing for New Guinea. Wood came to Australia
some years ago with about <£loo,ooo, which he
razzled away in less than three years. Since then,
he has been private-secretary to almost everybody
of note in the South Seas—including New Guinea
Macgregor—and, in Tyson’s last moments, Wood
was joyously departing to fill a minor magisterial
post in Papua. The writer, who was present at
the “send-off,” couldn’t help contrasting the
animal spirits and boisterous good-fellowship of
the impoverished plunger with the bloodless
stoicism and narrowness of the millionaire. ’Tis
better to have had and spent than never to have
spent at all.
Tattoo inventory of an Australian criminal:
Man in fighting attitude, two girls under wreath, mal-
tese cross, and star tattooed on breast, sailor climbing
mast on inside muscle of upper right arm ; crossed flags
on outside of same ; flower, butterfly, two women’s busts,
and heart pierced with dagger on right forearm ; star,
crossed flags, and maltese cross on upper left arm ; cat,
woman’s head, clasped hands, Prince of Wales’ feather,
two doves flying, and cross on lower left arm; star on left
wrist; two doves flying over cross and heart pierced with
dagger with wreath underneath and scroll on top, three
stars, woman’s bust, star, cross, anchorand heart, clasped
hands, and maltese cross on thigh of right leg ; woman’s
bust on right calf, woman in theatrical costume on thigh
of left leg ; cross, anchor, heart, and woman’s bust on
calf of left leg. One previous conviction.

Here I sit upon a stick,
A stick that hit another stick ;
The other stick this stick has hit
Is hard to find—but I’ll stick to it.
The end has come—the day is done,
There’s nothin’ particlar
But a hill full of ditches.

The-stick that hit the other stick ;
The stick I would not have had missed—

The N.S. W. Lager Bier Brewing Co., Ltd.,
of Moore Park, Sydney, has placed upon the mar-
ket a splendid Lager Bier called Sydney Lager
Lier. This bier is the choicest thing ever offered
to the people of N.S. W. Analysts praise it most
highly ; doctors recommend it to convalescents
and connoisseurs at the last Sydney Agricultural
gave it first prize against all competitors.
n £ troubled the rational people of
N.S. W. is ‘what to drink.” There is no fun
in summer weather in drinking .fiery alcoholic
liquors that break up the nerves, affect the diges-
tion, heart, liver and kidneys; and on the other
hand there is only an intense vagueness and total
dissatisfaction in drinking wish-wash. Sydney
Lager Bier is the happy mean between these ex-
tremes, and people in the country who desire to
have the newest and best drink going should com-
municate with the N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing
Lo., Moore Park, Sydney, for particulars. Al-
though'the-new .lager is so much superior to all
others, it is the most reasonable in cost upon this
market.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
_?• Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
lUizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest,buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Sydney D.T.’s London correspondent gives
some anecdotes from Barry O'Brien’s new life of
Parnell:—
“ How did Mr. Parnell look when he came to your
meeting?” an Irish member was asked bv an English
Radical after the divorce case. “ Well,” said the Irish
member, “he looked as if we had committed adultery
with his wife.”
Walking with his private secretary in the Strand, Par-
nell was one day saluted by a Nationalist member. “ Who
is that? ”he asked Mr. Campbell. “ Don’t 3 r ou know ?”
was the answer. “It is one of our party, Mr. ."
“Ah !” said Parnell, “I did not know we had such an
ugly man in the party.” ,
When the Lord Mayor of Dublin presented him, from
the Irish people, with the Parnell Tribute—a sum of
£37,080 —his lordship, who had a pretty knack of speech,
had naturally prepared a few appropriate observations.
The ceremony was to be held at Morrison's Hotel. The
Lord Mayor, having been announced, bowed and began,
“ Mr. Parnell—” “I believe,” said Parnell, “you have
got a cheque for me.” The Lord Mavor, somewhat sur-
prised at this interruption, said, “ Yes,” and was about to
re-commence his speech, when Parnell broke in, “ Is it
made payable to order and crossed ? ” The Lord Mayor
again answered in the affirmative, and was resuming the
thread of his discourse, when Parnell took the cheque,
folded it neatly, and put it in his waistcoat pocket. This
ended the interview. The whole business was disposed
of in five minutes, and there was no speechmaking.
Fruit in season. In summer she provides
her meat; in winter, finds her store complete.
This is the position the thrifty housewife finds
herself when she takes advantage of the bountiful
supply of fruit we are going to have this season.
M‘Lean Bros, and Rigg, of Sydney and Mel-
bourne, have anticipated the season and are now
landing their stock of specially-manufactured
fruit-jars. Lightning, Mason, Chicago, Perfec-
tion, &c., &c., all guaranteed and air-tight. Jam-
jars ail sizes and kinds.*
Messrs. Craig and Aitken, of 680-682
George-street, Sydney, are a famous firm of hair-
dressers and tobacconists. The excellent “plum”
razor which they put upon this market has made
shaving a real pleasure, and any man who goes to
the shaving-pot with the feelings of a martyr has
only to get a plum razor in order to negotiate the
business with the air of a conqueror. Craig and
Aitken, too, are to the fore with specialities for
ladies ; and, as the hair is so important a part of
a woman’s beauty, Craig and Aitken deserve the
thanks of the fair sex for the beautiful fringes,
wigs, &c., &c., which they stock. Country hair-
dressers and wigmakers should take care to keep
in touch with the popular Sydney fhm, for by so
doing they will get all that is up to date in the
way of their business. Country visitors to Syd-
ney should call at Craig and Aitken's for a hair-
cut or a shave ; and, if they feel inclined for a
good smoke as well, they will find at 680-682
George-street the best pipes, tobacco and cigars in
town.*

A London theatrical costumier on the subject of stage-
illusions : Just as Sir Henry Irving had to be shortened
for Napoleon, so another celebrated actor, Mr. Wilson
Barrett, has often to be lengthened. Mr. Barrett is a
little below the average height, yet when he appears in
Kingly or other parts demanding physical presence his
aspect is that of a more than ordinarily tall man. An
inch or two is really given him by elevated boots, but a
good many fictitious inches are added by the illusion of
clothes and surroundings. His garments are cut low and
clear at the neck, and made to sweep round the fin-ure
These devices, with a careful regard not to place himself
in the vicinity of someone really taller than himself, raise
him from the undersized man of about Bft. 4in. ” (this
must be understating Barrett’s height) “ to the tragedian
king of 6ft.” All the same, the physical fraudu-
lency of Barrett obtruded itself upon Australian
notice from the first. As Claudian he had an unnatural
appearance, and women remarked that he was mostly
head and legs. It was as though a surgeon had sawn him
in two across the stomach, cut off a big slice from the
bottom half, and fastened the noble Roman together
«fain. As Othello, his boots were so bogus that he had
the effect of walking on stilts. His Hamlet irritated with
its stodginess. Physically, as artistically, he was always
a short man trying to bluff the public into believing him
great. With the exception of Miss Jeffries, every mem-
| 3 ® r . his co - was lacking in height and presence, and
their duty as a rule was to appear unimportant in the
background whilst Barrett made the most of himself in
the centre of the stage.
“Ancient”: As illustrating the force of habit, this
memory comes in. G. V. Brooke, when acting, used to
stimulate with brandy. His first wife used to stand in
the wings ready with bottle and glass. Gave him
homeopathic nips, which served as well as bumpers.
When she left him his nigger valet used to administer the
poison. He served out conscientious doses. Result very
often that Othello was as tight as Cassio, and the growth
of a, habit that fed upon itself. In the ecstacy of his
acting water would have served the man as well as
brandy.
“ O P.” : Remember, when a kiddy, seeing Helen
hancit as Desdemona to Phelps’s Othello. The impreg-
M '?, n Inow recall is that she was singularly free from the
stilted and conventional style of acting at that time in
vogue. But don't we, who howl at the artificial, some-
times knock our heads against the other extreme ? Is
mere not stich a mistake as “ artificial naturalness ? ”
All ai t is exaggeration, but it seems to me, an ancient,
that much of modern acting is an exaggeration of re-
straint, and, therefore, without salt and flavorless. Be-
lieve lne, there is as much cant in restrained passion as
torced passion. As things go now, if an actor depicted
the rage of the man in the street, he would be dubbed
emotional and a ranter 1 Why is it that we should now
demand upon the stage a repose of manner and action
which we do not observe in our daily lives?
“S.R.” : “ N." accounts for actors taking up the
religious biz by the statement that they are seriously im-
pelled thereto. Rats! I’ve seen as much of munimers
as most. Have, now and then, and here and there, been
one myself. And 3 say that when you hear of an actor
groing' in for the pulpit line, you may be sure there is
Coin in it. The elocutionary effects which are stale on
the stage, make’em sit up in the Lord’s house; where,
as a rule, the anointed are such dismal frosts. It is a
meie question of church and stage, and a revelation of
the circumstance that, to be successful, they must be
run on similar lines.

lecturing” once in Melb., his announced chairman did
not turn up, and an old pal of Barry’s volunteered to act.
Unfortunately, however, he wasn’t in temperance .prize-
taking condition, and, though he held out manfully for
some time, he at last interrupted the captain in full
career with, “ Barry, old man, 1 think it’s time we had a
drink. The suggestion hit the audience straight, and an
adjournment en masse followed.

: When knocking about Maoriland (Mahurangi
district) just before the war, came upon a lady milking
cows. Struck me that there was something stagey about
her appearance and attitude. Wore bracelets. Hair
streamed, like Ophelia’s, down her lily-white shoulders.
An ancient dressing-gown trailed in the straw. When
she saw me, she sprang to her feet. I bowed, and inti-
mated that I desired refreshment, for which I was willing
to pay “Pay!” she exclaimed with staccato voice
» u ol hospitality. Never 1 Stranger, follow me.”
And she hitched her dressing-gown over her arm and
stalked with tragedy-step towards the house. We entered.
Barry ! ” she cried. “ Here ! ” returned a hollow voice,
from an inner room. “A stranger, Barry,” cried the
lady, who shelter seeks, and food.” “ What’s he like ?”
growled the voice. “ H’in 1 ” and she looked me over
‘ A goodly guest, my Barry.” As she spoke, there saun-
’■'e,*£c* i ; o, V the.room within a tall, clean-shaven man, with
ruffled hair, and clad in d*n£y shirt and trousers. I
recognised him instantly. It was Barry, an American
actor, who came over from California with Stark, the
tragedian, and who was not appreciated. So he had
bought a farm in Maoriland wilds, and there I met him.
Stayed with him a week, and left under protest. Next
time I saw him at Newcastle, where he gave a recital, and
got a vessel for 'Frisco. “ Australia is too young, my
boy,” said he ; “ Art loves a ripe community.'’

“ Old Jack ” : When things are fixed up satisfactorily in
“ The Liars,” the bad-tempered husband of the play says
to his skittish wife, “ I know I have been harsh in the
past, but in the future I will do all I can to make your
life attractive. Come and have supper at the Savoy.” If
I were to pitch that slab of dust-cart bathos at the But-
LRTiN as part of a story, what show would that yarn have
of escaping the VV.P.B ? But the fact is that the man
who nowadays wants to write a popular novel or play
must steep himself to the lips in the slough of common-
place. Now I’ll go home.

HOT WEATHER DRINKS.
Man’s thirst in days of heat and toil
Is boundless as the sea ;
But once the BILLY ’gins to boil,
The fragrant BILLY TBA

Oranuma and Fodah Porridge Meals.
Kiippers Elberfeld Lager.
During the month of last Sept, the New
South Wales Lager Bier Brewing Co. Ltd.
supplied the German warship Falke with 6000
quart bottles of Sydney Lager Bier for use on the
voyage to Samoa and Apia. During the vessel’s
stay in our harbor 600 gallons of bulk Sydney
Lager were supplied her. Another tribute to the
excellence of the local lager conies from the Ger-
man warship Buzzard, which ordered 5500 quart
bottles of Sydney Lager for the return voyage to
Kiel. During this vessel’s stay with us she
received 50 gallons of Sydney bulk Lager daily.
Both ships’ crews cannot speak too highly of the
excellence of Sydney Lager. As the firemen,
officers and crews come from the country that has
always been famous for lager production, it is
clear that the Sydney Lager Bier Brewing Co.
deserves all that has been said by every con-
noisseur in favor of its lager.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Seventeen N.S. W. military officers receive
over >£soo per annum. One expensive major
commands 25 heroic troops !
“ Vic.”: Our surviving ex-Premiers seem,
except Service, to drop into insignificance—
balloons with the gas out, lying flat on the ground
• O’Loghlen, Munro, Gillies, Shiels, Berry.
The N.S.W. Parliamentary sitting of 57
hours 55min., last week, was the record for the
province. The next best was the hours’ sit-
ting 12 years ago, when the Jennings-Dibbs
Ministry drove its ad valorem duties through in
the face of the great Parkes stonewall.
Postmaster Duffy, of Victoria, is of use
once or twice a year, anyhow. He is reputed to
be the composer of the Governor’s speech at the
opening and closing of Parliament, and his knack
of looking at the bright side of things and not
committing himself comes in useful there.
“ N.” : Premier Turner never made a mis-
take in polities, except when he dragged David
Syme and the Macedon selection on to the floor
of the House. It was hypocritical. He pretended
he wanted to vindicate the character of Land
Minister Grant, the long-forgotten, whereas the
eye of everyone was on David Syme. Nobody
cared a straw for Grant.
Art item from recent debate in N.S.W.
Assembly:—
Mr. Crick said that an oil-painting, which cost this pro-
vince either 250 or 450 guineas, was sent to the Adelaide
Exhibition. It was sold as an “ enlarged oleograph ” for
£l2 10s. (Laughter.) An Hon. Member : That’s all it is
worth. Mr. Crick : It is undoubtedly a very fine paint-
ing. Mr. Haynes : Where is it ? Mr. Crick : It is hanging
in the hall of my house. (Laughter.)
Hobart Clipper indulges in some plain
speaking :
It is notorious that Cabinet Ministers, leaders of the
Opposition, and members of Parliament have often em-
ployed election agents to facilitate the judicious trading
in votes, thus ensuring the return of a boodle gang to
power ; and, in Hobart, gangs and individuals make large
sums of money as electioneering experts. Low scoun-
drels, wife-beaters, brothel bullies, pothouse bummers,
blacklegs who have been warned off the racecurses, and
confidence men, enjoy powerful political patronage, and
are found useful as election touts.
About the most sensational part of the
great Westralian Post Office robbery was the fact
that the officials, when they went to lunch on pay-
day, were reduced to the expedient of hiding the
unpaid salary money,in the battery cupboard and
other weird places. Apparently the Porrest crowd
is so short of cash that it cannot afford safes for
its metropolitan establishments. Imagine having
to leave >£9oo hidden in a battery cupboard with
only one little Yale lock between it and the great
hungry world outside. Also, imagine anyone
risking it. The average person would have stood
there with his hair bristling till it was all paid
away, if he had to camp in the office and eat his
food off the point of his revolver.
By the Marine Act, 1897, to which the
Royal assent has just been given, Victoria brings
herself into line with British shipping legisla-
tion and consolidation on the following subjects :
Apprenticeship, licenses to supply seamen, en-
gagement, rating and discharge of seamen, wages
questions, power of court to rescind contracts,
property of deceased seamen, leaving seamen
abroad, distressed seamen, provisions, health,
and accommodation ; facilities for making com-
plaints, protection of seamen from imposition,
discipline, official logs, and mercantile marine
officers. This Act also makes the master of a ship
who arrives in Victoria with a submerged load-
line liable to =£loo penalty. There has been a
dangerous doubt on this subject since the repeal
of the old Merchant Shipping Act in 1894.
The N.S.W. Council, on the motion of
Dr. MacLaurin, made some much-needed repairs
in the supremely idiotic first clause of Premier
Reid’s Land and Income-Tax Amendment Bill.
As the Assembly passed it, if a man exported
£>50,000 worth of wool, or ore, or other goods, it
was to be taken as all clear profit, whether he
made any profit out of it or not, and income-tax
was to be chargeable on the whole lot. The
Council altered it to read that tax should be pay-
able only on profits derived from the export. The
Assembly and the Government actually failed to
see that (to take an easy example) the gold or ore a
mining co. exports isn’t all profit, because it has
to pay for wages, fuel, machinery, &c., out of the
proceeds. Think of the intelligence of a Legisla-
tive Chamber which wasn’t aware of a small
thing like that!!
The Protectionist parry in N.S.W. As-
sembly made not one serious protest against Pre-
mier Reid’s outrageous loan proposals for the ex-
penditure of over =£2,000,000 of borrowed money
in one year—in which the law-costs of the
M'Sharry case are charged as a “ permanent and
reproductive work ; ” also the “ landing of silt ”
and things of that kind. Apparently the only
chance of honest finance in Cornstalk Land lies in
the establishment of a representative Chamber
elected by the whole nation as one constituency.
The man who was elected by the nation might
oppose a loan on the ground that his constituency
—the nation—had to pay the interest. The man
who represents Smith’s Curse dare not —because
the Smith’s Curse wharf is down among the loan
proposals, and Smith’s Curse wants its wharf, and
his only hope of continued political existence lies
in pleasing Smith’s Curse.
Ten European lepers are
are rusticated on Stradbroke Is-
land (Q.). One female leper is
located at Dunwich. There are
13 alien lepers at Friday Island
tenS.S. Islanders, two aborigi-
nals, one Cingalee. “ There are
no Chinese lepers in Queens-
land,” say the Government apolo-
gists.. No— there are none in the
lazarettes. How many prowl
about unknown to the authorities
would be difficult to determine.
Meantime, the great fact is to be
noted that the disease flourishes
principally among those races
that hang about Chinese shops
in the Q. sugar towns.
The report of the British
Board of Trade of 1849, on which
the Constitution of N.S.W. was
based, recommended one tariff
for all Australia, however many
provinces it ni '.it afterwards
be divided into. To frame this
tariff, the Board proposed that
the Imperial Govt, should, in
the first instance, compile a list
of items, and that N.S.W. and
the provinces to arise afterwards
should create a General Assem-
bly to revise the list from time
to time. The General Assem-
bly was to be elected by the Leg-
islatures of the various provinces.
This and numerous other sug-
gestions and recommendations
of the Imperial authorities about
the same time show how clearly
the trained intellects of half-a-
century ago saw the necessity of
Australia being either a Union
or a Federation. It would have
been much better had Aus-
tralians seen it also.
Downer’s retirement from
the leadership (on sufferance) of
S.A. Opposition, if it comes off,
may make room for the rising
hope of the party, young John
Darling, a wealthy wheat mer-
chant, clever, ambitious and
otherwise famous as being the brother of cricketer
Joe. Pat Glynn, Downer’s rival of last June, is
now regarded as an impossible leader.
J. C. Campbell, M.L.C., is now sorry he
said those wild and untruthful things about the
Victorian woman suffragists, whom he described
as ladies of a class that stays out late at night.
Snowden, who sits beside him in the Patman’s
chamber, has announced publicly that Campbell
admits he “ went too far.” Campbell hasn’t the
manliness to offer his personal apology.
A bitter wail comes down from Thursday
Island. The whole of the slips or docks where
boats are built are owned by Japs. New boats are
being built despite the fact that the oyster-beds
are becoming impoverished. In 1890 there were 22
Japanese on the island ; in 1892, 32 ; in 1897 the
brown heathen number 331; and, at the present
time, 619. Other aliens, 575 ; total white popula-
tion, 608. . . . And the only restrictive legislation
likely to be carried is a measure to prevent Japan-
ese ownership of pearling-boats. It’s only alien
capitalism that Northern legislators object to.
The three “ commercial agents ” who have
lately been visiting the beauty-spots of Europe
and America in the alleged interests of Queens-
land trade, have already cost Piebaldland the
handsome sum of .£3779'65. sd. These three
gentlemen departed without any definite instruc-
tions, travelled promiscuously, and furnished no
definite reports of their doings. One is a
Councillor and a German ; his age is about 90.
Another was for a quarter-century clerk in the
Police Dept., and the
other was a Bris-
bane commis-
sion-agent.
AS OTHERS SEE US .
"Well, by cripes, Maria, that’s the queerest turn-
out I ever see."
As long as the N.S.W. Sand-Shifting
Ministry kept its relief works at an impossible
distance they were merely a name to the average
citizen. Scrub-cutting on the Bogan, sand-digging
at Kensington, forest-thinning at Narandera, were
too far off to worry about. But the spectacle of
some 2000 men working for charity under his very
nose has come as a rude shock to the average Syd-
neyite. Should the Rail-Painting Government
succeed in polling every vote of those so em-
ployed, it will not compensate for the moral shock
Sydney got this Xmas.
Melbourne City Council has placed its
printing contract h>r the next two years with a
non-union firm, to the evident satisfaction of ex-
Mayor Snowden. “If we were to go back on this
contract,” said Snowden, “we should be pledging
ourselves to accept no tender by any firm unless it
belonged to a union, and that would be an un-
righteous and unholy principle.” Snowden has
always been a warm defender of sweaters’ “ rights.”
He is one of those bloated capitalists who never
feel truly happy except in the summer-time, when
thev can sweat themselves.

Two hard-fighting members of N.S.W.
Opposition walk down to the House through Hyde
Park and they often rest by the way on one
special seat and have a smoke. Possibly Premier
Reid noticed this habit ; anyhow, that seat was
the very first painted by Reid’s “Fat” Xmas
gang, and after the aforesaid members had flopped
into it they had to engage a messenger to scrub
them with turpentine and other smellful com-
pounds, and the language which arose in the
Opposition room when they walked in nearly set
them in a blaze. It took all Barton’s influence to
prevent them moving the adjournment of the
House, so that the state of the nether portion of
their raiment might be inquired into.
The M.L. gumfields are a menagerie of
curious animals. All the cranks, ne'er-do-weels,
and drunks of the two islands gravitate there
sooner or later. Ex-doctors, lawyers, dudes,
merchants, broken-down artizans, and the miscel-
laneous dead-beats, walk through these undulating
stretches of stunted tea-tree to their graves, pay-
ing their way with the precarious gum as they go,
and only dependent on charity for their funerals.
M.L. had a grand asset in its gum deposits. If
the Government.had retained all the gum lands in
its own hands it would have had a means of sup-
plying charitable aid in exchange for unskilled
labor that would have been invaluable. The sale
of so much of the gum country was a grievous
mistake.
