Dear Bulletin. —There may be no truth
in Lormer’s story that Judge Williams lost his
chance of getting appointed to the Vic. Chief-
Justiceship in consequence of his wife having said
she would exclude certain undesirable persons from
the Government House crowd if her husband be-
came Acting-Governor. There is probably less
truth, however, in the report that Judge Wil-
liams said his wife wouldn’t have made such a
“snobbish” remark. Why “ snobbish ” ? The
suggestion in the original yarn is that Mrs. Jus-
tice Williams had made up her mind to give the
cold shoulder to certain malodorous bounders of
the boom persuasion, whom Lord Brassey snubbed,
but who have been cordially treated by Waxel-
lency Madden in his periods of vice-regal distinc-
tion. The Melbourne belief has always been that
Madden got the billet over Williams’s head be-
cause he was the more gaudy and less inconveni-
ently particular man of the pair. The Govern-
ment'at the time wanted a gaudy and not too un-
yielding man to put a pleasant face on everything
and “ restore confidence. J. Pen.
“ Hermes ” on a large subject:—
For pure unmitigated bill.vgoatishness the cry that to
make the N.S.W. Land-Tax Declaratory Bill retrospective
is “ un-English,” takes a lot of beating:. Presumably
“ un-English ” is used for ungenerous, low-minded, and
several other nasty things. What about the un-English-
ness of wriggling out of paying a tax through a technical
crack in the Bill ? People who try that little game on
Bull generally get caught in the long run, for the English
rule is that if an Act is passed and fails to accomplish it*
purpose through being badly drawn, and has to be
amended, the amending Act, even if it takes 10 years to
get through, is retrospective to the hour the original Act
crystalised the intention of the legislature into law.
Which is only common-sense. The notion that the Eng-
lish are more generous-which is the same thing as being
more foolish—in these little matters is a burst-up fetish.
Another myth that wants badly to be smashed up is that
the British are invariably more generous than other
people to a beaten enemy. All depends who the enemy
is. If he is a more or less humble individual into whose
back-yard Bull has burst. With great success Bull is some-
times generous ;he can afford to he. But if the enemy
is an irreverent party who has irrupted into Bull’s pad-
dock, he gets no generosity worth having. “ What
do you offer Harold of Norway?” asked Tos-
tig. “ Seven foot of English soil for a grave,”
grimly replied Harold of England. And ’twas all he got.
They howled to Dauphin Louis to come over and smash
King John. The finger of God touched John, and the
English turned on Louis and made the largest holocaust
of his army they knew how. Whi n the Spanish invaded
Ireland, Elizabeth's troops surrounded them in Port
Deloro. To avoid being stormed, the Spanish surren-
dered unconditionally—and Italeigh'killed every man of
them. When Monmonth was smashed at Sedgemoor,
Judge Jeffreys swept the rebellions counties like a wave.
When the Young Pretender was flattened out in Scotland
tire same old generosity got, the upper hand, and set to
work to exterminate the disaffected, root and branch ;
and it is doubtful if the “ Bloody Assize ” of Jeffreys was
in the same street with the horrible butchery that set the
Guelphs firmly on the throne. And the army that next
invades Britain will have to fight for its life, for, if it goes
under, it will find British generosity just about as terrible
as Death. There is a tendency now to write books about
the great war that is to come—the great universal burst-
up or Armageddon ; and some of these books deal with
tlie invasion ot England by a foreign enemy. England is
always on top in the last, chapter, and the blast of death
that Joshua blew in Palestine is nothing, as a rule, to
that last chapter. Bull is as generous as anybody else,
and that is about all. But it is 600 years since a French
king was crowned in London and n French army raised
Cain by the Thames, and, through long immunity, the
Briton has eorne to regard his soil as more sacred than
the average. If it were invaded, he would probably con-
sider the episode not as the fortune of war, but as a
crime that called for the suspension of ordinary laws and
regulations. So probably would any other community in
the same conditions.
The secret history of the effuses chat
brought about Julius Vogel’s second lease of
power in M.L. politics lias never been written.
Here is a contribution thereto by one who was
behind the scenes. Having lost the Agent-
Generalship, Vogel arrived in Wellington in 1884,
almost unheralded. He was apparently without
a political feather to fly with, hard-up, and so
crippled with gout that he could not leave his
chair. Maoriland was in a slough of depression,
and the Public Works policy of the “Hebraic
financier ” was execrated from one end of the
country to the other as the root of the evil. The
Wellington papers ignored him, and the political
leaders kept aloof. Atkinson, Premier of the
“Continuous Ministry,” his old-time lieutenant,
whatever his private inclinations may have been,
foresaw, or thought he foresaw, that, with a
general election approaching and the star of Stout
beaming with dangerous luminosity, it would be
fatal to recognise Vogel as a politic al entity. But
on the very day of the dissole' : on of Parliament,
fortune thrust the chance of rehabilitation into
Vogel's hands. Two ga’lery reporters—one repre-
senting an Auckland and one a Dunedin paper—
finding time heavy on their hands determined to
pay their respects to Vogel. The' astute Julius
r eeived the pressmen with fl ittering cordiality,
and the, fruit of this casual visit was a four-column
interview, which was really a. Vogelian manifesto-
to the electors of Maor iland. In it, Vogel, with
consummate artifice, bid for support from every
quarter. He showed that the failure of his
policy was due to the fact that his successors
had not carried out the trunk railways in both
islands, but bad frittered away the loan money
in “spurs” to oblige political friends. A hint
about the Midland railway as a political possi-
bility made Canterbury and Westland reel with
joy. Otago Stout’s own country was pro-
pitiated by a proposal wild-cattish in the
extreme —to'divert the waters of the Molyneaux
to enable the rich “ gutter ” gold in the bed of the
stream to be worked As the glacier-fed Moly-
neaux discharges as much water as the Nile, the
paralysing magnitude of this proposal may be
imagined. The interview was reprinted every-
where, leaders were written about it, and minor
politicians made it a text for their orations. Pre-
sently, amidst the clash of parties shouting the
shibboleths of Stout and Atkinson, began to be
heard with ever-increasing distinctness the name
of Vogel. More than one seat was offered him,
and the party wire-pullers, much against their
will, were forced to recognise in the political dead-
beat of yesterday the kingpin of the situation.
Then it was given out that a rapprochement had
been brought about between Stout and Vogel, and
at the general election the verdict of the electors
was in favor of this hastily-made combination.
The meeting of the new Parliament saw the birth
of the Stout-Vogel Ministry, which held office for
12 days. Atkinson followed with seven days’
occupancy of office, when the Stout-Vogel Minis-
try again turned the tables, and held the benches
for three years.

Ba.vswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir. -For years past (about ten), I have been
trouble with “Acne” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene ”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “Cum Grano Hall's’' and was in-
clined not to use it; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now'entirely cured;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligmolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.
WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY
Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit dow'n to record pro-
gress with regard to my old enemy “ Eczema Capitas,”
from which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treat-
ment for some years under the prescriptions of Pro-
fessor M’Oall Anderson and other eminent derma-
tologists, with only partial and temporary relief.
When I met you I commenced somewhat sceptically
your ointment Ligmolliene, and after about two months’
treatment I may honestly say my enemy is defeated,
and lam perfectly well. I will only add that since I
commenced the treatment of myself I have prescribed it
to others, and am still doing so with brilliant results.
Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours sin-
cerely, TIIOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.ILC.S., L.

A week or two before the session closed
Vic. Assembly authorised “the expenditure of
.£5O for the purpose of the board appointed to
report upon the treatment of habitual drunkards.”
The Bulletin hopes that the board devoted the
money to a jovial treatment of drunks at Xmas.
One Futsch, a pious Roman Catholic capi-
talist, who expired in Melbourne recently, leaves
large sums of money to all the institutions con-
nected with the Faith tailing down to £lCft)—for
masses for the repose of his sowl. After this
comes a little unostentatious item of £50 —for the
Melbourne Hospital: £lOO for the soul of the
dead man, and £5O for the bodies of the living.
The N.S.W. Government promises to do
something next session with the great mass of
circulars and tracts and little church advertising-
sheets which go through the post at the tax-
payers’expense as “newspapers.” Alas, the Reid
Government is always going to do something next
session. Witness the Early Closing Bill, State
Land Bank, Arbitration and Conciliation Bill,
Navigation Bill, and every other democratic
measure of 1898. All waiting once more for
“ next session.”
What joy is there in any holiday festivity
—the hilarious picnic, the exhilarating social hop,
or even the gentle “ spoon” ’neath the umbrageous
eucalyptus—if you are not possessed of that
serene self-confidence which can only be bestowed
by good tailoring? If you would achieve this feel-
ing, and thus abstract the very best from every
joy that the New Year can offer, write for particu-
lars of stylish suits, head, nook, and foot-wear,
etc., to A. W. Feme and Co., London and Ameri-
can Supply Stores, Bourke-st., Melb. Prices
reasonable ; satisfaction immoderate.*
A Collingwood bootmaker, turned miracle
healer, has just been in trouble for obtaining
money under false pretences. Charles Bailey, dis-
covering that he was a competent medium,
“chucked” boot-clicking, and, placing himself
en rapport with the spook of a deceased doctor,
hung out his shingle, and undertook to cure sore
legs and visitations of Providence at cut rates.
He explained to his patients—who were frequent
—that he diagnosed cases in a trancs, having at
his command the spirit of the late lamented Dr.
Whitcombe. He didn’t have anyone to put him
into a trance ; he simply put himself into it, and
said, in a hollow voice, that it was a terribly bad
case, and charged 2s. od., which he collected in
his own person when he woke up. The advantage
of this system is that the live man gets the 2s. 6d.
and the spirit (who can’t be found) has all the re-
sponsibility. The disadvantage is that the spirit
flatly refuses to pay the £5 fine.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
South Australia’s unexpected victory over N.S.
Wales in Adelaide was earned mainly by C. Hill, Darling,
Jones, and Geo. Giffen. Although a failure with the bat,
Giffen’s bowling at a critical time, when N.S.W. looked
like winning, was splendid. The wicket was fiery and
uncertain, but the ex-champion was aided very little
thereby, and he never bowled better in his life. The best
innings of the match was Jones’ 82. It was a chanceless
display of determined hitting and safe cricket at a time
when the S.A. batting was commencing to collapse. The
popular Jonah has been fooling these three years ; he is a
batsman of the first water when he chooses to keep his
head.
Clem. Hill is the best batsman in Australia this
season—so far. He made his 73 and 109 without giving
a chance in either innings. Clem, is in wonderful form
and should be the batsman of the prospective Australian
Eleven. His batting this year is sound all round the
the wicket. And Clem, is only 22 next March ! Darling
has a high opinion of Noble’s bowling and admitted that
the curve ball which bowled him in the first innings
completely beat him.
Of the N.S.W. batting, Trumper’s innings of 69
was as pretty a display as the writer has seen this season,
and his chance of squeezing into the team for England
has considerably improved. Donnan’s 170 (not out) was
invaluable, but there was an utter absence of attractive-
ness. With all Donnan's big scores, he has few admirers,
and yet he is the hardest man in Australia to dispose of
just now.
It was whispered that if McKenzie, the S.A.
wicket-keeper, showed good form, he would have a big
chance for England, as Johns is not considered capable of
standing the hard work of the trip. But the S.A. man
absolutely failed, missing almost every opportunity that
came to him. And now he has been left out of the S.A.
team travelling east in favor of A. H. Jarvis.
The N.S. Wales players ascribe their narrow
defeat to Giffen’s splendid bowling. On the whole, the
best team lost. S.A. fielding waS again disgraceful :
catches were dropped by men like Hill, Reedman and
McKenzie in the same manner as in the match against
Victoria. But S.A. won the toss ; solid batting and bowl-
ing by one or two men, and a fair share of luck, did the
rest.
* * * *
The mterprovincial match N.S. Wales v. Vic-
toria started in Melbourne on Saturday last, on what
Charley Bannerman considered one of the worst wickets
he had ever seen. Victoria started batting, and ran up
189, of which VVorrall made 109. The N.S. Wales fielding
was execrable, VVorrall being missed by M’Kibbin, Coning-
ham, Pye and Mackenzie. N.S. Wales then went in, and
made 35 for the loss of eight wickets, Kelly (13) being the
only man to make double figures. On Monday, N.S.
Wales resumed, and ran up the score to 99—Mackenzie
23, Noble (not out) 34. The Victorians then commenced
their second innings, and very monotonous was the play
until Graham got going, as may be judged from the fact
that it took McLeod an hour and a-half to make his 18
runs. At the time of going to press, Victoria had finished
its second innings for 219, and N.S.W. wanted 310 to win.
* * * *
Jack Worrall, the Victorian skipper, who
|ast year scored 305 out of 181, repeated his performance
in the first innings of this year’s Victoria-N.S. W. match
by amassing 109 on top of his 104 last week against S.A.
These two performances on sticky wickets should make
the nuggety Jack an absolute certainty for the Tenth
Australian Eleven. Worrall can also bowl at a pinch,
occasionally getting in an unanswerable one which he
terms a “Jenny Hayser.’’
* * * •
The cricket-team for ’Ome is now the burning
topic, and some most ridiculous claims are put for-
ward. When ’tis all over, the following 13 are perhaps
as likely as any : N.S.W., Gregory, Iredale, Kelly, Howell,
Donnan ; S.A., Darling, Hill, Jones, Giffen ; Vic.,
Trumble, M’Leod, Worrall, Johns. If 14 are taken Noble
will likely be other man ; but, good man though he be
out here, he does not look like wearing out a tough Hing-
lish season.
* • * •
“ Podge ” : Correspondents of Sydney Tele-
graph are sending in their selections for “The
Best Australian Eleven.” Not one so far has
less than seven N.S. Wales players, the majority
tipping nine. As S.A. has defeated the mother
province, and Victoria, when this journal was going to
press, seemed likely to also defeat it, the tipsters will
probably have it brought home to them that N.S.W. can-
not boss all Australia any more in sport than in drafting
a Federation Bill.
* • • •
“Face”: Apparently no amount of proof can
knock it into the thick head of the average crieketer that
the only way to do anything on a wet wicket is by going
for the bowling and taking all risks. Worrall has done
this time after time and has almost always succeeded in
banging up a good score, and has at last banged himself
into a trip ’Ome. Still most of the other men continue to
potter about, and get out, and stay at home. No batsman
should be taken to England until he has learned the
right way to bat on a wet wicket.
* * * •
Randwick had a very small following on Satur-
day, and the few who braved the storm had also a bad
time of it financially. For the Hurdle Race the ex-
galloway The Nipper was made favorite, but was unable
to run better than second to Auditor, who started at 10
to 1. The scratchings were very heavy in the Villiers
Stakes, and when things settled down Carberra was made
favorite at sto 1. The Carbine mare failed to run into a
place, and Satan, at 14 to 1, won by half-a-length from
Howlong.
Arquebus was made favorite for the High-
weight Handicap, but could do no better than third;
Astronomer making up for past disappointments by win-
ning easily from Linda. Henry won the Trial Stakes
from Shaughraun ; and then the public thought it had a
real good thing in Beaulieu for the December Stakes, and
backed him down to 2 to 1 ; but it was not to be, as Low-
land Chief and Cornquist finished in front of him. But
the A. J.C. remained for backers to make a recovery, and
they maiie a favorite of the Rosehill winner, Auda. The
mare finished absolutely last, and Shaughraun, at 10 to 1,
won from Standard. The frame of mind in which punters
started for home may be gauged from the starting-price
of winners; 3t01,7t01, 5 to 1, two at 10 to 1, and 14 to 1.
Sara Fielder has a slippery youngster in the
December Stakes winner, Lowland Chief, a big chestnut
colt by Antwus-Legardo. On form, Beaulieu was in his
ritfht position as first favorite for the race, but he shaped
badly on the wet course and Lowland Chief won with a
lot in hand, Fielder has been unlucky of late. The big
Carbine colt, “ Sensation,” for which he save 600 guineas
as a yearling, has been a rank failure and has never won
a race, whilst his well-bred two-year-olds appear to be
just striking form after an unsuccessful trip to Melb.
Two of Sam’s old charges, Vanitas and Parthenopteus, are
now champions in India, whilst Amiable, reckoned by
Sam the best mare he has ever owned, should soon be
heard of from 'Ome.
* * * *
Vivian was a strong course tip for the Villiers,
it being whispered that he had been sold for England and
was running his last race for Earnshaw. When Stand-off
and Tubal Cain were scratched a lot of money went on
him, and with Billy Delaney up he ran a good race, but
wasn’t quite good enough on the day.
* * • •
Backers had a better time on the second day of
the A.J.C. meeting, three favorites getting home, al-
though the public were woefully at fault in the Summer
Cup, the places being filled by Strathray, Merloolas and
Prince Carbine, not one of whom was mentioned in the
betting. The day began badly for backers when Helms-
man at 14 to 1 carried off the Christmas Handicap; but
things looked better when two favorites came in—Fulmi-
nate, who won the Nursery Handicap; and Spieler, the
Steeplechase. Victoria, unthought of, won the Maiden
Handicap, and then Whapawatea, the public fancy, sent
the crowd home in good temper by an easy win of the
Welter Handicap.
• • * *
Tatt.’s Club meeting begins at Rand wick,
N.S.W., to-day (Sat.) when, given Hue weather, it should
even surpass the A.J.C. meeting just held. The Hurdles
look well for Playboy or Lord Gresford, whilst Lady
Hampden Stakes may fall to Marvie or Colleen. The
time-honored Carrington is sure to bring out a big field
of fleet ’uns, as there is sweep-money afloat. Djin-Djin,
Telegraph, Monrefield and Burleigh seem a likely quar-
tette, and of these Telegraph will perhaps turn out the
absolute winner. On Monday Tatt.’s Cup is the principal
event, and this looks well for Clarion or his stable com-
panion, Philip. Admonition should win a race during
the meeting, as she is very well.
• * * •
The Chow jockey is coming ahead rapidly. The
other week, this paper recorded the successes of a Mon-
golian horseman at Omeo, Gippsland ; and now Ah Sam,
of Cobungra (Vic.) gets two wins and four places out of
six mounts in one day. The Chow is a good sport, too,
but puts no trust in bookies—he goes in heavily for totes
and sweeps.
• • * *
“E.F.” : Your correspondent, “Victor,” doesn’t
understand why I call him “artless.” Let me explain.
Previous to the Melb. Cup. he wrote “ A wary backer
of any horse for a big event generally wishes to be satisfied
on the following points—are owner, trainer, and jock
right ?" I remarked that an owner’s rightness or wrong-
ness is of little importance as long as the other two parties
go straight, and then the artless one rose to “explain”
that the owner has the power to scratch a horse, which
brings us back to the original paragraph. “ Victor ”
tipped Carbinier for Melb. Cup, as being the good horse
of a right owner, and he was artless because it didn’t
occur to him that every sanguine owner of an outsider
(Carbinier would have been a 30-to-l chance at the post
if started, but he didn’t start) is honest in so far that he
couldn’t possibly make more than £lOO or so by “ milk-
ing” his horse, whereas he could getsome thousands from
the V.R.C. for winning the race. When “ Victor ” spoke of
the necessity for wariness in backing a horse for Melb.
Cup he was talking solemn nonsense. He may be well
up in turf history. He properly corrected me re the
Christian name (John, not Dick) of the Porter who ordered
the late Fred Gretton’s horses out of his stable. All the
same, “ Victor ” can have no practical knowledge of the
racing business.
* * * *
It isn’t every man who can boast of having a
Bill introduced into Parliament specially to “deal with ”
himself; consequently Wren, of Collingwood tote, since
the last night of Vic. session wears his tongue in his
cheek and sports a superior smile. On the second read-
ing, recently, Attorney-General Isaacs’ “ Bill for the
better Suppression of tote-man Wren” was thrown out
by one vote. Several nasty remarks were passed about
the incapacity of the detectives, and many generous
offers of help were made if the Government were in
earnest in desiring to suppress betting—to begin with
Tatt’s and the Victorian Club. But the excessive power
given by the proposed measure to the police to enter and
search adjacent houses was more than the Assembly could
stomach, and the Vic. police authorities will have to sup-
press Wren’s with the means at their present disposal or
let it rip. In spite of their axes and crowbars, they
haven’t made any very earnest attempt, so far.
• * * *
When Dr. Slate and co. talked of backing Fritz
against any trotter or pacer in the world they took on a
big contract. The pacer, Star Pointer, recently paced an
exhibition mile in Jsec. under 2min.
* * * »
A metropolitan sly-totalisator shop, run by a
humorist at a profit of perhaps £2OOO a year, purports to
be a club for the study of Federation. It has 2500
members at ss. a year, and the authorities on the Federal
problem filed by the club consist of three sporting papers.
The only other literature in the place consists of lists of
horses in the big current and other races, scratching-
lists, &c. There is no sign of any speeches bv Barton or
Reid or Kingston; if they do exist, the secretary evi-
dently files them carefully away to keep them from get-
ting dusty. A strange feature of the club is the appalling
interest members take in the Federal problem on race-
days ; on Melbourne Cup day, for instance, over 2000
members from time to time crowded into the club-room,
a small, dingy, cheerless place with a broken down table,
one chair, one stool, and dirty walls. One of the numer-
ous parsons in that holy city was recently shown over the
club by the cute proprietor. The cleric,’who said he was
an enthusiast on Federal questions, intimated his inten-
tion of becoming a member. He said he was greatly in-
fluenced in his decision by a placard which is prominent
on the wall—“ betting strictly prohibited.”
» * * *
The Auckland magistrate has decided* like
various other persons of deficient intellect, that the il-
legal bookmaker can’t be got at because of the same old
hitch in the law. Maoriland’s statute, like many others,
prohibits the person with the bag from using any
place for the purpose of his illegal calling. A bookie was
found so using Ellerslie racecourse, but the law decided
that it wasn’t sure Ellerslie racecourse was a place “ with-
in the meaning of the Act." In the magistrate's opinion
defendant “ had not occupied a‘place’ for betting, be-
cause the area outside the grand stand at Ellerslie is not
enclosed within any reasonable meaning of the word, and
also the actual spot occupied by bookie was itself in no
way circumscribed or deiined. The defendant possessed
no desk, box, stool or umbrella to determine his position
or to indicate his profession. He certainly invited custom
and carried a bag, but these he declined to construe to
mean the employment of a ‘place.'” Tins Bulletin
would like to ask this magistrate and a lot more like him
if Maoriland is a “place.” Every sane human being
regards it as such, though it isn't enclosed and there was
no desk or umbrella in the whole two islands until quite
recently. And defendant was using Maoriland as a place
wherein to bawl his discordant odds, and was desecrating
the green archipelago with his uncomely bag. If Maori-
laud isn’t a place, then what is it ?
• * * *
Strong man Sampson is having a bad time in
these provinces. Following upon the Sydney unpleasant-
ness over the alleged coin breaking, “ Professor ” Miller
has written to the Melbourne press, saying that Sampson
should have his weights tested by a committee, as used
to be done with Miller’s own weights when he was per-
forming in the strong-man business. Sampson’s answer
is a good deal mixed. He asks “ Does a boxer give away
the secret of his knock-out blow ?” Of course, he doesn’t,
only the public didn't know there was any secretin the
weight-lifting business. Sampson also says that he hasn’t
time to attend to all the tests proposed to him, and t hat
his weights have been weighed so often that he is sick of
having them weighed. But he offers to let Miller call in
ai, 3’ morning and test his weights—which isn’t exactly
the same thing. The one idea is to see the weights tested
on the spot, and have their enormous avoirdupois guaran-
teed, and then see Sampson elevate these same weights.
The other is to have Miller state that he tesied certain
weights in the morning, and they were all right, and
that he supposes these are the same ones.
• * * *
“ Magnus ”: Sampson’s strength-feats are partly
genuine and partly bogus. Take the weight-lifting—
with no assurance of the weights. Take the chain-test,
and note that he always supplies his own chains. Any-
one who has anything to do with iron or steel knows
that it can be tempered so as to stand a slow strain when
it won’t stand a quick jerk— note Sampson’s stipulation
that the horses shall pull steadily, while he snaps sud-
denly. It s a matter of the way tne atoms of the metal
cohere. Some steels fracture like glass in a freezing
temperature. The platform chain - test is similarly
worthless. Who knows where the chains come from,
and how they’re made ? A chain - breaker may
have specially-forged chains, with one link flawed
in a definite way. Get the strain on the flaw in the
manner known to the manufacturer and the strong-man,
and it snaps easily, though a horse mightn’t break it at a
straight pull. Every audience ought to bring its own
chains, and test them beforehand link by link. Of
course, Sampson’s strong ; but he isn’t necessarily as
strong as he would like people to believe. Ask Sandovv.
* * * *
Writer was at the winning post at the finish of
100yds. handicap at a recent Sydney sports meeting, and
it was won, in the opinion of all hands by (say) Smith,
with (say) Jones, the scratch man and little'tin-god of the
amateur authorities, a close third. The judges came
together and Jones picked up his coat, bustled up near
them saying, “ Nothing but first place will suit me,” and
walked away off the track. Then the judges had a heated
discussion, and finally awarded the race to Jones, and
placed Smith, the absolute winner, third. And this hap-
pened among the lily-white amateurs who race only for
“ pots.”

Dear B. Another coincidence. On the occasion
when Peter Kemp was defeated for the sculling cham-
pionship by Searle, the Little Nell steam launch foun-
deied through contact with the Undine,one man drowned.
Now the Little Nell founders and drowns the ex-cham-
pion’s father. Seems as if the “Nell” was a kind of
family banshee and always foundered when there was
disaster in the Kemp family.--N.J.

On Saturday last, Miss Irene Pritchard put up
a world’s record when she sailed the 8-footer Zephyr to
victory in Port Jackson in a gale of wind. The Zephyr,
built by Miss Pritchard’s father, is Bft. long, Bft. beam’
and 7ft. across the tuck; and her racing sail is 19ft. on
the boom.

The fight for the featherweight chain pi' nship
of N.S.YV., at the Golden Gate Athletic Club (S-. ,ey) on
Christmas Eve, resulted in a further endorsement of “The
Kaffirs superiority over Tom Hope, and of Tub Bulle-
tin s tip of two months hack, that Hope had a lot to learn
before he coiild have a chance with the “ Rocks ” cham-
pion, Tweedie. The contest lasted nine rounds, but was
actually over in the eighth, when “ The Kaffir” landed on
the vulnerable “point” with a heavily-charged right
swing, and laid his man out in great style. Clinches
which gave the referee a deal of trouble, and savage
exchanges that caused the 600 onlookers to get up
on their hind legs and yell themselves hoarse, were in
evidence from beginning to end; Hope doing the greater
part of the leading, but the black getting in the most
effective work. “The Kaffir” weighed Sst. 41bs. and his
opponent Bst. 01bs.

Lucifer and Dunn, not being satisfied with their
match (or, rather, Lucifer not being satisfied with his
share of the match) at Sydney Lyceum, wrestle in three
styles at Sydney Opera House on Friday (December 30)
for £5O. Also, Dillon and Billy McKell will bang each
other for four rounds.
* * • *
“Yellow Jimmy,” otherwise James Phoenix,
an old-time pugilist of note, shuffled off at West Mait-
land on Tuesday last week, full of years if not of honors.
He was a conspicuous figure in the fifties and sixties, and
fought many bare knuckle battles in those stirring times.
One of Jimmy’s best campaigns was that with the Parra-
matta butcher, “Stonewall ” Jackson, at Rooty Hill, in
|5B The half-caste gave away a big lump in weight and
inches in height, but won after a long struggle—mainly
through his slippery tactics. Dick Hunt, one of Jimmy’s
old antagonists, is still alive. Phoenix, the crack Mait-
land cross-country jockey, is a son of “ Yellow Jimmy.”
* * * *
How is this for the “long arm of coincidence ” ?
A. A. Middleton fell in the 25-mile race at the late Austral
meeting and dislocated his collar-bone from the scapula,
a most uncommon form of dislocation of that particular
bone. A. 0. Middleton some time back came to grief in
the 25-mile road championship and disrated his collar-
bone in exactly the same fashion. Two two 25-
mile races, two collar-bones dislocated, and both in the
same unusual fashion.

Government Notices.
Public Service Board,
50 Young-street, Sydney,
17th December, 1898.
A COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION of CANDIDATES for
JUNIOR CLERKSHIPS in the PUBLIC SERVICE
of the COLONY will be held in Sydney, and at various
Country centres, which will he fixed as far as possible to
suit the convenience of candidates, on the 20th MARCH
next and four following days. The estimated number of
appointments to be made is 50. Candidates must be not
less than 16 nor more than 21 years of age on the first day
of examination, of good character, and in sound health.
Persons already employed in the General Division of the
Public Service will be admitted to the examination, pro-
vided their age does not exceed thirty years. The sub-
jects in which Candidates will be examined are Hand-
writing, English (Dictation, Composition, Precis writing,
Parsing, Analysis, and derivation of words), Arithmetic
(full course), Geometry (Euclid, first Book), Geography
(outlines of physical and political geography, and the
geography of the Australasian Colonies in moderate
detail), English History from the Middle of t.he Eighteenth
Century to the present time, especially with reference to
the Colonies and Dependencies. Candidates will also be
allowed to take any two, but not more than two, of the
following subjects Mathematics, English, a prescribed
Author, viz.: Shakespeare’s “Richard III.,” Latin, French,
German, Elementary Chemistry, and Elementary Geology.
Applications to attend the examination must be made
on a form for the purpose, which can be obtained at
the office of the Board, and must reach the under-
signed not later than the Ist March. Candidates
will be required to pay an examination-fee of 10s.,
which must be lodged with a Clerk of Petty Sessions or
the Treasury, and the receipt therefor forwarded to the
Secretary to the Board with the form of application. A
list of registered candidates with centres of examination
will be published in the Government Gazette, after the
time for the receipt of applications has expired, and can-
didates will also be advised direct of the place at which
they will be examined. Candidates may be examined at
any centre (outside the Sydney radius) where persons
approved by the Board of Examiners are willing to super-
intend the examination, and a form containing the signa-
tures of such persons should be forwarded to the Secretary
of the Board of Examiners not later than Ist March, 1599.
Forms for the purpose may be obtained from the Secre-
tary of the Board of Examiners, 7, Richmond-terrace,
Domain, Sydney. The requisite stationery will be sup-
plied by ttie Public Service Board. The Regulations and
copies of the papers set at the last examination can be
obtained at the Government Printing Office and the prin-
cipal booksellers.
By order of the Board.
9a 50 J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

Dear Bulletin. — Never realised the all-
pervading influence of “ vested interests ” till, at
the Federal election, I asked a man in our street
if he was for Federation, and he replied, “No
fear ! ’twouldn’t suit me ! There’s an old girl—a
squatter's widow, with plenty of coin—comes down
from Riverina every three months to have a few
weeks in Sydney, and she pays me 30s. a week to
cart her round the pleasure-resorts, an’ all that
sorter thing. Well, if this blanky Federation
comes off, her wool ’ll go to Melbourne, an’ she ’ll
follow it, an’ some Victorian galoot ’ll collar that
30s. a-week ! ” — Hermes.

Dear Bulletin. —You say (Nov. 19) re
“ O.L’s ” suggestion that Albany would do for
the Federal capital. “Not near enough to Aus-
tralia,” I assume that you have no other objection
to raise to Albany as the Federal capital. Recently
you urged that capital must have a cool climate.
Well, Albany has the coolest summer climate in
Australia. Ordinary summer weather keeps in
the seventies, while it is a hot day if 80 is passed.
The record is 99, recorded for two hours in the
great heat-wave week. Albany never has hot
winds. Site for great city admirable—miles of
harbor frontage, hills, valleys, rivers. Grass
green all the year round. Immense areas of
garden lands in the district —three crops potatoes
every year. Rainfall average about 34in. Death-
rate low. Town clean and smart-looking. The
place now regarded as one of the choicest health-
resorts on continent. It would be hard to find
better site for Federal capital; and I consider
that your objection, “Too far from Australia,”
is somewhat weak. Whatever place is fixed upon
will be far away. Albury is far away, and so is
Ballarat, and so is every other place that has been
mentioned. In fact, you cannot have a Federal
capital without it being far away. All that can
be done is to select the best site, provided that it
is easy of access. Well, Albany is easy of access
to all the world. It is the front-door of Australia,
and Sydney is the side-door ; and you always ex-
pect swell company to use the front-door. In
fact, if there is such a thing as destiny, Albany
must have been planned carefully as the site of the
future Federal capital, for there seems to be no-
thing lacking. When the Federal capital question
comes on for settlement, Albany ought to be in
the running if merit goes for anything.—F.

DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT PILLS
OX PER!IVEA.]NrE]\rTLY
BILIOUSNESS, INDIGESTION, ai)d CONSTIPATION.
THEY STAND ALONE AS A WOMAN’S BEST FRIEND!
A Miracle Wrought.
MRS. LANG’S TERRIBLE CONDITION—
THREE LEADING SPECIALISTS FOR
FEMALE AILMENTS BAFFLED -HOS-
PITALS FAIL—The SURGEON’S KNIFE
ADDS TO HER AGONY. DR. MORSE’S
INDIAN ROOT PILLS CURE HER
PERMANENTLY AND COMPLETELY.
Miracles have been chronicled of the past, and
it does not appear as if the word fits into our
present-day vocabulary; but no other word can
Eronerly express the marvellous recovery of Mrs.
,ang, 16 Little Albion-street, Surry Hills, Syd-
ney : “I had quite made up my mind that I
would die,” said Mrs. Lang to a reporter who had
been called to investigate her astonishing cure.
“ I had undergone operation after operation, each
of which would only give me slight relief from my
dreadful sufferings. In fact, the agony which I
endured seemed more than any human being could
live under, and yet, somehow, I held unto life
when death would have been a blessing.
“ Had you seen me at that time you would
have taken me for a Chinese, my skin was so
yellow. Only I had not the almond eyes,
many Chinese would have looked Europeans
beside me. The complaint I suffered from
lasted eight years, and they were the most
wretched years a mortal ever lived through,
if I can give the name of living to such an exist-
ence. I had been on the operating-table in the
hospital more than once, and under the treatment
of three of the best specialists for female ailments
in the city, and there were silver tubes inserted
in my body to carry off the matter wliich the
ailment created. I was growing weaker and more
hopeless every day, and was thoroughly resigned
to my end, only it seemed too slow in coming.
“But I wr.s not yet to die. It seemed by
the merest chance that my life was saved. I
had been taken home from the hospital by my
husband in a cab, and was in such torture that
I lay on the floor of the cab with my head on
the seat. From then he was forced to carry me
up-stairs and down-stairs, as I could not walk.
While lying on the sofa one afternoon a little
book was thrown in through the open door. My
husband picked it up and read of the astonishing
cures Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills had made.
He carefully read it through, and was so much
impressed by what he read that he urged I should
try them as a last hope. At first I scorned the
idea that any medicine could accomplish what
leading doctors and the hospitals had utterly
failed to do, but at last I acted on his advice and
tried Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills.”
At this stage Mrs. Lang produced her photo.,
taken when her friends thought she was dying.
It was the picture of a woman with a drawn face
and the expression of intense suffering. She then
said : “ The difference you see between that
photo, and the perfect specimen of health and
strength which you now see before you is solely
due to the use of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills.
The pills went quietly and painlessly to work and
cleared my system of the impurities, and I began
to mend rapidly. My appetite is now splendid,
and I gained 28 pounds in three months after
starting the use of the pills. I suppose I had
been attended by at least half-a-dozen doctors
during my illness, besides trying all the patent
medicines that I thought would help me ; in fact,
I spent nearly half a fortune in trying to be cured.
A few bottles of the pills have caused a complete
cure in my case, when you might say thatdeath was
staring me. You are at liberty to make the facts
known, and 1 will gladly endorse my statements
if anyone doubting will call on me. I consider it
my duty to make the facts known. lam in per-
fect health, and can walk any distance, thanks to

How is Your Breath?
Foul breath is always an indication of poor
health—bad digestion. It is the starting-point of
many very serious maladies. Upon the healthy
action of the digestive organs the blood depends
for its richness and purity. If digestion stops,
poisonous matter accumulates and is forced into
the blood—there is no place else for it to go.
The bad breath is a danger-signal. Look out
for it! If you have it, or any other symptom of
indigestion, take a bottle or two of Dr. Morses
Indian Root Pills. They will straighten out the
trouble, make your blood pure and healthy, and
full of nutriment for the tissues. It is a strong
statement, but a true one.

HAVE CURED

One of the roots from which these Pills are
made is a sudorific, which opens the pores of the
skin, and assists nature in throwing out the finer
parts of the corruption within. The second is a
plant which is an expectorant that opens and un-
clogs the passage to the lungs, and thus, in a
soothing manner, performs its duty by throwing
off the phlegm and other humors from the lungs
by copious spitting. The third is a diuretic,
which gives ease and double strength to the kid-
neys ; thus encouraged, they draw large amounts
of impurity from the blood, which is then thrown
out bountifully by the urinary or water passage,
and which could not have been discharged in any
other way. The fourth is a Cathartic, and accom-
panies the other properties of the Pills while en-
gaged in purifying the blood ; the coarser particles
of impurities, which cannot pass by other outlets,
are thus taken up and conveyed off in great quan-
tities by the bowels.

To W. WEBBER, Esq., Proprietor of Vitadatio,
Launceston, Tasmania. ... „ . , , • „ •.
Dear Sir,—Believing that this testimonial will be the means of inspiring some poor sufferer with hope, and know ng it
to be my duty to let other! know of the benefit 1 have received from your medicine, I gladly do so. On the 30th day of
November 1894, I was suddenly seized with a violent hemorrhage from the lungs ; 1 at once sought medical aid, and was
obliged to remain perfectly quiet. Two days after I had another bad attack of hemorrhage, and this time a piece of my lung
came away with the blood Once more, a fortnight later, I had another attack, and the doctor held out no hope of my re-
covery. However, with skilful attention, I slowly recovered, and went back to business. I often atter that time expector-
ated blood, but not in very large quantities ; I would feel well for a month or two, and then I would have another bad turn.
However to make a long story short, I started studying under tutors with a view of entering the ministry, and whilst doing
so had another bad turn ; I went to the doctor, who said I must reduce my hours of study. A friend of mine asked me to
consult another doctor, who, after examining me, said, “ Mr. Wylie I am very sorry for you ; you are m consumption
He also ordered me away to Echuca. I went away to Eehoca, and the doctor who examined me, said that he agreed with
the other medical men, and that I was suffering from consumption. I came home from Echuca, and a week after l was .e. zed
with another violent hemorrhage. My father, who knew Mr. Palmer in New Zealand, and knewthat. VitadAt.o had cured hin
wrote to me whilst in Echuca, telling me about your medicine. I was very sceptical, and only laughed at the ideaof
medicine doing me any good. However, I consented to visit Mr. Palmer, and did so ; directly he saw me he said, I can
cure you.” ifaughed at him, and told him about my health, and how long I had suffered. He said, Never mind, I can
cure vou,” and he persuaded me to take a bottle of Vitadatio home with-me. I had taken six bottles when I discontinued it.
I received great benefit from the contents of the six bottles, but as soon as I discontinued taking it I drifted back into a bad
state of health again. The hemorrhages came on, and I was exceedingly weak and ill. Mr. I alme.r once more urged me . o
try Vitadatio, and to give it a fair trial. I consented, and started to take it again. Ihe result is all I could wish for , I can
truly say I never felt better ; I have increased in weight fourteen pounds June stone), and my friends are unanimous in
praise of Vitadatio. A reverend friend of mine, speaking about me, said ' Thanks to God and Vitadatio he is a perfect
miracle.” Another friend said to me just a day or two ag<>, You never looked better in your life. I thank God that Mr.
Palmer ever persuaded me to take the medicine, and I do most strongly urge upon others to try your marvellous remedy,
Vitadatio. To show you how ill I have been, I may mention just here that I have received over =£Bo from my lodge, and on
the 13th day of September (this year) my doctor declared me off the funds of my lodge, and said I was fit to do light woik
again. I need hardly say that you are at perfect liberty to make what use you like of this testimonial.
Believe me to be, faithfully yours,
ARTHUR J. WYLIE.

“ H.”: What innumerable odd antique
little houses there are in the back parts of Western
Melb. One would think it a thousand years old.
Recent telegrams from Maoriland report
heavy landslips on the Rimutaka railway-line
between Wellington and the Wairarapa valley.
This line is a splendid object-lesson in log-rolling.
Although a fairly level route could have been
found, political influence dragged the line labori-
ously over a 5000 ft. range in order to cut through
the property of some supporters of the then Govt.
A special locomotive which grips a third or central
rail drags the train over this stupendous job. The
line has always been a bad one to work, and a
good many years ago a train was blown completely
off the track at Siberia, and several lives were
lost. Now there are thoughts of building a new
line by a practicable route, and writing off the
Rimutaka incline as a bad debt.

To judge by a few recent Maoriland sen-
tences the receipt of the franchise doesn’t appear
to have much improved woman’s position in that
“advanced” community. A woman —who rightly
or wrongly believed herself to have been cruelly in
jured by another woman fired at and very slightly
wounded the latter and received seven years gaol.
At the same sessions a man, charged with shooting
at his wnfe with intent, received only one year.
Also, in another case in which a big, burly brute
had kicked and pounded his wife all but to death,
the sentence was six months for “ common
assault.” Considering that the evidence in this
case was so revolting that it had to be heard with
closed doors it is hard to conceive what “ justice ”
of this brand would define as an uncommon
assault. Apparently the present woman with a
vote can be kicked just as cheaply as the previous
woman without one, and if she gors on the shoot
she gets seven times as much gaol as a man does.

Perhaps you are one of those people, and are not
aware of it. You have something the matter
with you, but, not having any experience, treat it
lightly, while it gets worse and worse, untimately
destroying you.
The wise man will at once put himself in the
witness-box, and ask himself the following ques-
tions, as will the wise woman —•
Do I sleep badly ; do I wake up in the morning,
even when I appear to have slept all night, fagged
and jaded, with lack-lustre eyes and an inclination
only to go to bed again. Do I eat badly, or, when
I do eat “well,” does my food agree with and
nourish me ? Do Igo about my work dejectedly—
do I suffer from headaches, pains in either the
limbs or body, and indulge in depressing retros-
pections ? Do I, in short, suffer from a loss of
mental and physical elasticity—that exultant
appreciation of life which all healthy people must
have ?
If to even one of these questions the answer be
“Yes,” then that man realises that he wants a
tonic —something to give him an appetite, to
steady his nerves, and to make him strong and
active in the daytime, and afford him calm and
refreshing sleep at night.
Such a tonic is Minerva Tonic, the world’s
greatest and only permanent restorative, the
praise of whieh is general and unstinted.
Professor Macadam, Consulting Chemist to the
British Government, writes :—“ Minerva is a
judicious combination of iron, phosphorus, and
alkaloid compounds, which will be found highly
serviceable as a tonic remedy for strengthening
the. system.”
Very Rev. Dr. O’Haran, P.S., to his Eminence
the Cardinal Archbishop of Sydney, writes :
“Minerva is most palatable and energising.
Everyone who has had experience of it pronounces
it admirable.”
Mr. G. D. Clark, ex-M.L.A., Grand Lodge of
Good Templars, writes :—“lt is most helpful in
clearing the brain, stimulating thought, and mak-
ing work a pleasure. ”
Mr. Wm. Stevenson, Manager of the Peesby-
teeian, Sydney, writes “Itis a strengthening
and invigorating medicine. It is especially suit-
able for the Australian climate. ”

round the joints aA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,

The Cycloon, Paddy Cahill, and
the G.R.
Far in the north of Australia lies a little-
known land, a vast half-finished sort of region,
wherein Nature has been apparently practising
how to make better places. This is the Northern
Territory of South Australia. Britain, it is
said, thinks of establishing an Imperial naval
station at Port Darwin. But let Britain beware !
The Northern Territory has “broke” everybody
that ever touched it in any shape or form, and it
will break Britain if she meddles with it. The
decline and fall of the British Empire will date
from the day that Britannia starts to monkey with
the Northern Territory.
* • • *
This vast possession, which extends half-
way down the Continent of Australia, is not,
strictly speaking, a part of the S.A. province. It
is a Crown possession, handed over to the Adelaide
folk to manage and work for their own loss, and
for years they have poured their capital like water
into this huge sink. And still, after swallowing
two and a-half millions of Government money,
and Heaven only knows how much private capital,
the place is steadily going seventy thousand a year
to the bad. Year after year the South Austra-
lians have swallowed the same old wheeze about
the immense undeveloped resources of “ our mag-
nificent Northern Territory,” and have hung on
pluckily, in the hope of one day getting some of
their money back—and possibly, also, in the fear
of the N.T.’s resumption as a Crown colony,
an event which would at once be followed by an
influx of cheap Asiatics from Britain’s Eastern
possessions. And, in fact, the Territory itself is
now clamoring for the introduction of the cheap
and nasty Chow, notwithstanding that it is
breeding its own Chinky fast enough, in all con-
science. The Territory people want more Chows,
and would gladly cut loose from South Australia
to get them. As for the trifle of two and a-half
millions that they owe, they would attend to that
small matter after the wet season. In the Terri-
tory everything good is always going to happen
after the wet season.
* * * •
The capital of the Northern Territory is
Palmerston, on Port Darwin, a harbor little,
if at all, inferior to Port Jackson. Pal-
merston is unique among Australian towns,
inasmuch as it is filled with the boilings over
of the great cauldron of Oriental humanity.
Here come the vagrant and shifting population of
all the Eastern races. Here are gathered together
Canton coolies, Japanese pearl-divers, Malays,
Manilamen, Portuguese from adjacent Timor,
Cingalese, Zanzibar niggers looking for billets as
stokers, frail (but not fair) damsels from Kobe;
all sorts and conditions of men. Kipling tells
what befell the man who “tried to hustle the
East, ” but the man who tried to hustle Palmerston
would get a knife in him quick-and-lively. The
Chow and the Jap and the Malay consider them-
selves quite as good as any alleged white man. In
Japtown (the Easterners’ quarters), Chinese
children by the dozen play about all day long in
the dusty streets; gaily-dressed, cheerful little
barbarians, revelling in the heat. The gold,
fields are all worked by Chinese labor, and hun-
dreds of Chinese fossick about the old alluvial
claims; fifty pearling - luggers go out every
tide, carrying seven hands each, practically all
colored men—3so yellow, brown, and brindled
vagrants moving backwards and forwards with the
tide. And more boats building, and more brindle-
colored Japanese arriving every month. To supply
the needs of all these, there are stores of every
kind in Japtown, and the storekeepers all deal
with the East for their supplies. There is an
Eastern flavor over everything; when the Palmer-
stonians want to gamble at the annual races they
do it by Calcutta sweeps, an Eastern form of
betting little known or practised elsewhere in Aus-
tralia.
* * * *
Palmerston is supported by the pearlers,
the gold-mines, and the Government officials. The
Overland telegraph ends at Palmerston and em-
ploys a large staff, known as the O.T. men ; and
the Singapore cable which there leaves Australia,
also employs a large staff of British and Austra-
lasian Telegraph (“B.A.T. ”) officials. These,
with a publican or two, the Government Resident
(always referred to as “the G.R.”), a couple of
lawyers, a doctor, a few storekeepers, customs
and railway officials and Paddy Cahill, the buffalo-
shooter, pretty well make up the white population
of a place upon which the Government has never-
theless squandered money madly. The huge jetty
cost £70,000, and ere it was well finished the teredo
had eaten the piles away and a gigantic crane, that
had just been erected, fell into the water with a
mighty splash. It is there still, but they will get
it out —“after the wet season.” Also, the little
tin-pot railway to Pine Creek cost a million and
doesn’t pay working expenses ; and yet S.A. Par-
liament talks of spending nine millions in pro-
longing this useless railway down the centre of the
continent.
* * • •
There’s a curse on all N.T. undertakings.
Private enterprise, as represented by Fisher and
Lyons, Dr. Brown, and many other “big ” men of
the past, has poured into it hundreds of thousands
of pounds in cattle-stocking, and so on. What is
there to show for it all? When not dead, the
cattle are unsaleable, because there are no markets.
Not a station in the Territory to-day would fetch
at auction half the money it cost; not a mine in
the Territory pays steady interest on its capital.
Sugar planting and quinine planting have failed ;
the blacks now hunt for wild-goose eggs on the
lagoons at Sergison’s abandoned sugar-plantation,
and the wild buffaloes wallow in the swamps
below Beatrice Hills where the quinine was.
Once, though, a ray of hope broke the gloom when
ruby-like gems were discovered in the Mac Donnell
ranges. These stones look exactly like rubies,
which at their best are far more valuable than
diamonds, and as they lay about in any quantity
it was thought for awhile that the Territory was
Saved. A few three-bushel bags were hastily filled
with “rubies” and sent to England. Alas, the
curse of the Territory was on those stones—the
English experts on examination pronounced them
no more than worthless natural simulacra of
the ruby. The gold - mines were rich down
to water-level ; but there the ore became
refractory, and now all the mining is sur-
face. A few market-riggers bought a lot of
mines from the Chinese for about £17,000, and
then sub-divided these properties, and watered the
capital till it now stands at about =£90,000 nominal
value. But sub-divide and water as they like,
they are still the same £17,000 worth of Chinese
gold-mine, and apparently not likely to pay inter-
est on even that modest capital. Out in the ranges
are all sorts of prospectus claims —some of them
good shows ; but no one does any work in the Ter-
ritory. They put everything off till “ after the
wet season. ” It is the land of Later On. If a
Northern Territory man knew that his mine was
full of gold, he would not dig it out. He would
sit down and wait for a Chinaman to come along
and take it on tribute. If no Chinaman came, he
would “send it Home to float.” Said one miner,
“ I’d sooner be in W. A. on one feed a day than be
on good gold here. They don’t ’elp a man to do
nothin’ here. If the G. R. would only let us have
a Guv’ment battery we might get some stone out
and have a crushin’. ” And there he sat waiting
for a Government battery. Waiting—always
waiting, that is the typical Northern Territory
attitude. The old brisk days have gone; the
pushing men have departed ; and those who have
stayed have got the white-ant in their systems.
There is always a wet season just past or coming.
If it is past, they wait “till the ground dries”;
and by the time it is dry they think the next wet
season might come early, and they—wait!
* • * •
The Government sent up a buoy to mark
a dangerous reef. The buoy was taken out with
great ceremony, and anchored over the reef, and
immediately sank. They didn’t get it up again.
It is at the bottom of the sea now, and the reef is
unmarked. Another buoy got adrift from a dan-
gerous reef; this buoy was cruising about Vernon
Straits for some time, but no one fetched it back.
When some lepers were discovered at Palmerston
once, a leper station was formed at a little island
in the harbor, and the lepers were landed there
with great precaution, but as soon as the tide went
down (it falls 24ft.) the lepers calmly waded ashore
and returned to town. Nobody bothered any
more about them,
* • • •
There is only one great land-mark in Palmerston
history—the cyclone which some years ago blew
the town down. A lot of it isn’t rebuilt yet. This
atmospheric disturbance, locally known as “the
cycloon,” is one of the three topics of conversation
in Palmerston; the second is the Government
Resident (the G.R.). He is an English barrister,
and, in his own person, Supreme Court, head of
the Mining Jurisdiction, Protector of Blacks, and
Police Magistrate. No wonder they talk about
him. Good man for the position, too, as he
doesn’t care a damn for anybody, and, starting
from that safe basis, discharges his varied duties
with a light heart. The third subject of discus-
sion is Paddy Cahill, the buffalo-shooter; he is
popularly reported to pursue the infuriated buffalo
at full gallop, standing on his saddle, and dressed
in a towel and a diamond ring, and yelling like
a wild Indian. The trinity of the N.T. : The
cycloon, the G.R., and Paddy Cahill! The in-
habitants sit about the shady verandahs and drink,
and talk about one or all of these three. They
start drinking square-gin immediately after break-
fast, and keep it up at intervals till midnight.
They don’t do anything else to speak of, yet they
have a curious delusion that they are a very ener-
getic and reckless set of people. But it’s all talk
and drink. Palmerston is the city of booze, blow,
and blasphemy. There is an Act compelling
a publican to refuse drink to an habitual inebriate.
This is locally known as the “Dog Act,’’and to be
brought under the Dog Act is a glorious distinc-
tion, a sort of V.C. of Northern Territory life.
» • • *
To sum up, the Northern Territory is a vast,
wild land, full of huge possibilities, but, up to
now, a colossal failure. She has leagues and
leagues of magnificent country—with no water.
Miles and miles of splendidly-watered country—
where the grass is sour, rank, and worthless.
Mines with rich ore—that it doesn’t pay to treat.
Quantities of precious stones —that have no value.
The pastoral industry and the mines are not pay-
ing, and the pearling, which does, is getting too
much into Jap hands. The hordes of aliens that
have accumulated are a menace to the rest of Aus-
tralia. Nevertheless, the white folk, there are
hospitable to a fault. The strangers within their
gates never have a dull moment—nor a sober
moment —if the inhabitants can help it. And,
after all the hard things I have written of it, I
would give “my weary soul ” to be back at
Palmerston in that curious lukewarm atmosphere
and watch the white-sailed pearling boats beating
out; to see the giant form of Barney Flynn, the
buffalo-shooter, stalking emu-like through the
dwarfish crowd of Japs, and Manila men ; to be
back once more with the B.A.T. and the O.T. and
Paddy Cahill and the G.R., while the Cycloon
hummed and buzzed on the horizon ; or to be in
the buffalo-camp with Rees and Martin, shooting
the big, blue bulls at full gallop, or riding home in
the cool moonlight with the packhorses laden with
hides. —
If you’ve heard the East a-callin’ you don’t never heed
naught else.
And the man who once goes to the Territory
always has a hankering to get back there. Some
day it will be civilised and spoilt; but up to the
present it has triumphantly overthrown all who
have attempted to improve it. It is still “the
Territory.” Long may it wave !
THE BANJO.
“ N. ” : The sickening crocodilery of the
American Congressional chaplain over Spain re-
minds me of that stupid picture of the lady duel-
lists, with rapiers, stripped to the waist. When
one has struck the other, she blubbers over her.
Just at present there are in Sydney many
brilliant legal lights who are on the look-out for
offices. The offices need not be sumptuously fur-
nished, or even centrally situated. If they con-
tain a couple of chairs and a table the eminent
lawyers will be satisfied; they are prepared to
provide their own mattresses and blankets. This
sudden demand for accommodation is the result
of an unexpectedly boorish attitude adopted by
certain owners of official premises in the city who
wanted their miserable, sordid rent. Meantime,
if a client with only a little ready money wishes to
take action for the restitution of conjugal rights,
or to seek advice in connection with the registra-
tion of a dog, he must do so in the archway, or in
the threepenny parlor of the Beetle and Gluepot.
One ancient legal veteran for a fortnight past has
been going about cursing the tyranny of Capital.
The documents connected with the vast and in-
tricate cases he has in hand he carries in his hat.
It seems that his office-rent was so far in arrear as
to be practically out of sight. The landlord
ordered him to quit scores of times, but the
veteran smiled imperturbably and took no notice.
The landlord did not wish to hurt the feelings of
his tenant by resorting to forcible ejection. He
thought the matter over awhile and then hit
upon a plan. The office occupied by the veteran
is a detached apartment at the top of a steep
flight of wooden stairs. The landlord sawed away
eight steps. The veteran has since been trying to
borrow a rope-ladder with grappling-hook attach-
ments.

DOAN’S BACKACHE KIDNEY PILLS
Impure thoughts or a dissolute life are mirrored in the face. A man’s face, therefore, is what his character makes it, and Nature
las wise y posted the warning where all the world may see it As she has provided a warning for character, so has she provided a
warning for all diseases. A man is not stricken with Disease of the Kidneys
in a day. He is repeatedly warned ;it may be by pains in the back, by
■ scanty or too much urine, by sediment, by dizziness, rheumatism, slight
t dropsy, irregular heart’s action, loss of memory, hot dry skin, disordered
eyesight, loss of strength, &c. These symptoms are the signals the kidneys
give that something is wrong. They are fair warnings, and the man’s life
can be saved if he heeds them, but if he does not they will kill like light-
ning later on.
Is it not wise to heed the warnings ?
\s a man’s character is reflected in his face, so is the character of a
medicine reflected in its results, and in its claims. If those results are always
given in the word of people in our own town whom we can go and see, and
whose statements we can verify, is not that the most straightforward kind
of evidence ? Is it not entitled to our confidence ? If the medicine is for
kidney diseases, and it be always recommended for that specific purpose, is
not that honest as well ?
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills are establishing a solid foundation and
character here in Sydney, first, because they are always endorsed by Sydney
people, and second, because they are for kidney diseases and claim nothing
more. In other words, they are winning because they are honest. It’s a
homely name, but an honest medicine.

The Never-Never Novelist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They keep on writing novels (it’s the kind of stuff
that pays)
Of the Never-Never country wild and free ;
Drawing highly-colored sketches of the wild bush-
riders’ ways
From the very tame bush-riders that they see.
There you’ll see the “stalwart plainsman ” (here
he’s simply called a “ bloke ”)
Careering o’er the prairie on his steed ;
Here a Darling River drover on a broken-winded
moke,
Or a lonely swagman cadging for a feed.
There the noble, naked savage, with his boome-
rang and spear,
Has deadly wars with tomahawk and knife ;
Here an educated nigger, reared on politics and
beer,
Who never swung a nullah in his life.
There—majestic, rolling prairies, and forests dark
and dense,
All fragrant with the scent of wattle-gum ;
Here-a pessimistic “cookie” sitting sadly on a
fence,
Smoking hard, and praying hard for Kingdom
come.
There the yawning mountain chasms, and the
lofty, snow-clad peak,
Where lightnings play and tropic storms appall;
Here—a range of dreary sandhills and a God-
forsaken creek,
With a dam to catch the rains that never fall.
The man who writes the novels (in his easy,
cushioned chair)
Will smile to see his books upon your shelf ;
For though he paints a paradise he’ll take all
sorts of care
That he doesn’t go and live out there himself.
So they keep on writing novels (it’s the kind of
stuff that pays)
Of the Never-Never country, wild and free.
And we read their stirring stories of the wild
bushriders’ ways,

RACKA ROCK.
Some time ago an Australian lydy,
who fancied that she was a top-notcher in
sassiety, was assisted out of an over-turned
baggy, in which she had been driving,
by “ such a gentlemanly man.” To show
her gratitude, she invited him to a small
“evening,” presided over by herself. She was
rather disgusted, on presenting the aforesaid
assister to the lion of the evening, Capt. , of
H.M.S. , to be informed by him that they
were already acquainted. “ You see,” said the
R.N., “ Mister Smith is my cabin-steward ! ”
On Our Selection—About
Dancing.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Our selection was a great place for dancing. We
could all dance from Dan down—and there wasn’t
a figure or a movement we didn’t know. We
learned young. Mother was a firm believer in
early tuition. She used to say it was nice for
young people to know how to dance, and be able
to take their part when they went out anywhere,
and not be awkward and stupid-looking when they
went into society. It was awful, she thought, to
see young fellows and big lumps of girls like the
Bradys stalk into a ballroom and sit the whole
night long in a corner, without attempting to get
up. She didn’t know how mothers could bring
children up so ignorantly, and didn t wonder at
some of them not being able to find husbands for
their daughters.
But we had a lot to feel thankful for. Besides a
sympathetic mother, every other facility was
afforded us to become accomplished. Abundance
of freedom ; enthusiastic sisters; and no matter
how things were going—whether the corn wouldn’t
come up, or the wheat had failed, or the pump-
kins given out, or the water-hole run dry—we
always had a concertina in the house. It never
failed to attract company. Paddy Maloney and
the well-sinkers, after belting and blasting all day
long, used to drop in at night, and throw the table
outside, and take the girls up, and prance about
the floor with them till all hours.
* • * »
Nearly every week mother gave a ball. It might
have been every night only for Dad. He said the
jumping about destroyed the ground-floor—wore
it away and made the room like a well. And
whenever it rained hard and the water rushed in
he had to bale it out. Dad always looked on the
dark side of things. He had no ear for music,
either. His want of appreciation for melody
often made the home miserable when it might
have been the merriest on earth. Sometimes it
happened that he had to throw down the plough-
.reins for half-an-hour or so to run round the
wheat-paddock after a horse or an old cow ; then
if he found Dave or Sal, or any of us, sitting in-
side playing the concertina when he came to get a
drink, he would nearly go mad.
“ Can’t y’ find anything better t’ do than ever-
lastingly playing at that damn thing ? ” he would
shout. And if we didn’t put the instrument
down immediately he would tear it from our
hands and pitch it outside. If we did lay it
down quietly he would snatch it up and heave it
out just as hard. The next evening he would
devote all his time to patching the fragments to-
gether with sealing-wax.
Still, despite Dad’s antagonism, we all turned
out good players. It cost us nothing either. We
learnt from each other. Kate was the first that
learnt. She taught Sal. Sal taught Dave, and so
on. Paddy Maloney was Kate’s tutor. He passed
our place every evening going to his selection,
where he used to sleep at night (fulfilling con-
ditions), and always stopped at the fence to yarn
with Kate about dancing. Paddy was a fine
dancer himself, very light on his feet and easy to
waltz with—so the girls made out. When the
dancing subject was exhausted Paddy would drag
some hair out of his horse’s mane, and say, “ How’s
the concertina?” “It’s in there,’’ Kate would
answer. Then turning round she would callout,
* J- oe, bring the coneer’.”
In an instant Joe woidd strut along with it.
And Paddy, for the fiftieth time, would examine
it and laugh at the kangaroo-skin straps that
Dave had tacked to it, and the scraps of brown
paper that were plastered over the ribs of it to
keep the wind in ; and, cocking his left leg over
the pommel of his saddle, would sound a full blast
on it as a preliminary. Then he would strike up
“The Rocky Road to Dublin,” or “The Wind
Among the Barley,’’or some other beautiful air,
and grind away untiringly until it got dark—until
mother came and asked him if he wouldn’t come
in and have supper. Of course, he always would.
After supper he would play some more. Then
there would be a dance.
* * * •
A ball was to be held at Anderson’s one Friday
night, and only Sal and Dave were asked from our
place. Dave was very pleased to be invited ;it
was the first time he had been asked anywhere,
and he began to practise vigorously. The evening
before it took place Dad sent him to put the
draught horses in the top paddock. He went
off merrily with them. The sun was just going
down when he let them go, and save the noise of
the birds as they departed to rest the paddock was
quiet and still. Dave was filled with emotion and
enthusiastic thoughts about the ball. He threw
the winkers down and looked around. For a
moment or two he stood erect, then he bowed
gracefully to the saplings on his right, then
to the stumps and trees on his left, and
humming a tune, ambled across a small patch
of ground that was bare and black, and
pranced back again. He opened his arms
and, clasping some beautiful imaginary form
in them, swung round and round like a wind-
mill. Then he paused for breath, embraced his
partner again, and “galloped” up and down.
And young Johnson, who had been watching him
in wonder from behind a fence, bolted for our
place.
“ Mrs. Ross ! Mrs. Ross ! ” he shouted from the
verandah. Mother went out.
“ Wot’s —wot’s up with Dave ? ”
Mother turned pale.
“ There’s something / ”
“My God ! ” mother exclaimed— “ whatever
has happened ? ”
Young Johnson hesitated. He was in doubt.
“Oh ! what is it ? ” mother moaned.
“Well” (he drew close to her) “he’s—he’s
mad.”
“ Oh-h!”
“He is. I seen ’im just now up in your pad-
dick, an' he’s clean off he’s pannikin.”
Just then Dave came down the track, whistling.
Young Johnson saw him and fled.
For some time mother regarded Dave with
grave suspicion, then she questioned him closely.
“ Yairs,” he said, grinning hard, “ I was goin’
through th’ fust set.”
Q.
STEELE RUDD.
There is a lot of pathos about the tale of
those women of Granada who whacked the statue
of Columbus because he discovered America and
thereby started their country’s misfortunes.
Columbus was the beginning of all Spain’s disas-
ters. He began the greatest gold and land boom
the world ever saw, and Spain, as the principal
boomer, burst so badly that she has never got
straight again since.
Cheerful tale from the frozen road to
Klondyke:—
“Half a mile to Hades, either way!” Such is the
message to be found on a tree where two trails fork, a ten
days’ tramp from Hazelton, on the Ashcroft, trail. J. A.
Grey, of New York City, and J. Atwood, of Boston, two
passengers by the Horsa to-day, tell a story which con-
firms former reports of the horrors of the Ashcroft trail,
but it is more comprehensive. They left Ashcroft on
April 28. At QuesnelLe they were tnjd it was 3(1 days to
Hazellnn. They took 30 days’ provisions, and as a con-
sequence nearly starved to death, for instead of 250 miles
it was 375. The trail was very had, but, not, a circum-
stance to the awful experience from Hazelton to Glenora.
“ We were told again it was 30 days’ travel, but, found
it 45. Instead of 350 miles it was 440. Advertisements
said it was 370 miles from Ashcroft to Glenora ;
instead it was 1400. Our sufferings between II zc 1-
ton and Glenora were almost beyond endurai ee. It uas
the valley of the shadow of death. For ten days we
lived on a few ounces of porridge daily, and for live, when
the pangs of hunger drove ns to desperation, we shot a
horse and lived on horse-flesh, and no meat ever tasted
nicer. In one place within five miles I counted 100 dead
horses, and Indians told of men who had laid down and
died, starved to death rather than eat the half-famished
beasts of burden which had helped them patiently to wal-
low wearily through mud and mire until death was
longed for as a release from their trials. In one case we
passed through a forest of soggy moss, with not a vestige
of underbrush. We had no feed, and tied the horses to
trees, where they choked down great wads of moss and
dirt and small tree branches. The 30 miles of moss was
the scene of a thousand horrors. Coming out of the
soggy forest, we struck poison plains, so named on
account of devil-weed covering the ground. The stench
is awful, for horses lie heaped in hundreds, who
have died from eating the poisonous vegetable
here growing in rank profusion. Further on,
we arrived at quicksands, with dense forests on
each side that we could not penetrate. Then there were
quagmires with horses in rows, dead with the packs still
on their backs. It is the solemn truth that our party of
20 led our horses over the hacks of the poor buried brutes.
Beyond this we came to a tree on which was blazed :
“ Eat here and turn back ; just beyond is the Slough Of
Despond.” For two awful, weary miles we trudged
wearily on, our horses actually at times up to the backs
in mud, with their noses stuck in the awful omnipresent
ooze. One after the other, we dragged them out, and put
a bullet through their heads. To make matters worse, it
rained for ten days and nights. Along the road were
strewn hardware, blankets, rifles, cartridges—every con-
ceivable part of a Klondyker’s outfit thrown aside by
heart-broken, half-distracted wayfarers. We came to
another blazed tree, and on it was a sad story, and
beneath a mound of earth, whether the grave of the
writer or not we do not know. The story read
“Here is another fool who left home to seek fortune
over this God-forbidden trail, after borrowing all the
money he could squeeze out of his friends and poor rela-
tions. God help you, poor fellow-pilgrims.”

Oh, the Multitude Who Say It!
Speaking of another world than this, a certain
Book, from which so many draw comfort in times
of trouble, says : “4The inhabitant never saith, i
am sick.” But in this world the air is full of the
weary, wailing cry, “I am sick,” “I suffer so,”
“ Can nothing be done to rid me of pain ? ”
Here is another case of it another out of a
number beyond all counting or imagining. Yet
this one got well. Do you want to hear about it ?
or do you know of any pain-racked mortal who
might like to hear about it ? Then read, either
for yourself or for someone else. We give the facts
exactly as they were given to us.
“ In the early part of 1889 I began to suffer from
illness. I had at first a bad taste in my mouth,
and belched up a sour, gaseous fluid. My appetite
was poor, and, after eating, I had a burning pain
at my chest, and palpitation of the heart. I had
also a gnawing, grinding sensation at the pit of the
stomach, with a feeling of sinking, as though I
were being bodily lowered into a pit. My food
never seemed to settle, but repeated, and I had to
spit it out; this going on until my stomach was
empty.
“ I lost a deal of sleep at night. I got very weak
and thin, losing over a stone in weight; I was so
feeble that I could hardly drag myself along. As
time went on 1 grew irritable and nervous, and
took no interest or pleasure in anything.
“ Sometimes better and at other times worse, I
continued in this way until August of last year
(1893), when I had to give up my employment as
collier at the Waterloo Main Colliery, where I
had been at work for seven years. At this time I
had got so bad that I sat in my chair from morn-
ing till night, too weak and prostrated to go about.
When I did venture out I dare not go alone for
fear I should fall down in the street.
“ At times a strange feeling came over me, as if
my heart had stopped beating; and on one of
these occasions I felt as if I were dying, and said
to my wife, ‘Lass, it’s all up with me.’
“ During my long illness I consulted several
doctors, and they all said my heart was sound,
and that the trouble was all from my stomach and
liver. They gave me medicines and recommended
a change of air. I therefore went to Blackpool,
Scarborough, and Whitby, but was no better either
from the change or from the doctor’s physic.
“ One day a book was left at my house telling
about Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and I
thought it might possibly help me. So I got a
bottle from Mr. Webster, chemist, Green Road,
and when I had finished that bottle I found relief,
for my food no longer repeated and the pain at
my heart was easier. I kept on with the medicine
and soon my appetite returned, and I could eat
without feeling any pain or distress. Gradually I
improved, getting stronger and stronger every
day. I can now eat anything, and do my work
with pleasure. I feel quite a new man, and con-
sider that Mother Seigel’s Syrup has saved my
life. Yours truly, (Signed) George Hopton, 25
Bayswater Terrace, Roundhay Road, Leeds,
February Bth, 1894.”
No words of ours can add an ounce to the
weight of this plain and manly letter, From it,
as from a hilltop, we can see the sad picture o '
those four years of misery. The discouraged and
suffering husband, the patient, burden-bearing,
helpful wife. Heaven be praised that it all ended
as it did—in renewed health —instead of as it
might have ended. The disease was that insatiate
fiend and monster —indigestion and dyspepsia;
cunning as a fox, destructive as a man-eating
tiger. In this case, as in so many others, Mother
Seigel’s Syrup demonstrated that power which has
made it celebrated all over the world—in palace
and in hut, wherever the inhabitant saith, I am
sick. If you are one of these, try it now. If you
are healthy and hearty, thank God for that, and
pass the news of George Hopton’s illness and re-
covery on to any who need to hear it.
